Australian Flapperature
It must be remembered that when one
reads the work of Mary Grant Bruce and
other purveyors of pleasant stories for
flappers, one has instinctively to apply
to such work a standard of criticism lower
than that one applies to a full-grown novel
like, say, Betty Wayside. The wants of
flappers are more easily filled than the wants
of adults. Flapperature stands in a class
of fiction of its own. So in stating that
From Billabong to London-, by Mary Grant
Bruce (Ward, Lock), is admirably written,
her simple-minded clientele must be taken
into consideration. Her readers want
nothing more than this leisurely painting
of station life; and they will gladly read a
whole chapter devoted to the breaking in of
a bucking colt, though the incident is
merely incidental. So her account of a trip
from Australia to England during war time,
with its occasional mild thrills —including
the capture of a German spy and the threat
of the hold-up of. the vessel by a Hun
auxiliary cruiser—is well written —for the
readers who are content with just that
sort of thrill. And her readers do not
object to the introduction of other adven-
tures that have no bearing on the plot—even
if there were a plot. _ There is no need,
either, of any development of character in
the nice but colorless persons she intro-
duces to us. Mary Grant Bruce merely
depicts with an unobtrusive realism life on a
beautiful Australian station and the experi-
ences of her travellers. She writes well —
well enough for her readers. As flappera-
ture From Billabong to London is honest
and satisfying fare. 1
Ethel Turner is in a higher class. The
Cub, a story of war-time (Ward, Lock), is
her 18th novel; and with it she still charms
her large and loyal public. It is a story
of children and youths that will be eagerly
read by children and youths—and enjoyed by
their elders. Curiously enough, like Mary
Grant Bruce, she has staged the greater
part of her story on shipboard. A little
Belgian refugee orphan is being brought to
Australia by her rescuer, a child of 15; and
there we learn the graphic and pitiable story
of one of the innumerable atrocities committed
by the invading Huns. The shipboard life is
devoted not to thrilling adventure but to a
quiet and interesting study in character.
The Cub, a long-legged Australian boy, is
an admirable piece of characterisation, held
strictly, of course, within the limits neces-
sary for Ethel Turner’s traditional readers.
There must be no love-making, no stirring of
sexual attraction, which at the age of the
Cub and the girl are such frequent psycho-
logical effects of our climate.
The effects of war upon the social life of
Sydney is depicted with insight, and many
a touch that is both accurate and acute. And
though the heroism of the Cub is heightened
somewhat beyond credence, again we must
remember Ethel Turner’s special public. Her
girl readers demand a hero, and that sort of
hero. But where Ethel Turner shows the
reason for her popularity is in her ability to
impart in the midst of her genial commen-
tary upon Australian life that touch of the
genuine pathetic that can seduce even the
hardened heart of the adult. And specially
is this marked in the final chapter where
the girl is seeing her hero off to the war.
She has her little Belgian prot6g6 with her
on the wharf; and that child is a healthy
and genuine child. She does not sorrow
over the departure of her big friend: "she
has found a most delightful puddle on the
wharf. “Brigid herself had one golosh
firmly planted on it, and, charming occur-
rence, a curled brown gum-leaf, marvellously
like a boat, was alongside the golosh, just
as it it were a boat at a quay....lt was
really the most glorious game of all her
whole life.”
That incongruous, yet artistic, touch of
reality is Ethel Turner's real secret.
The age of the reader of HVr’s Heart
Throbs, by Lilian Turner, is 20. This is a
story of young love, sentiment without
passion, and of the change in character,
caused by the coming of the war, in a bored,
selfish and foolish Australian girl. Cynthia
was letting herself flop on the comfortable
station outback. Nothing ever happened to
her —not even a man ; and that is a parlous
situation for any nice-looking Australian
girl. But, in a delightful scene, the declara-
tion of war comes while Cynthia is in the
middle of the perplexities and worries of
matching a certain mauve button. She had
been in every shop in Sydney, and had
failed. That moment was inconsiderately
chosen by England to declare war. But,
with the new duties that crowded upon the
girl, the “knit two —purl two” and Red
Cross duties, and with the enlistment-of the
two men who love her, Cynthia discovers
herself. Lilian Turner writes with a natural
facility, she can add a genuine touch of
pathos at the right place, and she has a skill
in characterisation sufficient for her some-
what unsophisticated and unexacting reader
of 20.
The age of Vera G. Dwyer’s gentle reader
is 17. The reader of A War of Girls ( Ward,
Lock) must be able to look back upon her
early school days with happy memories, but
with a complacent knowledge that she has
so outgrown them that she can now appre-
ciate the humor of those distant days ; and
she must also be able to look eagerly for-
ward to the delights of flirtations and the
prodigious possibility of a proposal. It is
not a book for the young school-girl, for
Vera-G. Dwyer makes fun of. and not with,
the school-girl. It is sympathetic fun, of
course, but the young school-girl takes her-
self quite seriously. And wliat school-girl
would be encouraged to continue A War of
Girls when on the second page she meets
language so diffuse as this sentence: “It had
seemed to Miss Stockley that the unpopu-
lated rear of the hall would be a good place
in which she might increase her lack of
orthographical wisdom, and develop a proper
sense of contrition for it?” And why, unless
she is appealing to adults, should she use a
circumlocution like this: “He had been in
Such desperately ruinous financial difficulties
that, but for the other’s timely aid, tendered
at considerable sacrifice to himself, he would
have been overwhelmed by them”? But her
story has feminine humor in it, and just
enough young love to please feminine 17.
The plot is rather cleverly and capably
woven, from the slightest strands, and here
and there are some interesting characterisa-
tions. A War of Girls is excellent flappera-
ture.
A Little Bush Poppy , by Edith Graham
(Lothian, Melb., 3s. 6d.), is for the girl of
12 or 15. Here we are pleasantly intro-
duced to some more “Little Australians”
and follow their escapades with sympathetic
interest. The humor —an important thing
in this sort of story—would be understood
by the child-reader herself. The children
are real; and the author rightly makes the
love interest of the grown-ups, who. of
course, are never older than 21. a subsidiary
part of the plot. Yet one wonders why such
a subject need be introduced at all into a
story of children, for children. One curious
point in Australian flapperature is that the
authors almost invariably introduce an
author among their leading characters. Usu-
ally it is the hero who writes an Australian
novel, which is immediately accepted and
brings the author “fame.” Occasionally it
is the heroine who does it; but invariably
at least one of the kiddies has aspirations
toward fiction or verse. In A Little Bush
Poppy the child not only writes stories and
verse, but Edith Graham reprints them.
Either every Australian girl writes, or wants
to write, verse, or the authors of our flappera-
ture. are singularly alike and singularly fool-
ish in their choice of their ideal man.
j*
The Isle of Palms, by Charles Barrett
(Lothian, Melb., 3s. 6d.) is hardly to be
included under this title, since it is an
adventure story for boys. But what girl
does not read her brother’s books? This is
a good yarn of treasure-hunting on the
usual Pacific island of romance, done by a
writer who is also a naturalist. The yarn
is told in simple style; the adventures are
exactly the sort of adventures that recur in
beys’ books, and, incidentally, the child-
reader will gather some interesting facts
about the life of insect, bird and animal.
Just the thing for the Australian kid.
■■ ■ •
A second edition of .Tas. A. Philp’s shilling
volume, Some Bulletin Stories, has been
called for.
Readers are reminded that the competi-
tion for the six best Australasian short
stories closes on December 10. A guinea
will be paid the competitor who submits a
list of six, or less, short stories written by
Australasians that most nearly corresponds,
in order and in contents, with the six stories
that top the poll of the aggregate votes.
War Personalities
According to A. 6. Gardiner, author of
The War Lords (Dent, per Dymoek’s
Library), the person primarily to blame for
all the trouble was not the Kaiser, but the
Crown Prince. It is reported, on high
authority, that when the Kaiser threw his
pen on the table after signing the declara-
tion of war against Russia, he said to the
triumphant soldiers around him: “Gentle-
men, you will live to regret this.” Add to
this another “well-informed” incident. When
the Crown Prince heard of his father’s
hurried return from his cruise in Norwegian
waters, he said to one of the military cabal:
illiam is back ; but he is too late.”
Elsewhere Gardiner says: “The Crown
Prince played a leading part in the military
conspiracy that led to the war. His rela-
tions with his father had been notoriously
bad. He had allied himself openly with the
military extremists; and there is a wide-
spread and well-informed opinion that it was
the fear of his son that was largely respon-
sible for the marked change in the attitude
of the Kaiser [toward war] in 1913 It
was believed that the Kaiser would never be
coerced into making the plunge, and he was
openly accused-of cowardice. In the Crown
Prince was found an easy tool with which to
bring the Kaiser to heel. The alliance of
the heir-apparent with the war party became
an open menace to the authority of the
Kaiser.” In the notorious Zabern incident
the Crown Prince openly and defiantly threw
over his allegiance to his father. But the
indictment of history against the Kaiser will
not be that he wanted war, but that his
policy could not end in anything but war.
This series of character impressions of 20
personalities thrown into sudden fame by
the war, ranging from the Kaiser to M.
Venizelos, is brilliantly written, as those who
recall this author’s Pillars of Society will
expect. His conclusion about Wilhelm is:
“Perhaps the Kaiser is mad. Pride such as
his is hardly consistent with sanity. But
certainly the peoples of Europe will be mad
if, after this frightful lesson, they do not
make an end on the earth forever of the
doctrine of the Divine right of kings.”
d*
Gardiner states liis belief that there is a
letter in existence written by Admiral Fisher
in 1905 in which he prophesied that there
would be war with Germany in 1914, and
that the then Captain Jellicoe would be the
Admiralissimo. Remarkable, if true. But
Gardiner shows that this twin-prophecy was
something more than guesswork. 3905 was
the year of the creation of the Dreadnought,
and the Dreadnought threw the Kiel Canal
out of commission for a certain number of
years. For to meet the Dreadnought the
Germans had to build as big a boat, and the
just completed Kiel Canal was too shallow
to float it. It would take till 1914 before
the Kiel Canal could be reconstructed. As
for the second part of the alleged prophecy,
Fisher, convinced that the first part would
prove true, had to meet the expected chal-
lenge with the right man in charge of the
British navy. He had discovered Captain
Jellicoe, and decided that he was the only
man for the big job. But there was no
chance for Jellicoe’s promotion in time if
the red-tape of seniority was considered.
“Those who in the intervals have followed
events in the navy closely know how the ful-
filment of the prophecy has been brought
about. Perhaps only the courage of a
Churchill could have carried through the
rapid shufflings of men and officers necessary
to accomplish the object. It was accom-
plished at the last moment. As the hour
struck Jellicoe appeared as the Admiral-
issimo of the fleet.” Fisher, barring acci-
dents, was betting on a cert.
One of the most interesting character
sketches in the volume is that of Nicholas
of Montenegro. He.is the reincarnation of
the Anthony Hope romance. And, incident-
ally, he does a flourishing export business-
in daughters. One of his daughters is the
Queen of Italy, one the Queen of Servia, one
is' the wife of the Russian Grand Duke
Nicholas, and the fourth a wife of his bro-
ther. King Ferdinand of Bulgaria is an-
other character from fiction. “He is the
king of melodrama.... There are times when
you almost doubt whether he was not in-
vented by Dumas or Stevenson or Anthony
Hope.... As a matter of fact, Ferdinand was
invented by his mother.” For it was she who
trained him to fit any throne that might
happen to be to-let. “He was whisked from
capital to capital, habituated to the company
of princes, indoctrinated with the diplomatic
subtleties of The Prince, taught the facile
graces of the charmeur, made to cultivate
entomology as one of those hobbies that sit
so prettily on potentates, coached in half a
dozen languages, even in Hungarian, for one
never kn. w whence the call to kingship
would come. Thrones might spring up or
fall vacant anywhere. One must be ready to
pounce.” He pounced—upon Bulgaria.
Another interesting biography is that of
Karl Liebknecht, the leader of the German
Socialists —the other king in Potsdam. “There
is the Kaiser who reviews his legions on the
parade ground before the Old Palace, and
there is Karl Liebkneeht who gathers his
legions in the streets. His election to the
Reichstag as the Socialist representative of
the Kaiser’s own borough in 1912 was the
most bitter insult the Kaiser ever received
from his people. It was as though Windsor
had returned a Republican to Parliament.
The Kaiser’s sons ostentatiously led the way
to the polling booth in the early morning, but
at night the people of Potsdam had elected
Karl Liebkneeht as their democratic king.”
He is too powerful to be constrained. A. G.
Gardiner sees in him “the symbol of the Ger-
many with whom we are going to be recon-
ciled.” Again and again he has made his
protest against the war —and the Kaiser
dare not touch him.
At this moment, however, the character
sketch of Venizelos, the Greek, will attract
more attention. One wonders whether the
Allies will confirm the statement that Veni-
zelos is still “the greatest statesman in
Europe to-day.” It was in all probability
due to a too great dependence on Venizelos’
word that the disastrous naval attack on the
Dardanelles was initiated. A statesman of
his calibre ought to have known the strength
of the forces organised by the Greek Court
against him. History will put the blame
either upon the organiser of that desperate
adventure for too great a trust in Venizelos,
or on Venizelos for too great a trust in his
own power.
Gallipoli Bill, Author
He is still writing, the bloke outback in
Gallipoli, still winning his literary laurels.
A correspondent forwards some examples of
Bill’s capacity for graphic expression, all
taken from Australian soldiers' letters:—•
“He was that excited that his eyes stuck
out far enough for a fowl to roost on them.”
—Pvt. Ludermann (Bendigo).
Pvt. W. liyan (Molong), of shrapnel:
“It’s just like throwing a dishful of rice at
a man. You would have thought a fly
couldn’t have lived there. The 'bullets were
so thick. .. .If there had been another one
in the air, they’d have jammed.”
“Put our fellows into Hell, and they will
clean up the joint first and fight their way
out again.”—Pvt. B. Ashton (Coalville).
Thus spake a private to an officer who
shot dead at close charging Turk:
“What in Hell did you do that for? I could
have shot him five minutes ago, but I
wanted to harpoon the cow!”
Corp. Dibley (Sydney) : “With the dawn
came Lizzie’s deep contralto... .In a little
while we heard a report like a thousand
thunder-claps rolled into one. It was only
Lizzie sounding a low B.”
Pvt. R. L. Houston (Bendigo), under fire:
“My teeth vVere playing ‘Home, Sweet
Home,’ with variations, and my nerves were
jumping like a Morse code apparatus.”
Sergeant W. Trott (Manly), in hospital:
“The doctor called two nurses to have a
look at a narrow escape. I was the narrow
escape.”
Corp. W. V. Gray (Parkville) tells of a
comrade at the great landing: “He jumped
out before the boat landed, and as he floun-
dered ashore he was heard to gurgle: ‘Well,
here’s a blooming submarine at them, any-
how !”
According to a British officer an Indian
trooper thus indicated the difference between
Briton and Australian: “When shrapneL
comes along, an Englishman will duck and
get. in a dug-out; but an Australian will
turn round and say, ‘Who in Hell threw that
thing ?’ ”
•
“Jack Spur” on flappers, literature and
bishops :
Keel Page fiction reviewers do the short-skirted
section an injustice in calling the Garvice brand of
story Flapperature. The present writer reckons he
knows as many flappers as the next man, and never
knew one yet who entertained any illusions con-
cerning Charles or his imitators. While home on a
recent furlough I was surprised to find baby sister
(aged 17) tackling Bergson. We do not claim to be
an exceptional crowd, and there are no geniuses on
the distaff side of the family. Bergson had been intro-
duced to her by a more sophisticated schoolmate as
“a writer one really ought to know.” At least a
dozen flappers qf my acquaintance know Cannan
but not Corelli, and could pass a searching exami-
nation on G. B. Shaw, Pinero and Barrie. The
spread of secondary education and the popularity of
the repertory theatre have improved Miss Pigtail’s
taste for reading matter. When she is not conning
French verbs and botany she gets busy on H. G.
Wells or Shaw, Anne Veronica and Arms and the
Man being hot favorites.
Any bookseller will tell you that Action of the
mildly-naughty type is rushed by middle-aged
matrons who would blush at the mention of Dean
Swift or Rabelais. From personal observations I
should say that commercial travellers and jockeys
are the greatest readers of the gospel according to
Garvice. When the barmaid has time to open a
book it is generally something by Dickens or Hugo’s
latest.
While on a recent train journey my fellow travel-
lers were a bishop, a bookmaker and three commer-
cials. The following publications were visible to
the naked eye. The bishop and one of the commer-
cials were devouring magazines. So was I. Another
commercial was being edified by Nat Gould. The
rest had newspapers. It was a long, tedious trip,
and, after a couple of hours had dawdled by, the
bishop yawned, indulged in a polite commonplace,
and swapped a brand-new Sthand Magazine for the
well-thumbed, dog-eared Nat Gould.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Road to Waverley.
Noise and dust by night and day ;
Running ever towards the sea;
Overlooking beach and bay—
That’s the road to Waverley.
Often ’neath the noonday sky,
While all life works tirelessly,
Plumed funerals go by,
On the road to Waverley.
Tread whatever road you may,
You and I, sometime to be,
We shall take the silent way—
Pass the road to Waverley.
Then we shall no more awake,
See no more as now we see.
As full length at peace we take
The long road to Waverley.
Tread whatever road we may,
Let us face it joyously,
Noon of day, or end of day,
The long road to Waverley.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Magic Rose.
With sweets of lavender and musk,
The petals drifting through the dusk,
Make me an amber rosary.
And you, a soft-toned penitent.
Whose prayers, with the faint perfume blent,
Whisper of secret sins to me.
Sins? Yet I see your foam-white feet
Stainless beneath the winding-sheet
Which folds around them tenderly.
Love leads you down the shining hills
Where tender Silence, list’ning, thrills
With soundless magic melody.
He leads you to a silver plain
Encinctured with a rose-wrought chain,
So that, while bound, you yet are frt
He weaves the sunlight in your hair,
Crowns it with stars and blossoms fair—
All part of Youth’s first ecstasy.
And dark-winged Doubt before you flies,
Love lightens so your dewy eyes,
She feareth their sweet mystery.
Hope from her harp draws songs of these,
Sad Nicolette, sweet Heloise,
The while you listen dreamily.
Ah ! Unforgotten dawn of gold !
Wisdom, nor wealth, nor precept cold
Can ever bring you back to me!
Night broods upon the grey-leaved trees,
Crooning sad dirges to the breeze,
Dimming the path of Phantasy.
Past is the dream! The petals shed
Are crimson where my heart has bled.
Pain stoops and gathers them for me.
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
A. HOWE.
The Joy Riders.
True, Mary Jane, you do not sport Milady's
silk and fur,
And yet for us the lights whiz by—a glorious
silver blur!
The night wind riots through your curls,
and screeches in our ears;
But w r e, my girl, with'hearts a-whirl, are
done with frets and fears.
In yonder park old Moneygrub lives in a
mansion white ;
But, Mary Jane, he seeks in vain, this blood-
red surge of life!
Wealth, and the things that wealth can
bring, are his ; and yet they cloy—
He's never felt, for all his wealth, this
splendid hour of joy!
Milady’s robes are not for you; no gems
gleam in your hair;
And yet I vow she is not now as fair as
you are fair !
When Cornucopia gave largess a blank, girl,
was your prize :
What matter, dear? Enough to peer into
your wondrous eyes !
Enough for me to have you sit close-
snuggled by my side
What time we in old Moneygrub’s luxurious
auto ride;
Enough for me to hear your voice, and see
your teeth of pearl—
What counts the ban if I’m liis man, and
you his servant girl!
Ah, fain would Madam be as you, to feel
the joys we feel
When Beauty smiles a-down the miles, and
Love is at the wheel.
And fain would Madam’s husband give his
money and his might
To glimpse as we the witchery that we have
passed to-night;
To feel the freedom that we feel, and know
the things we know ;
To taste the spice of Paradise in all the
winds that blow;
To see the tints we see around, the silver
and the gold—
Ah, could he, he’d he sorrow-freed, yes, freed
a thousand-fold.
To-night, sweetheart, this car is like some
wild, exultant thing;
Now watch me graze this post (like that!)
and round this corner swing;
Now twig the trees merge into one, and
hear the breezes hiss—
And yet despite our breathless flight we
still have time to kiss!
What roads shall flash beneath to-night?
What dangers shall we dare?
I do not know (a bump, what-oh!) —and
neither do I care!
For this is Joy’s own sacred hour, with
nought our path to bar—
God bless the portly toff who bought this
thousand-guinea car!
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable,, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable). Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture

suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 11, 1015.
BRITAIN’S PRIME MINISTER FLOGS
HIS OWN JOSS.
Asquith gives the whole show away with
this admission :
Voluntaryism is well enough in time of peace, but
it operates in a haphazard, capricious and unjust way
in time of war.
And that is the beginning, the middle and
the end of iff —an appropriate inscription
for the tombstone. Asquith, a life-long-
supporter of the system, could not have
damned it more effectively had he used a
thousand words instead of a score. He
kicks away the last conceivable justifica-
tion for allowing the defence of a nation to
depend upon the eleventh-hour effort of an
untrained multitude.
* * * *
Of course voluntaryism is good enough
when the life of the nation is not at stake,
or when it. is only engaged in such enter-
prises as demand a limited force, or has. to
supply a guard of honor for a visiting-
potentate. Any old army does then. In
the same way, when a man is living in a
remote part of the country, uninhabited by
burglars, it is not necessary for him to have
a policeman on the beat. But when that
citizen comes to town he finds that in the
multitude of John Hobs there is safety for
his watch and his hat and his purse; and
when his premises are raided and his furni-
ture carried off, his goods are torn
away from the thieves, if the protective
force can get there quick enough, and the
criminals are thrown into prison. Unless
this citizen is a blithering lunatic he doesn’t
write to the newspapers complaining that
he has been spared from suffering serious
loss because the State has taken at least
reasonable precautions to protect its law-
abiding inhabitants against attack by those
who covet their neighbor’s ox and ass and
everything that is his. Nor if flames break
out in his house does the sane citizen rise
in revolt against the fire brigade which
dashes up with 15 hoses and squirts cold
water through the windows and drags him
out of bed and slides down a ladder with
him hanging over its shoulder. The only
test of protective agencies, whether they, be
armies or navies or John Hops or fire-
brigades, is their effectiveness to do some-
thing sudden and decisive when they are
wanted. An umbrella with one rib and a
rent covering may be useful as a walking-
stick on a fine evening, but in a cloud-burst
it is about as handy as a pineapple.
* * * *
To say that a military system is hap-
hazard, capricious and unjust in time of war
is merely a polite way of remarking that
the thing is hideously dangerous. Armies
are wanted for war, not for peace, and to
go on clinging to what is haphazard in
organising for defence is no more sensible
than it would be to resist the employment
of long-range ordnance and high-explosive
shells, in order to have the privilege <>f
assailing the enemy with bows and arrows.
For a century John Bull has been encour-
aged by the peculiar circumstances associ-
ated with his insular position and liis un-
challenged naval ascendancy to regard the
maintenance of voluntary service in the
army as one of the props of the Constitu-
tion. Opposition to national service became
with him a cult. As he got along fairly
well without it the idea became fastened
in bis mind that he owed his liberties
and his progress and all the rest of
it to freedom from “compulsion”—that his
navy would guard him against invasion
and keep the seas open to his commerce,
and that the army which was strong enough
for what was doing when Wellington
fought in Spain would rise triumphant over
any call that could possibly be made upon
its devotion at any time soever. The delu-
sion has been systematically nurtured by
Splendid Isolationists like Gladstone, and
pacificists like Bright and visionaries like
Cobden ; it received encouragement from
commercial and financial interests, German
and otherwise; it became embedded in the
doctrines of every political party—in that of
the Conservatives because, for one thing,
they regarded a career in the army as the
special privilege of the wealthy ; in that of
the Liberals because they imagined military
weakness to be the complement of Freetrade;
and in that of the Radicals because to them
“conscription” savored of oppression. So
universal service became a thing to be jeered
at, to be intolerantly rejected by public meet-
ings at Peckham Rye as “un-English,” and
“our splendid volunteer system” developed
into a catchcry—vain and futile as all such
phrases are—not because that system would
stand the only test by which it should be
judged, nor even because it embodied an
expression of intelligent principle, but
because the people had been bemused by a
false reading of history and by unsound
judgments on the course of contemporary
events.
From the argument always advanced by
philosophic exponents of the virtues of the
voluntary system—that a large army was a
danger to the people from whom it was drawn
because it could be made an instrument
of Jingo adventure, conquest and oppression
in the hands of unscrupulous rulers and
Parliaments —two gross errors stand up like
lamp-posts. One is that the argument
ignores what is going to happen to the
nation against which this instrument is
turned by another; the second is that, when
applied to a democracy, the meaning of the
argument is that the democracy cannot trust
itself. .In Germany the military strength of the
nation lias been prostituted, and the nation
turned into an international house-
breaker. But it has not been so in France,
nor in Russia, nor Italy nor Servia. By
those countries the men of the nation are
standing up against a brigand, and had they
not been Teady to do it—had they depended
on the system described by Asquith as
“haphazard”—the brigand would have won.
John Bull is merely learning to-day a lesson
that has been taught to the world ever since
the first man set out with a club —that
what a nation has it must be ready to de-
fend by force. Unless it is ready, then,
sooner or later, an attempt will be made to
employ force against it. From this comes
the inescapable conclusion that participation
in defence must be a duty of citizenship—
that every man must be prepared to defend
his country, the liberties of his race and the
freedom of his children. The only alterna-
tives to that are to let things slide or to do
the best that can be done by inviting people
to come along at the last minute and take
lessons in shooting when the enemy is
climbing over the back fence. That is
what John Bull has been doing. And now
it is no longer a matter of being excessively
proud of it, but of wondering just how
much longer dependence for life is going to
rest on a farce that worked with great suc-
cess when no one’s skin was in danger.
According to Asquith it ought to continue
about five minutes. According to a num-
ber of dreary dodderers it should be kept
going—through injustice and unnecessary
suffering A-to the bitter, wretched end.
WAR AND MORALS.
An Adelaide parson of the uttermost
respectability has ventured to echo the sug-
gestion of English scientists that the present
upheaval must make a big difference in con-
ventional morality. And that means all
morality, for all morality is conventional.
In one country the most blameless woman
will go about in public bare above the waist;
in another such an action would be out of
bounds to the most degraded. In one coun-
try a man with four wives is perfectly
moral, because law and custom favor that
extravagance; in another he would be a
monster of iniquity, because they don’t favor
it- —though if the law and custom of that
country happened to change he would be as
moral as the other man. Some of the most
popular characters in history—. Jacob, for
instance, and David —had more than one
wife at a time, and nobody thinks of blam-
ing them. Our own conventional morality
admits its folly when it rules that, of two
people coinbining to have a child without
any marriage ceremony, the man is excus-
able but the woman isn’t. It could
without any great, shock change its capri-
cious mind and say that she also is excus-
able ; and signs are not wanting that this is
what it is going to do.
* * * *
Population is the crying need of the stir-
ring age we are thrown in. If this war be-
comes, one of exhaustion, the side that can
heave'the final half-million men into the gap
will w T in; and the nation —presumably
Russia —that has a spare million or two 20
years hence will be able to talk pretty con-
fidently to its less heavily populated neigh-
bors. And how is even the normal growth of
population going to be kept up in an age
when a completely abnormal growth is
demanded? The marriageable men are
being killed every day. The marriageable
women are not. Australia and .England and
Russia will notice a disproportion. In Ger-
many and France and Belgium the dispro-
Dortion will be staggering. With strict
monogamy still enforced, millions —-literally
millions —of splendid women now just grow-
ing up must go unmated. That is, as the
Adelaide parson suggested, they must, for
no fault of their own, lose the full meaning
of their womanhood; also the nations they
belong to must lose their enormously valu-
able potentialities as repairers of the waste
caused by war.
Well, what is going to be done about it?
»So far as Nature decrees, there might be
50 women for every one man, and still the
next generation need not run short. To
run counter to the conventional morality of
centuries is hard work, all the same. The
first thing would be to cease to make the
unmarried mother feel herself in any way
outside the pale. Europe is rapidly taking
that step. It already encourages her to
declare herself a victim of the horrors of
war —which may or may not be true in any
given case—and presently, in its gratitude
to her for helping to enrich her country, it
will not trouble to ask any questions at
all. In Australia she has only to say that
the father of her child has been killed at
Gallipoli to gain a kindly sympathy which
would have been utterly lacking a year or
two back. The maternity bonus has already
in Australia ceased to be a joke and become
a plain business question. As other nations
adopt it, they can and will increase it im-
mensely. Comfortable and perfectly ap-
pointed and free maternity hospitals follow
as a matter of course, with a continuing
provision for the child as ward of the State.
The one really difficult step is that at start-
ing.
* * * *
In any case, it is all merely a phase that
will pass. The children who are growing
up. and the children hereafter to be born,
will balance in the matter of sex as hereto-
fore. In 25 years or so the discrepancy that
will immediately follow the war will cease
to be of importance. The business during
that time will be to see that the healthy
women now aged from 15 to 40 do not —as
they otherwise must—live their lives un-
mated ; to see that the time when births are
of unusual importance shall not be just the
time when births become scarce. It will Ife
work for statesmen who can defy the wow-
ser and think of the country’s good; and it
will be most interesting to see how they will
set about it —in Belgium !
THE SHAKEN FAITH OF THE SMALL
NATIONS.
In spite of the small and shuddering gleam
which Asquith’s speech lias shed on
the national darkness, there is a
decently optimistic view which can rea-
sonably be taken of the situation. In
the face of a big, dangerous enemy
who has thus far had more successes than
reverses, a. British Prime Minister can still
openly acknowledge his mistakes, boldly
claim the responsibility for them, and declare
that he will stick at nothing to bring the
big fellow at. last to the dust. The absence
of apology, cringe or special pleading on the
part of those who are doing the muddling
through is worth something, and it may yet
prove to be worth a great deal. The
temper of all the British peoples is such
that no failure on the part of frank and
honest leaders will entirely discourage them.
Tied by cords of antique superstition and
dragging after her weighty chains of custom,
England has little freedom uf movement; and
taking this into full account her organising
efforts of the last 12 months have been re-
markable. That those organising efforts
should have been made 20 years late, and
that they are even to-day incomplete, cannot
wholly destroy their effectiveness; and it is
at least even-chances that enough has been
done up-to-date to keep the ITell-broth of
battle boiling while the completed national
effort is being made. The history of the
past goes to show that a Britain heartily
walloped, or at least stung by partial defeat,
has produced a Britain strong and deter-
mined and capable; and there is at least a
decent chance that history will repeat itself
in the present instance.
* * * *
But it must be Britain, and not France or
Russia, that wins out if her national prestige
is to remain when the last shell bangs into
a discomfited and broken foe. Fifteen months
ago Belgium, in an agony of surprise for the
thing that had burst upon her, called to Eng-
land for aid. England, true to her treaty
obligations and her traditions, sent a force;
but it was utterly inadequate, and it was sent
too late. The war with its chances and changes
boomed on through months of pain and
death, and then Servia, in more insistent and
heartbroken tones, called from her hungry
hills for British help. Taught by ex-
perience in the case of Belgium, British
statesmen responded to the appeal with a
result that cannot yet be known; but all the.
present indications are that the help sent is
insufficient, and that once more it lias been
dispatched too late. Should English forces
in the Balkans redeem the situation and
march through triumphantly to the Danube
the lesson of the opening stages of the war
will have been well learned ; but should the
southern European campaign resolve itself
into a repetition of the stalemate of the
western Front, the world’s faith in England
will have been shaken to such an extent that
Avliile her failures may be atoned for they
can never be altogether forgiven. No national
crime can be greater than that of encourag-
ing small and weak peoples to put their
trust in the orotection of a Power which will
not make adequate sacrifices to ensure that
protection being effective. * ,
All present indications point to the fact
that Britain with the assistance of heY Allies
will muddle through. The Germans are
held securely enough on the west; winter
fights with the Russians on the east; hunger
bites in Berlin; Italy keeps a lot of Aus-
trians busy in the Trentino and elsewhere.
The one big German chance is a histrionic
display at the gates of Constantinople. But
the Kaiser would much rather hear Asquith
declare in the name of both British political
parties that there would be no conscription
under any circumstances than enter those
historic portals. With her whole available
male population under arms England will
more than merely win. Berlin knows this quite
as well as it knows the price of sausaged
war-horse. But the delay fights the Teuton
battle and helps the Kaiser to dream of a
peace made by the Pope and Woodrow
Wilson in which lie may still masquerade
as a world conqueror. Asquith’s speech
makes it plain that the temper of England
is all right. Whether the war lasts one year
or 17 England will nmadle through. But
there is nothing to indicate how long it
will take to mop up the remembrance of the
blood spilled in Belgium and Servia which
might never have been spilled if Britain had
drilled her manhood in the cause of peace.
One thing is certain. In the better days
to come, when the guns are quiet and men
look hack at the record of things done in
Cabinet and field, the person who mentions
the British habit of muudling through as if
it were a national virtue will deserve to
have a brick thrown at him without warning
or explanation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Peaceful Arrangement to Mark Time.
Partly because the thing had been badly
stage-managed and the progress of the war
has not been according to schedule, but
mainly because the r chances of securing a
favorable verdict were unpleasantly blue,
Commonwealth Prime Minister Hughes
looked for a soft place to fall on, and the
party behind him was just as anxious as
he to save its face. Therefore it happens
that a method of procedure, previously
rejected because of its inadequacy, has been
agreed to by the Federal .Government: the
Referenda proposals have been withdrawn,
and the Commonwealth, if all goes well, will
by the grace of the States get for the period of
the war'and a year afterwards a substantial
part of the powers asked for under the sug-
gested constitutional amendments. From
llolman and Joe Cook and the S. M.
Herald and others baskets of flowers have
been handed up to Hughes, and from the
serried ranks of Lib. and Lab. has come
a chorus : “Thank God, that’s over, anyhow !”
Twelve months after the Great Disturbance
has ceased the old job will be waiting for
someone to tackle afresh. Meanwhile the
Commonwealth will, for “the purposes of
carrying on the war,” have a wider
authority. But it is only partially true that
the powers to be conferred were wanted
for this purpose. The Referenda proposals
arose out of conditions associated with
peace, and it is impossible to see; how that,
so far as the purely w r ar activities of the
Commonwealth are concerned, these.extended
powers are going to be of tremendous
material value! The merit about*'them in
their original form was that they contem-
plated a permanent limitation, of State
powers, and represented a step towards the
establishment of One sovereign authority in
the government of Australia. In this direc-
tion the advance now achieved, pending sub-
sequent retreat, represents about half an
inch, and this paper is much too tired of
such piffling substitutes for Unification to
join in the hymn of joy raised by party bar-
rackers of, every brand over the great achieve-
ment.

Groan, Brother, Groan!
(Dedtrnted in any and every persinist.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Groan, Brother! Groan your dismal song
Of wrack and ruin and schemes gone wrong!
Look out, look down, with a bilious eye
And con the world with your brain awry!
Never a spark of hope you see
For poor, deluded humanity!
Your wearisome croak is heard all day
Of debts and devils we’ve got to pay.
The past is dead and the future’s black,
And the walls of Heaven begin to crack!
Groan, Brother! Groan, if Groan you must,
Till worlds stop spinning and white stars
bust,
And the molten yolk of each one pours
Line red egg-meat on Hell’s sizzling floors!
Groan till angels on wings of cloud
With joys of Heaven come bent and bowed,
And the fairest of all, as in air she stands,
Takes your crying soul in her lilied hands
And bears it away to the Central Throne.
(Oh yes, dear brother, in Heaven we’ll
Groan.)
Groan, Brother! Groan! The Halls of
Grace
Are doubtless a very draughty place;
The tremulous throb of golden harps,
Sounding their numberless flats and sharps,
Will tear at your nerves till, cold and lone,
You utter a most tremendous Groan,
And a senior angel from afar
Flies like a racing motor-car —
Soars like a bird and drops like a stone
To find who uttered the Heavenly Groan.
Groan, Brother !. Groan ! ’Tis your employ
And pessimism’s your only joy!
Groan for the follies and faults of men
And when you’re dead you will Groan again!
Groan in Heaven and Groan in Hell
Till Satan watches his hot tail swell,
And devils sit by their fires asleep,
And angels droop at their harps and
weep....
Misery! Misery! Slcin and hone!
What laugh contents like a hearty Groan!
N.S.W.

overtime. The enormous quantity of war
material required was one of the surprises
or the present struggle. Germany may not
have been taken unawares, but certainly the
other nations had no idea that anything like
so much fireworks would be required. A
sufficient supply of munitions of war to
guarantee peace will be one of the problems
of the future, and with the invention of new
and improved weapons this, rather than the
number of men in an army, may come to be
regarded as the index of a people’s strength.
But here the difficulty arises that munitions
of war are by reason of those very inven-
tions particularly had stock. Not the muni-
tions, but the skilled men and intelligent
machinery to make them, must therefore be
the first consideration. Australia will be wise
to learn this in time. Her citizen army, being
necessarily limited in size, even if increased
to its true dimensions by taking in every
able-bodied man who can hold a gun, must
be doubly well supplied with things to throw
at the enemy. As these cannot be made in
large supply beforehand without locking up
national capital in material which a bril-
liant idea of an inventor may render obso-
lete, it follows that they must be stored in
the skilled fingers and trained brains of our
mechanics. Let every lad on being apnren-
ticed to one. of the engineering trades have
included in his training a certain period
spent in munition-making workshops. Let
every chemist be compelled to pass a prac-
tical examination in the compounding of
explosives. Let no tailor be allowed the
full rate of trade wages unless lie has
learned to satisfactorily make a military
uniform. Carry out the. same idea in every
calling and keep the general machinery
handy, and then when llie war-gong clangs
an army of workers can take its place
behind the armies in the field, and the nation
can rest in confidence that war supplies
will be fully forthcoming when needed. This
is no more than Germany did. It is less, in
fact; for every German workshop, on the
outbreak of any war, automatically stopped
its ordinary job and turned to the particular
national work to which it had been allotted.
The other way is to leave things to chance
and throw a jam-tin and a sheep’s head at
the enemy before going out to challenge him
with a crowbar.

National Waste.
Thus William Cokin, Chief Electrical
Engineer in the Public Works Department
of N. S. Wales, concerning only one item in
the appalling waste that proceeds in Aus-
tralia -
Last year 304,800 tons of coke were manufactured
in N. S. Wales, of which 252,409 tons were made
on the Southern coalfields. At the new iron and
steel works belonging to the B. H. Proprietary, at
Port Waratah, 66 by-product ovens have been
erected, and other examples of by-product recovery
exist in the Northern district; but so far as the
South is concerned, with one exception, viz., the
Oorriinal mine, the whole of the coke-oven gas is
wasted, and in no case is any attempt made to
recover the valuable by-products. If coke-ovens were
elected on a suitable site, with plant for the
recovery of ammonia and tar, and possibly benzine,
toluol, cyanide and other by-products, the whole of
the South Coast coke could be produced therefrom,
and in addition there would be available from the
gas no less than 140 million units, which, if the
industry be regarded as a whole, and the by-pro-
ducts be sold at market rates, could be supplied for
less than a farthing per unit delivered. It need
scarcely be pointed out that ammonia is being in-
creasingly required for refrigeration; that sulphate
of ammonia is a valuable fertiliser (and, though
more would be produced than is required at present
in this country, its export would add to the material
wealth of the State) ; that the large quantity of tar
resulting would enable a supply to be furnished at
low rates for improving the roads of the State ; and
that there is likely to .be a large demand for toluol
for the manufacture of tri-nitro-toluol (T.N.T.), if,
as seems probable, Australia relies upon herself for
her future supplies of explosives. Confidential figures
have been supplied by several British coke-oven
owners, showing conclusively the great profits which
are being made from this industry. This is evi-
denced by the increasing number of coke ovens with
recovery plants which are being employed in all
parts of the world. The four chief firms supplying
these plants within recent years constructed, up to
and including the year 1913, no less than 6540 ovens
in Great Britain and Ireland, of which 2662 were
constructed since the year 1908. One firm alone had
constructed, from 1902-1912 inclusive, 5088 ovens
in Germany, 1507 in the United States, and 1370 in
England.
When those who talk most about “national
economy” mention the subject they are three
times out of four thinking that somebody or
other ought to spend a shilling or two a week
less on beer or tobacco or jam or millinery
or something-. But if the things that are
wasted in this richest country in the world
wore used, there wouldn’t be any need for
the population to cut out a tin of jam or a
new hat. There is, in fact, enough going-
over the dump in Australia to buy a lot of
extra jam and quite two more hats.

Some French Naval History, with an
Australian Application.
As there is a lot of thoroughly well-deserved
admiration about for France, the French, and
French methods generally, perhaps the time
may be considered more or less matured for
imitation of a comparatively recent French
reform. After the war of 1870-71 the French
navy and its dockyards became a sort of
freak or toy for the politicians and “groups”
to play 'with. The inevitable result was that
the fleet became a collection of marine
curios, while the dockyards were turned into
extremely comfortable semi-charitable insti-
tutions wherein the Government super-stroke
flourished like the green absinthe-tree. While
John Bull was putting his pre-Dread-
noughts into commission 80 months from the
time of laying the keels,' and' while the Ger-
man Michael was doing almost as well, the
-average French battleship built in the Gov-
ernment yards was found to take seven
years to complete. In fact, ships of the
. Charles Martel class took nearly nine
years to send to sea. France, of course, in
‘the ’Bo’s' and ’9o’s was full of troubles,
scandals, “reorganisations,” war-scares and
Dreyfus cases, and consequently nobody
bothered much about the fleet, which became
the Cinderella of the nation. Then, in
1900, came the German Navy Law and the
Kaiser’s welt-macht speeches. What, pri-
marily, was a challenge to England was seen
by the logical and brainy French to be
equally a menace to them. They had
definitely abandoned the idea of naval com-
petition with Britain, but their traditions
and their interests alike forbade them meekly
to acquiesce in playing third fiddle when
they had so long played second. In 1908-9
by which year the Mailed Fist had really
commenced to make a splash in the North
Sea —the French were seriously studying
the position. Even with the Inscription
Maritime (introduced by the great Colbert
in 1009), it was found that the country was
paying about £15,000,000 a year for what
Delcasse described as “a naval menagerie.” A
special Commission was appointed to inquire
into things, and its conclusions, in the form
of a report of 220 pages, and 730 pages of
evidence, so shook the Government that the
Minister for Marine was promptly jettisoned,
and France’s best sailor, Admiral Boue de
Lapeyrere, was put at the head of the De-
partment and given almost limitless powers.
As a result of De Lapeyrere's efforts France
has now quite a respectable and efficient
fleet, which at the close of the war will pro-
bably rank in its old place. This ancient —
yet not-so-very-ancient—history has an Aus-
tralian application. The French Parlia-
mentary Commission reported, inter alia.
that once a ship had been authorised, instead
of the dockyard getting promptly and ener-
getically to work upon the job, endless alter-
ations and modifications were made in the
plans by all the faddists on deck. (As at
Cockatoo Dock, N.S.W.) Again, the Com-
mission pointed out “that the yards were
not in a state to carry out with rapidity new
construction and repairs.” (Just as at
Cockatoo.) Also, “the plant and mechanical
equipment were in general inadequate and
out of date” (the same as at Cockatoo).
Furthermore, “the abolition of piecework,
which had coincided with a reduction of
working hours -and the diminution of the
powers and authority of the superinten-
dents in chargej had resulted in an alarming
lessening of production.” (Ditto at Cocka-
too.) In addition, “lack of material some-
times caused-a stoppage of work.” (Precisely
as., at Cockatoo.) And the Commission
recommended (and the recommendation was
acted upon) “that there be an immediate re-
introduction of piecework, distributed and
remunerated in an equitable manner,”. . . .
while “the authority of the foremen and
heads of departments should be strengthened
by increasing their power of dispensing pro-
motion, rewards and punishments.” All of
this applies most closely to the awful institu-
tion alleged to be run by the Commonwealth
Government at Sydney. Australia can have
one of two things. She can have (1) Cocka-
tooism as it is at present and an expensive
and futile and out-of-date fleet: or (2) no
Cockatooism and a fleet worthy of her
ambitions.

Towser.
“Carfax” reports the decline and fall of
Towser: —
He was one of tlic bulldog breed, lean and hungry to
look at, and bearing many scars. But he carried Ins
head aloft, and his roving eye looked so balefully
at the cat that Thomas slunk away. And there was
no droop in the tyke’s tail.
“D'yer want a pup, boss?” asked the tattered
pirate who alleged he was the owner. Well, 1
didn’t, but it struck me that that brute of Samp-
son’s next door was getting a bit of a nuisance; so
Towser changed hands there at the back door.
He had been reared in a hard school, had Towser,
having apparently been left to shift .for himself at
the earliest age. Feeding him was at first unneces-
sary. He preferred the larcenous meals of a high-
wayman. On the morning after his arrival on the
premises the butcher drove up to deliver a pound
of chops. Being in a respectable neighborhood
and a quiet street, he left the tailboard of his cart
down. Towser, entrenched behind a rosebush, ad-
vanced silently to the banquet and bore off two or
three feet of choice steak. The butcher was still
wondering where that steak went when, three days
later, he hurried back to his cart and detected
Towser attached to a leg of mutton. That cost me
ss. 6d.
Next evening Towser was enjoying a bone when
Sampson's tvke appeared for the first time —a big,
hairy animal which had vanquished everything on
four legs in the district. “Ha ! a bone. Right into
my hands!” and he proceeded to the act of appro-
priation. It was a meatless bone, too. But as
the stranger drew close Towser’s teeth were bared
across the prize, and an ominous growl warned the
district robber that if he wanted that bone he would
have to fight for it. And fight they did —a sharp,
desperate battle from which a black dog, with
torn throat and bleeding ear, vanished, into Samp-
son's, howling with agony and surprise. The inci-
dent led to a very marked coldness between my wife
and Mrs. Sampson, who had known each other for
40 years. Sampson’s dog kept within Sampson’s
frontier for the rest of his days, and died eventually
of dropsy.
For a long time Towser fought every dog that
appeared in our street. Some he openly challenged
to combat and advanced upon with threatening noise.
These were generally dogs of obese habit of body—-
fat, waddling dogs that were short in the wind and
accompanied ladies on afternoon-tea expeditions. On
these occasions the discreet suburban air was filled
by the squeaks of old lady dog-owners and the
frantic yelps of vanquished poodles. The other sort
of dog—the one that looked like a snag or one hav-
ing a punch about it; the kind young boys are fond
of taking exercise with—found themselves up against
it all of a sudden, so to speak. There was no pre-
liminary declaration of war. Towser would hurl
himself at them from the rear without remark and
get to work with the silent resolution of a cracks-
man. He was lord , of the street and all the neigh-
boring lanes, the terror of kids and old maids and
corpulent old gentlemen ; a brazen thief who got away
with ham bones and sausages from adjacent sculleries
and drove all the cats to permanent habitation on
the tiles.
In- time, however, I began to notice that Towser’s
circumference was increasing. He became fond of
dozing in the sun, of leaving a bone With meat on
it outside his kennel while he slumbered between
chews. One warm evening a strange little tyke hap-
pened into the yard to explore the rubbish tin, and,
seeing a bone near a sleeping clog, went to try his
luck. He had home too soon. But the fight was
quite interesting, and, though the prospector of rub-
bish tins retreated, he .did so. with his tail pointing
to the stars. Towser was out of breath, and his tail
pointed the other way. He went into his kennel and
slept. .
It was not long afterwards that Bn, no came round
with rabbits, and, stalking behind him, a wicked-
looking brute with a spotted hide and a heavy head,
hungry and gaunt. I had just placed before Towser
the usual dish of assorted meats and very nice gravy,
and he was about to pick out the best-looking piece,
when with a growling bound the rabbit-o’s pup
shoved an unlovely-looking face into his own. Tow-
ser drew back with a snarl, then a whimper, and
leaped for his kennel! And as he looked out he
saw a long red tongue gratefully lick the dish that
was his. Towser had joined the Peace Society.
The fleas are thoroughly satisfied.
An Experiment that Was Squashed in
the Egg.
Tasmania’s attempt to abolish party war-
fare during the Great Disturbance has ended
disappointingly. The Assembly agreed,
almost unanimously, to postpone the general
elections for a year, three members of the
Opposition (Lee, Lewis and Evans) to act
in a consultative capacity with the Earle
Ministry. The parties pledged themselves
to stick to the agreement for six months,
whatever happened ; but if the pact proved
unworkable either party might then end it by
giving the other side a month’s notice. It
would have been an interesting experiment,
but the Last Say Chamber, furious at its
“Liberal” allies in the Assembly even sug-
gesting an armistice with the hated Labor
Government, flung the proposal out even more
unanimously than the Assembly had agreed
to it. Then the Assembly Libs, promptly
handed a no-confidence motion to John
Earle, and the discussion thereon is now
raging as violently as party bitterness knows
how. There does not seem to be very much
for the parties to fight about. The hostile
motion is apparently moved on general prin-
ciples. The Opposition declares that the
Government’s very modest Public Works pro-
posals might be and ought to be cut down,
and demands that the cutting down shall be
done, not by Parliament in the usual way, but
by a joint committee or combined secret
caucus of six, the abbreviated result to
be presented to the House for formal endorse-
ment. As the independent party of
Whitsitt says he intends to support the
Government, the no-confidence motion will
probably be negatived by a majority of one.
But as an election is now inevitable within a
few months, the necessity of mounting the
party stump and saying a few words through
Parliament to the electors is more pressing
than it was. Hence the outbreak of hostilities.
The Labor Government naturally wants to
get a new Electoral Bill up to the doorstep
of the Fee-Fo-Fum Chamber before Parlia-
ment expires next January ; also a Referen-
dum proposal, and a proposal to increase
death duties, and a Necessary Commodities
and Prices Control Bill. The time taken up in
nominally discussing the want of confidence
motion will, however, cut into the available
time so severely that there will be little left
over after the Estimates are bullocked
through. Clearly the Libs are out for ob-
struction. so the lot of the Labor crowd isn’t
happy. They may do something desperate in
their dire extremity, and if a dissolution
happens within a. few weeks and the general
elections are fixed for December, nobody, bar-
ring a few highly-unimaginative Libs, need
be astonished.
Tins is Tiie Bulletin’s special report of
Asquith’s speech; none others are genuine:
I am not going to defend myself, because I speak
as head of the Government. That’s the job to which,
in the days of peace, I was elected, and, personally,
I see no reason why I cannot conduct a war just as
beautifully as I conduct elections. Things are rot-
ten at present; but are we downhearted? (Cheers.)
All we have to do to win this war is to possess a
proper sense of perspective, a limitless stock of pati-
ence and an overflowing reservoir of courage. Organi-
sation or energy doesn’t matter. Just preserve your
perspective, be very, very patient, and very, very,
very brave, and everything will come out all right.
(Cheers.) In fact, everything is all right. It is
true that we haven’t three million men at the Flan-
ders front; but we have nearly one million. (Loud
cheers.) Then there are the contingents from the
Dominions. (Cheers.) The most remarkable fact
is that hitherto the loss of life in the whole of these
gigantic oversea operations is less than one-tenth of
one per cent. (Cheers.) That is, of course, referring
only to military loss on the seas. The loss on land
is somewhat larger, though there is no need for me
at this moment to enlarge on the sickness at the
Dardanelles. (Cheers.) Anyway, lam not going to
apologise for the Government, for I am the Govern-
ment. It is true it .was guilty of many shortcomings
and mistakes, but it means to muddle through in the
grand old Bulldog wa}'. (Loud cheers.) And it is
well to remember that there are always the Rus-
sians. (Cheers.) Also Mesopotamia. (Loud cheers.)
It is well to recall what a blessed word is Mesopo-
tamia. We have also nearly got to Bagdad. (Cheers.)
Bagdad—not Aehi Baba. Lord Fisher didn’t like
the naval attack upon the Dardanelles; but then
Lord Fisher wasn't the Cabinet, and everybody
knows that the Cabinet is the finest possible instru-
ment for winning the war. Even the War Council
approved of it, and, also the Grand Duke Nicholas.
(Cheers.) But Kitchener hadn't any troops to spare,
so I decided to make the attempt without them.
(Cheers.) I made a mistake —(Cheers) —but I admit
it. (Cheers.) I fully admit it. (Renewed cheers.)
No one was more responsible than I. (Tremendous
cheers.) I also admit that when we did send the
troops, I was keenly disappointed because they were
sent too late. (Cheers.) But the nation must not
forget the magnificent bravery of the Australians,
(Cheers.) And if the Allies are too late to save
Servia, it must be remembered that up,to the last
moment our diplomacy believed in Greece. (Cheers.)
Why ? Our diplomacy was wrong. I admit it was
wrong. (Cheers.) Servia, or what is left of Servia,
may be assured that her independence, or what is
left of it, is regarded by us as one of the essential
objects of the Alliance. (Cheers.) The financial
position is, of course, bad. But Germany’s is worse.
(Cheers.) Voluntary recruiting is haphazard and
capricious and unjust. But compulsion would for-
feit the maintenance of national unity. (Cheers.)
So would defeat, but I am not going to enlarge on
that subject. (Cheers.) There ought to be a War
Cabinet, but so far I haven’t appointed one. (Cheers:)
And, personally, I ain’t going to resign. (Cheers.)

If it weren’t so disastrous to the State it
would be amusing to wateli the efforts
of The Age to scrape the Peacock
Ministry off the rock. The combina-
tion boasts a following of less than one-
third of the House; but the other two-
thirds will not coalesce to heave it out. Of
■course no real work is clone or can be done.
There is urgent need of a Greater Melbourne
Bill, an effective Public Health Act, and
other measures that the public has long been
looking for —and will continue to look for
until it has sense enough to drop the Party
system stupidity, and insist on Elective
Ministries, with the Initiative and Referen-
dum. While the Age earnestly calls on Pea-
cock to turn it up it offers nothing in his
place. Thb Tory corner is unthinkable; the
Labor Opposition is anathema ; so the only
choice is among Peacock’s private followers
—a matter of Tweedledum and Tweedledee.
Meanwhile the illogical journal meSiodically
opposes every movement to transfer to the
National Parliament any of the powers now
unused or neglected by the petty State
legislatures.
According to new Prime Minister Hughes,
the proper attitude of Australians in connec-
tion with the war is to “mind their own
business”—to train and equip men for ser-
vice, and not discuss or criticise the conduct
of the campaign. What fine, crusted old
Toryism!
God bless the Squire and his relations,
And keep us in our proper stations.
The war is a matter of transcendent im-
portance to Australia, and for the nation to
carry on such a struggle with its head in a
bucket is equivalent to the surrender of its
self-governing rights. So far as this paper
is aware, no Parliament in the world ever
submitted to a war being waged by the
country it represented without wanting to
know something about the disturbance. In
France, in England, in Russia, even in Ger-
many, the war is almost the sole subject of
Parliamentary discussion. And the proposi-
tion that Australia’s place in the Empire is
that of a mere sleeping partner—an inferior
person who must be told nothing and keep
his mouth shut —is one to which the utmost
disrespect is due.

“Rag” : The paymaster’s window at Victoria
Barracks, Sydney, presents a woeful spec-
tacle these times. Any day there is a crowd
of maimed Gallipoli warriors, ranking from
bugle-boy to major, jostling for the money
we owe them. When a man battles his way
to the window, lie delivers his book, and is
informed that he is in credit (say) £5O. He
asks for it, and is informed that “the most
we can do for. you is (say) £5. Come again
on. Monday, and we may be able to let you
have a few more.” “But, damn it, man, I’ve
my wife and children stopping with me at
tlie hotel, and I owe this much already.”
“Can’t be helped,” grunts the clerk, and that
settles it. One officer, after waiting with
others for three hours, was given a cheque
on the Commonwealth Bank, but upon pre-
senting it he was informed that it was; only
negotiable through an account. Not know-
ing anyone handy who ran an account be
had to wait until next day to have it
“opened” by the pay-clerk at the- Barracks.
Upon presenting it at the bank a second
time he was told that it was not in order
owing to whoever made it out having for-
gotten to endorse it with the rubber stamp
of the pay office. I met him as he was re-
turning to try his luck for the third time.
It was all right, but he swore that he’d pre-
fer to charge an enemy’s machine-gun across
open country than contend here for his
arrears of pay. Every returned soldier I’ve
questioned says likewise.
“Real Australian” : Japan stirred up a
remarkable agitation in China when she
forced upon her an ultimatum. A “National
Salvation Fund Movement” was started to
raise £5,000,000 to arm the Republic,
establish arsenals and develop home
industries. The fund is open to Chinese
only, and beggars, coolies, schoolboys
and merchants are subscribing. A national
boycott against Japanese goods in China
was started at the same time. The
Pekin (Chinese) Chamber of Commerce
sent out a stirring anneal, headed
“Never Forget the Nation’s Disgrace!” In
Hankow and Hangchow 200 Japanese shops
were closed, and in the latter city was
formed “The United Association for Advis-
ing the Nation to Use - Native Goods.” It
is really a very serious position for Japan.
The leading men of the movement assert
there' are 200,000,000 males in China, and
they have asked the Government to raise
an army of 10,000,000, of whom one million
shall always be on active service. Should
this new spirit of solidarity amongst the
Chinese gather force and spread, Japan has
a contract ahead of her. What a fine
national .Spirit is revealed in the titles of
these sodalities —“The National Salvation
Fund,” “The Society to Propagate the Use
of National G00,.5” and “The Society for
the Protection of Native Trade.” The
associations are opening “Show-houses for
Native Goods” everywhere. Meanwhile the
people themselves are demanding heavier
taxes and Government iteform so as to nre-
pare properly for national defence. The
President has created a Board of Censors
whose job,, it is to criticise all governmental
action and suggest administrative and
economic improvements. That’s a charming
idea; and when, the Board has finished it
ought to be picnicked down to Australia to
tell the Governments. Jliat—buLmwins-money
to waste is not the way to build a strong,
self-reliant nation.

War by Shadowgraph.
News from the part of the world ou which
Australian eyes are fixed is still so meagre
and so unreliable that discussion about the
struggle, except in its very broadest
aspects, has become a more or less pathetic
sort of joke. What our army is doing;
what it is going to do; what steps are being
taken to provide it with winter clothing and
shelter; where it is going to winter; what
has been done to grapple with the heart-
breaking ravages of sickness among the
men ; whether it is going to be reinforced —
on these matters not one authoritative word
has been spoken. They are points about
which the Australian people should have
information. To withhold it is a scandal too
gross for defence. In every other country
engaged in the war the people are at least
told something about the welfare of their
troops. Australians are told nothing. Their
Minister for Defence Is a mere clerk. All
this is horribly unfair to the men who are
fighting and to the families they have left
behind them. It blindfolds the nation and,
worst of all, acts as a powerful influence
against recruiting. The gag that a por-
tentous silence was necessary in Australia,
in order to keep the enemy ignorant,
long ago lost whatever litttle force it may
have had. It is not the enemy in Germany
who is being kept in the dark now. It is
the patriot in the next street —the patriot
who would turn on the administrators if he
was permitted to know of administra-
tive misdeeds. So scandals are hushed
up on the hypocritical preteilce that Pots-
dam must not learn of our weaknesses.
Meanwhile the position in the Balkans is
changing rapidly. German and Bulgarian
armies seem to have junctioned, the Ser-
vians are being steadily forced backwards,
and railway 1 communication between Con-
stantinople and Austria appears to be some-
where close at hand —-that is, unless the
Allied troops landed at Salonica and now
in touch with the Bulgar ran get astride of
that, line of communication. Apparently a
considerable force is being thrown ashore,
and as there are rumors of a Russian army
hovering ominously in the North, and as
Kitchener is going out to somewhere near
the. Front thereabouts, and as an Anglo-
Indian army is on the skyline near Bagdad,
the Hun and his friends in the baggy pants
are. going to have a hard row to hoe. But
it is a vague tangled-up yarn that comes
along from day to day, the only certain
things being that the Servians are going
astern and that the Greek is sitting down
and being a friend to everybody while he
waits to see who carries the punch. All
things considered, the most amazing feature
of a wildly complicated business has been
the success of the crude bluff put up by the
Greek. But to get very much further with-
out stubbing his toe he will need the luck of
a Chinaman.
■*- — ■
This is the way of the Defence Depart-
ment, which says that munitions are the
most important thing of all. As long ago
as. last June certain patriotic citizens of
Williamstown (Vic.) formed'a co. for the
purpose of manufacturing machine guns, and
endeavored to obtain drawings and plans or
the loan of a gun to enable them to get busy.
First they were told it would take six
months to turn out a gun (they didn’t ex-
pect it to be done in a week) ; then it was
said it would take two years (the war pro-
mises to last quite that long) ; then they
were told there were neither guns nor plans
available, and they are no further ahead
than they were When they started. We
could not make shell-cases, it was said!
They are being made. We could not build
complete aeroplanes. They have been built.
And the North Melbourne firm that built
the . engine for the first flier declared its
willingness to build machine guns, yet so
far it has got no orders, while the Williams-
town company is blocked at every turn.
Will Minister Pearce cease expatiating on
the feats of his department and explain just
why Australia isn’t supplying its troops
with machine guns on the same scale as the
Huns are supplying the Turks?

“Clubman” on the Ford car controversy:
lie Ford, the motor-car manufacturer, and his
antagonism to the recent British war loan. The
“trade” story (B. 4/11/T5) endeavors to cloud the
fact that profits on Ford cars sold here go to Ford,
who is described as a “peace crank.” Apparently
his idea of peace is a peace to our disadvantage, if
hampering the British war loan is considered by him'
a step in the direction of bringing peace nearer.
The patriotism of firms and their employees who
happen to handle his cars under the British flag in
no way excuses his attitude, for they are but the
channel through which the money flows from us to
him ; neither does it condone the fact that he used
his money or threatened to use it to hinder the
British war loan. The “trade” story is that Aus-
tralia gets only Canadian cars. Well, who owns
the Canadian works? Or, rather, who. is the largest
owner of those works? If Ford is, then the
profits on the output go chiefly to a man who
“would tie a tincan to this joint Anglo-French Com-
mission, and chase it back to Europe.”

The 2nd Engineer’s report on a musical
steam trial: —
I wasn’t offered a programme when I arrived at
Verbrugghen’s concert in the Sydney Town Hall
last Saturday. But I didn’t worry about that. I
have enjoyed many a thunderstorm without a pro-
gramme, and I don’t suppose printed slips will be
passed around at the Last .Judgment. It sounded as if
the Judgment had come, when, right at the. end, the
brass division stood up in their high seats to get an
uninterrupted view of the conductor’s beat and
blew like three typhoons. The big, bell-mouthed
brute that looks like a fire-engine in convulsions
puffed deep elephant-grunts into the boiling pool of
violins below, and the enraged trombone stretched
and contracted its double neck and snorted madly
on the brink like the last of the dragons, while the
bass viol hippopotami plunged and grunted in the pool
where the trombone wanted to swim. Away up in the
opposite corner, right behind , the great kettledrums
and the side drums and the bass drum, a fierce,
elderly gentleman with spectacles and a short beard
swung a long, two-handed flail at the father of all
bass drums, while the nearest of the 3000 listeners
shrank back in their seats and tried to look unem-
barrassed as the flashing stick fanned their noses. It
was a truly noble row, in which even the smallest
and meekest contrivance had a part. And, by way
of contrast, there were times when the forest of
violin bows were still and the man behind the
kettledrum took a short holiday like the gentle-
men who buzzed through the wooden waterspouts
and boomed down the brass bath-lieaters, while a
bald-headed, retiring instrumentalist whose head just
showed above the rows in front tootled a gentle
little back-parlor ditty on something like an office-
ruler. One of the honorary hissers you can always
find at these shows had a seat behind me in the
gallery. He made death-adder sounds all the after-
noon at the people round about him, and thoroughly
enjoyed himself. If he had been near enough to the
balustrade 1 would have pushed him over. In the
awful silence just before one item some unlucky
brute dropped his boxer hat on the floor ; it sounded
like a bomb. I would like a heavy penalty imposed
on the woman who goes to crowded afternoon shows

“The Man Who Stayed at Home” was
awarded another week at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, by a grateful management. But he
catches his last spy in Sydney on Friday
evening. “Kick-in’' makes a start at this
theatre on Saturday with a new glossary of
U.S. slang. After one brief week it changes
over to the Criterion, and makes room for
“Paul Jones,” with Florence Young in the
lead.

To-night will be the last occasion at Syd-
ney Criterion when “A Pair of Sixes” will
win at poker. Next week the theatre will
be closed.
The Waybaeks, who have come down to
Sydney, will leave the Palace homestead to-
morrow evening and continue the harvesting
at the Royal. Dad reckons about two weeks
will see them through with the job there.

It is rumored that gentle Aggie has been
deported from the Little Theatre in Sydney,
where Howard Vernon and Vinia de Lioitte
still refuse to admit they are downhearted,
and affirm that reinforcements have been
engaged to take the place of the strenuous
amateur with whom the show opened.
But the reports from headquarters do
not make it clear whether when gentle
Aggie left she took the “bird” home
with her. Upon that the length of the
campaign under General “X” at the Little
Theatre will depend.
AT SYDNEY
TIVOLI.
Walter James, "King of the Laugh Trust,”
and Hazel Moran, from the Wild-vaudeville
West.
The open-air scheme of entertainment is
spreading in Sydney. Premier Holman
announces his intention to take a pitch in
Macquarie-street and compete for business
with the Violet Dandies, showing in the
Garden Theatre, Bayswater-road. The main
difference between the shows will be that
to Branseombe’s you take your best
girl; to Holman’s it will be better to take
your best corkscrew.
** * *
Walter James (who arrives roaring from
the U.S.) is one of the week-end novelties at
Sydney Tivoli, and Hazel Moran is the
other. Hazel is a stage cowlady who skips
lightly and draws beautiful falling curves
and circles in the air with a slim, snaky
rope. Violiniste Mary Law is now fiddling
away her last ‘nights, and cooniste Coy de
Trickey is still giving the audience the tip
that her complexion isn’t really smudgy by
leaving one white arm unpainted and letting
down her great golden mane.- Jim Harkins’s
boyish confidences, the Staffords’ merry out-
door turn, juggler Nolan’s fooling, and
Morris Golden’s fiddling are other bright
features of the show.

the old trio—the back-at-the-office husband, the neg-
lected wife, and the Other Man —occupy five acts in

Lyric : “No Greater Love” —Le Queux’s popular
