* * * *
“The Old Guard” surrenders on Friday at
Melbourne Majesty’s, but it doesn’t die.
Some day it will go into fiction again, and
then perhaps the inane fooling which has
ever been incidental to Murielle’s song will
be eliminated by order of the Censor. Poor
Murielle, who is Gladys Moncrieff in this
revival, is required to stand stock still and
look foolish whilst the Mayor and the Mar-
quis pretend to play cricket and football with
her top-note. This causes the groundlings
to titter and the more judicious to feel sick.
Nothing sillier was ever perpetrated by two
comedians acting in collusion. The spec-
tacle of those laborious entertainers feign-
ing to bowl, bat," batch or kick an invisible
ball, which is vaguely understood to be a
musical .sound—the spectacle of a pair of
often-amusing male mummers outcircusing
the wickedest antics of circus clowning,
whilst a sweet soprano voice waits mutely
for the supposititious ball to be thrown back
into her mouth that she may finish her song
—it is enough to make angels weep and The
Bulletin representative tear his burnished
scalp. Then he remembers that the wretched
fooling was born with the first production
of “The Old Guard,” and has been growing
ever since. The comedians are merely fol-
lowing precedent and trying to go one better.
It isn’t their fault; and sudden death would
be more than they deserve. But they ought to
be sharply spoken to. For next Saturday and
two following nights “Our Miss Gibbs”
will be revived, and on Wednesday matinde
and night “Ma Mie Rosettte” will conclude
the Royal Comics’ season.
* * * *
At Melbourne Royal four pieces of the
melodramatic season will be warmed up to-
night (Thursday) as a parting feast for the
gods. Muriel Starr and Millward are leav-
ing Melbourne to return no more, so, by way
of giving everybody one last chance to see
those performers in four different versions
of themselves, the management will present
them in one act of each of four plays—the act
of “Madame X” being No. 1, in which Muriel
Starr is just Muriel, and not the bedrugged
Madame of some years later. On Saturday
the newly-constructed J.C.W. Comedy Co.
comes to the Royal to ' play “A Pair of
Sixes.”
* H: * *
“A Scrape o’ the Pen” is nae drawin’ a’
the toon to the Princess’s, but it should
attract more people than are prepared to
love it for its ain Scawtch sake. Graham
Moffat’s play has at least an air of realism
in its homeliness and many touches of humor
in the dialogue. The thread of interest is
slender*, but it never breaks, and when at
last it has all been reeled off the audience
sits staring at the curtain until the little
orchestra plays the Anthem which spells
“Go!” The audience mostly seems sur-
prised to hear the band’s announcement.
Even the sudden arrival of the undertaker’s
man had not persuaded the crowd that the
play had breathed its last.
* * * *
The hero’s willingness to degrade himself
on a small point of honor remains the out-
standing feature of “O’Leary, V.C.,” at Mel-
bourne King’s. Warrior Allen Doom*
cheerfully throws up his commission merely
on the assumption that his sister’s supposed
“shame” is a smudge on his own military
record calling for his removal from the
company of brave men. The fact that his
sister is lawfully wedded makes Doone’s self-
sacrifice superfluous, but the lady' is not
allowed to mention the matter in his hearing
for fear of spoiling his great pose as a fal-
tered martyr bravely enduring the false
accusation of “Cow-ard!” while ruefully
gnawing crusts in a Parisian garret. Many
times her brother’s awful sufferings must
have tempted Eily Dale to open her mouth
and explain her position, but for the sake
of his limelight she maintains a silence that
is nothing less than an insult to the reputa-
tion of her sex.

New arrivals at the Tivoli in Melbourne
are Private Pearce from Gallipoli, who
sings songs of warlike sentiment in a good
baritone voice, and Betty Don. a lyric
soprano of the sweet and light class. Also
the agile dancing Tyrells are hack for ;i
time, and the Warwick Burlesquers and the
Belgian concertists are preceding the Follies
as before. There is heaps of stuff in the
Tivoli bill at present, and some of it is good
quality. By the way, the. Tivoli matinees
are now restricted to Wednesday and Satur-
day.

the other features of the Greatest Show on
Earth, are still at the Circus building in

For nearly a quarter of a century its enemies have been its best advertisement. Its serious
criticism begins just where all other publications leave off.
It never sets its sails to suit all breezes. It thrives on abuse. Don’t mind the squealers, but
get the Triad, and judge for yourself.

Three months ago Andrew Fisher said he
would “sooner be in the gutter than go back

“Nibbidard”: Henrietta Amelia Cochrane
is a young person of resource. With her
husband, John Andrew, she came before
Melbourne General Sessions last week on a
charge of forging a marriage certificate. The
Crown story was that Henrietta Amelia’s
father had refused to consent to her mar-
riage because she was not 18 years of age.
In those days she was Miss Kuelin. A
Collingwood parson, asked to provide the
connubial incantations, gave her a docu-
ment and told her that her father must sign
it. Two days later the couple were wed,
the signature being apparently in good
order and condition. Then the girl’s father
asked questions, and was told by the bride-
groom that “a man in the street” had signed
the paper. “I bound up my husband’s arms
so that he was unabje to write,” volunteered
the girl, “and the man in the street signed
the name given to iiim.” The jury smiled:
the Crown reduced the charge to one of
uttering; the pair, pleading guilty, were lec-
I.tired by ’s Onah, and then were released on
undertaking not to get married that way
any more.
R. W. Ilackett, of Adelaide, has got home
after being a prisoner in Germany for over a
year, Ilis travels took him to Hamburg
just as the war started, and he was allowed
lo stay there, this preference being given
to “colonials” when the British were in-
terned in the big central camp at Ruhleben.
They had to report to the police twice a
day, and stay in the house after 7 p.m. In
February, however, pressure was applied

from somewhere, and complete internment at
Ruhleben was enforced all round. Mr.
Hackett’s health stood it for two months
only, and after that he was in a sanitarium till
“exchanged” as an invalid at the beginning
of September. He describes the main camp
as reasonably run, and (except for the hard-
ships of a German winter) a fair thing for
a healthy man. Suggestions as to sanita-

were acted upon, a big cafe was available,
and sports, concerts and a first-class, orches-

but a British Relief Fund doled out to each
man ss. a week, to be repaid “when finan-
cially able.” Hackett’s finances won’t burst
under this strain. He is a son of the old
millionaire seedsman who died a few months
ago.

“T. 0.1.” : Mr. W. H. Ford, the father of
the stage the world over, and now resident
in a Melbourne suburb, passed into his 86th
year on Guy Fawkes Day. He appeared at
Astle.v’s, London, at six years of age; his
mother and grandmother were both connected
with that theatre. Ford came to Sydney in
1847, and built the first theatre in what is
now Queensland. Later, in Sydney, he
was the first manager of the old Scandi-
navian Music Hall, opened about 1870, on
the site of the Tivoli. Had “Old Ford”
stuck to sheep, gnd put the same work into
jumbucks that he did into variety shows, he
might have made a fortune. As it is
“E.N.M.”; Since “ILF.” —who is evi-
dently not a Turk, although he uses (B.
28/10/T5) the word “bosh” in the best
Stamboul manner—puts me iff the position
ot defender of the' “Philharmonic” pitch, as
against the “Continental” wicket or links, I
wish to point out that, despite “R.F.’s”
frantic denial, the Continental pitch is a
pretty deadly thing to all (which means all
in Australia) who have been used to the
Philharmonic. “R.F.” emphasises my point
as to its unsuitability for bands by remark-
coolly that the P.P. “is retained only by
military bands !” Also “by some firms of
pianoforte makers.” Yes —British, not Ger-
man ! And Australian, not Hungarian! As
for the voice, “R.F.’s” statement about Patti
and Sims Reeves is ridiculous and beside
the question. It all depended upon what
instrument maker those great artists were
singing for. If, say, for Broadwood, they
sang to Broadwood’s pitch (which isn’t
Continental) ; if for Bechstein ( Gott
strafe Bechstein!) —why, then, they sang for
Bechstein at Bechstein’s beastly old Con-
tinental pil^h.
“W.J.S.”: “R.F.’s” contention (B.
28/10/T5) that “English” pitch changes an
E flat symphony “virtually” into E natural
is untrue. Each key, G major, E flat major,
etc., has a certain definite mental effect. The
whole character . of, say, a hymn-tune is
changed by transposing to another key; and
competent organists frequently avail them-
selves of this knowledge when a hymn, set
to one tune, is sung to another. It is for
this reason that songs written for a soprano
are a failure when transposed for a mezzo-
soprano or for alto voice. This knowledge
wisely used is no small factor in deciding
whether Smith, Pulwiski or Tremolomolo is
the soundest musician. No change of pitch
alters the microscopic intervals of a key.
“J.F.H.”: London Incorporated Society
of Authors, Composers and Playwrights
issues a public warning against a very com-
mon and widely-prevalent form of robbery.
Church choirs and musical societies are in
the habit of buying one or two copies of a
work, and then with their pens multiplying
it for use and distribution amongst their
members, “in order to save the cost of pur-
chasing an adequate supply.” This sort of
thing is a breach of the Copyright Act,
which “entails serious loss to composers,”
and; the Society intends to use its sharpest
axe on offenders. All over the world many
good ladies have indulged in this music-
copying business, not realising that the pro-
perty created by a man’s brains is just as
much his own as the property created by
his hands, and equally entitled to protection.
“Busk” takes a few jerks backwards: —
Died in America a few weeks ago, Eleanor Carey,
known in Australia as Lena Carey. She first emerged
in Sydney from the ballet. Montgomery was fasci-
nated, and her friends wanted her to marry him,
but she said, “I won’t be sold to Montgomery.”
Anyhow, this was published in the newspapers. Lena
came to Melbourne, engaged by Coppin as leading
lady. Hattie Shepparde was in the company. Dr.
Neild, critic of the Australasian, championed
Hattie’s claim to be in the lead, but Coppin knew
better. Neild even changed his signature of “Jaques”
to “Tahite,” an anagram of Hattie. Great was his
platonic infatuation; he had 14 children, too. After
the Royal was burnt Coppin produced Robertson’s
“School,” and other plays, in St. George’s Hall, with
Carey and Shepparde, supported by Ireland and
Appleton. Miss Carey was admirable as the original
Galatea in Australia, and' made a hit as Mercy
Merrick, in “The New Magdalen,” supported by Dam-
piei as Julian Gray. She must have almost reached
the age of Ellen Terry.
“Jaques” : If real thrill is the thing at the
picture-show there is one theatre in Sydney
.that has quite satisfied me on two occa-
sions. Over 2000 people were startled the
other night when a boy squealed excitedly,
“Look at the rat! Look at the rat!” and
pointed roofwards. There, sure enough, was
a big rodent doing Blondin on a girder. The
screams of the women and girls combined
with the exclamations of the kids must have
scared the animal, as it missed its footing
and seemed to hang on with one hand. How-
ever, it clawed back and performed the
journey from one end of the theatre to the
other and disappeared. On two occasions
that has happened in the same theatre and
next time I’m betting the rat falls. Then
there will be a circus.
London theatres are almost entirely given
over to revues, frivolities and musical
comedies. Hardly a - single high-class,
“elevating,” superior play is on anywhere.
A Berlin paper prints in parallel columns
the programmes of the principal shows in
the two capitals. A striking contrast be-
yond a doubt. The Berlin theatres are
nearly all staging Shakspeare, Schiller,
■ Hauptmann and other heavy-weight drama-
tists. The German argument seems to be
that people like the Londoners who run
their theatres this way in war-time ought
to be annihilated by Ku.ltur.
“B. J.”: The other day a husband who
had long been a-missing was hauled before
a Melbourne suburban court and ordered to
pay 10 shillings a week towards the support
of his wife. The lady had despaired of find-
ing him until one day a friend rushed in
with the glad news that She had visited a
picture show where a military funeral was
being screened, and in the driver of one of
the mourning coaches had recognised the
missing matrimonial link.
“J.F.H.”: Passed out, London’s veteran
low comedian Arthur Williams, who strongly
reminded old Australians of George Coppin,
whom he strikingly resembled in face and
broad humor. His greatest success was
Lurcher in “Dorothy.” When the part was
first handed to him it contained only 30
lines, but he expanded it night by night
until it gradually became the outstanding
character of the piece. Fred Robson, the
reigning low comedian of 60 years ago, whose
style and mannerisms were closely copied by
George Coppin, first gave Williams a helping
hand and started him on a successful career
in the same line.

—Rosina Buckman ; Blarney, the stout , tenor
with the tired voice who supported Bel Sorel

D’Oisley and W. J. Samuel.
“Jwosp”: “Harrison 0'.,” the smiter of
American playwrights, should rejoice to
hear of the death of Paul Armstrong, the
part-author of “Via Wireless” and the
adapter of “Alias Jimmy Valentine,” which
Williamson Ltd. produced in Australia.
Armstrong was really a brilliant collabo-
rator rather than a dramatist. Heart disease
took him off: the stage after his main works
had been beating for 46 years. “Jimmy
Valentine” was his biggest success, and O.
Henry supplied the best part of it.
Frank Nankivell, the Australian artist
who has been a New Yorker for many years,
has made good in a new way by drawing-
reels for the ;These are not draw-
ings on the screen where the hand is shown,
but a complete story in drawings.' His two
latest are a humorous adaptation from iEsop
and a modernised version of Mother Goose.
Both are likely to be seen in Australia soon.

“Stargazer”: The reappearance of “Paul
Jones” recalls Fanny Lyddiard, Snazelle,
Leach and Johnnie Gourlay to ageing play-
goers. The most spirited performance, to
my thinking, was that of Gourlay, a sprightly
little American and a remarkable'dancer.
Leslie Holland is credited with being a light
and nimble Trocadero; but compared with
Gourlay he is as a traction-engine to a
feather in the wind. My recollection is of
an Opera House (Melb.) season which must
have been super-excellent, since so many of
the figures still hold out vividly in the public
mind. Gourlay survives in America, but
the other stars have ceased to twinkle.
“Row M.”: Maud Fane, the bright sing-
ing girl of Warwick’s Burlesquers, now
doing the Tivoli circuit, steps from polite
vaudeville into a principal part in the
J.C.W. panto., and thereby blazes a new
professional cross-trail—Or I think so. Maud
has an unusually powerful voice of good
quality, good looks, and —best of all—a sense
of humor. She suffers from musical-comedy
ambitions, and, now that she is with the
Firm, will probably get a chance to indulge
them. I rate her with the three most natural
comediennes I remember of recent years—
May Beatty (Stevenson’s Opera Co.), the
leader of the Sprightly Sisters (at Sydney
Tivoli about four years ago), and Grace
Ellsworth (of the unlucky “Mayor of
Tokio” co.). Miss Fane is the fourth in
about 15 years, which shows they don’t
come by every boat.
James T. Tanner, the well-known libret-
tist, died recently in England. Among other
things he perpetrated the book of “Our Miss
Gibbs,” which ran for 83 weeks in Sydney
during 1911, and the “Dancing Mistress,”
just staged for a couple of weeks at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.). Grinding out this sort
of stuff seems to be a paying job. Anyhow.
Tanner washed up for close on £12,000.
Major-General Godley backs Australia’s
war correspondent Bean, who has con-
sistently urged relatives and friends not to
believe' the stories of alleged atrocities
practised on soldiers at Gallipoli by the
Turks. Writing to Col. Chaffey, in Maori-
land, the General says:—
It is quite true that the Turk is a clean tighter,
and compares very favorably in this respect with
the German. I also have no hesitation in saying
that at this moment I believe he is a better and
braver tighter than the German. Anything mon
valiant than the way he has fought us lately it
would be impossible to conceive, and he is a really
formidable enemy. He has treated our wounded
and prisoners extremely well.
In the night of trouble that’s one comforting
item, anyhow.
There is evidently something stirring
down South, for the “Southern Lights”
were visible in Tasmania on Novem-
ber 2 for the second time this year. Their
former appearance—on June 17—-was much
more brilliant, but it is something of a record
for them to show up at all so late in the
season.
It is a beastly reflection on somebody or
something that with six Labor Governments
in power in Australia, there are more serious
strikes in progress at this moment than there
have been since the early ’nineties. And it
is a worse reflection still on somebody or
something that the strikes are the most
serious and the most numerous in the Ma
State, where the Government has been in
office longest and has the easiest working
majority. Either these Governments have
failed to supply labor with means to settle
industrial disputes by reason, or labor doesn’t
want reason to settle its disputes. Either
way you look at it things are pretty rotten
from the Labor standpoint.
POOR OLD ACTOR:
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O Gouty Lover, hobbling through your part,
For what lost "armchairs do you sigh in vain!
Upon the grave’s brink you must ply your art,
And sigh like air-brakes for a little gain.
Upon your shaveq cheeks should wave the beard
That masks Old Age and hides Time’s cruel scars;
Instead, your hairless chops are thickly smeared
With the queer colors'that are found on “stars."
Pity the old mime with the shining pate!
In foolish rags towards his poor grave he reels;
Wearied to death, he still must simulate
The youthful raptures he no longer feels;
With toothless gums he yammers of his love;
The limelight pours; the high gods shift their
feet;
And falling, falling from the gloom above
Drift the poor husks of that strange food they eat.
O Gouty Lover, while the peanuts rain,
For what lost armchairs do you sigh in vain!
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

“Warrumbucca”: From their tactics, it
seemed fairly certain that the Turks
were not launching any attack that
night, so most of our men slept the
heavy sleep of the trenches. Sentries
THE OPTIMIST.
“But we could never get married on your
salary."
"Of course not. But what a splendid excuse
it would he to ask the old pot for a
rise."
at various intervals peered through the
narrow, sand-bagged loopholes, ready to pick
off prowling snipers or give the alarm in
case of attack. From one of these sentry
posts came a sharp cry, followed hy the
thud of a falling body. Evidently a rifle
bullet had got a man through a loophole.
My sergeant gave the grim, familiar call in
a low tone: “Stretcher bearers!” The call
was picked up and passed along down the
communication trench like a low echo—-
“Stretcher hearers!” “Stretcher hearers!”
until it died away in the distance. With
the sergeant I walked along the fire trench
to see about having the casualty carried to
the communication trench where the hearers
would take him over. But the “casualty”
was sitting up now, painfully wiping sand
from his eye, A bullet had ripped the
sand-bagged end of the loophole, spirting
grit into the sentry’s eye and dazing him for
the moment. However, he pulled himself
together, assured me that he was all right,
and hopped hack to his post. Having in-
spected my section and returned to the
junction of the fire and communication
trenches, I waited there on hearing the
steady plod of two men up the long, steep,
tortuous communication trench. The new
arrivals were Joe, Bill and a stretcher. Joe
was leader and spokesman, and putting
down the stretcher demanded, in a sharp,
business-like voice, “Where’s the bloke V”
Other things had happened, and I momen-
tarily forgot the incident, so absently
queried, “What bloke?” That exploded Joe.
“Some rotten swine” had called for stretcher
bearers, “and me and Bill' shoved off.” I
should have reproved Joe for his language
(there was a lot more) but —well, we don’t
stand very much on ceremony here, anyhow.
Besides, I knew I was expected to share in
the just indignation of Joe and Bill that no
“bloke” had been and got hisself hurt, and
thus given Joe and Bill a good chance to
get sniped, too, as they took their case to
the dressing station.
* * * *
November 1 was the 91st anniversary of
the first trial by civil jury in Australasia.
It was a court of quarter sessions held in
Sydney. The first nisi prius case before an
Australian civil jury of 12 was heard on
February 12, 1825—the King v. Robert
Cooper, of the Brisbane distillery. Inci-
dentally, the defendant won.
“Jonz” : The Maori wowser is nursing his
eye. George Russell, Minister for Internal
Affairs, threw the bomb that did the damage.
He announces that be is going to run on be-
half of the State the biggest lottery that the
Loanlies ever gambled in. The prizes will
be pictures, some of them gifts, but most of
them purchased from local artists. When he
has them gathered together they are to be
sent through the country along with the
Government Collection and the valuable
Chevalier Collection in Auckland. The
whole proceeds go to the fund for Convales-
cent Camps for Soldiers which the National
Government is going to try to build and run
on subscribed money ; and if the frolic turns
out trumps, Russell may ask the State to go
in for it annually.

with the fire-line turnin’ an’ twistin’, stray bullets
is cornin’ from all points of th’ compass; an’ sich
places at times is worst of all. I know one bloke

th’ other bloke into enlistin’,- what about hoppin’
off t’ th’ nearest recruitin’ place and gettin’ a gun
yourself? We wapt to get this job done .so’s we
can hurry back and; have -a .long beer - and a sand-

“Mulga Scrub” from the war zone: — t
The Australians have a grouch against the.eternal
saluting of officers. As you might expect, those who
look for it most deserve it least, and some of the
Cold Country officahs squeal terribly if their petty
dignity is ’ bumped by non-recognition of stars or
crown. To a. decent-minded fellow this continuous
semaphoring is just plain rot. On the other hand,
the old gibe that the Australian resents discipline
has been sent kite-high by recent happenings. At
Lonesome Pine, for instance, the order was given
that the attack was to be carried out with bayc-et
only. “Don't shoot,” the order went, “if you can
possibly avoid it.’’ And see how the Australian
interpreted that. For him it simply meant that he
must not shoot at all—must die, rather. For days
afterwards our dead were found with the business end
of their bayonets showing signs of usage, but in every
case the 150 rounds of ammunition were intact.
There was no grouch about doing that. But for
the merely ceremonial things our fellows haven’t
either time or patience.
A poor but dishonest young man had a
lamentable piece of bad luck in St. Kilda
(Vic.) the other night, lie had entered a
washhouse on the nod and was endeavoring
to remove a lawn-mower with as little noise
as might be, when suddenly he found him-
self involved in an intricate kind of thief-
talcer. The contrivance simply jumped at
him out of the dark and wound itself all
about him. The more he struggled the more
hopelessly he was involved in the t<sils. The
misfortunate fellow’s light for life brought
the owner of the lawn-mower, in pyjamas,
and then the truth was revealed. A coil of
wire clothes-line that had been hung on a
hook had been dislodged, and had fallen
over the marauder. No housefather who has
had dealings with a coil of wire clothesline
needs further explanation-.

When Edward Ross Fairfax, “gentleman,
of Macquarie-stfeet, Sydney, and Tunbridge
Wells, England,” died at London on July
15 last, he bequeathed property in Aus-
tralia to his immediate relatives worth
£569,062. Although probate was granted
at Sydney on November 4 the daily papers
have made no mention of the fact. It was
unjust to thus overlook the benefactions of
the deceased. According to the last will
and testament of- the relative of the S. INI.
Herald he did not feel called upon to make
any bequests to charitable institutions,_ as
he had contributed largely to such organisa-
tions during his lifetime, and in proof he
modestly quoted this list: —
Prince Alfred Hospital .. .-.£500
Sydney News Boys . . . . ..... 400
y.M.C.A -..200
N.S.W. Deaf, Dumb and Blind Inst. .. 100
Sydney Female Refuge . . . . . . 100
Industrial Blind Inst. . . .. • ■ 100
Destitute Children’s Socy. .. .. .. 100
Sydney Ragged Schools .. .. .. 100.
Sydney Newsboys’ Brigade .. .. 100
Camden College . . . . . . • • 50
Bible Socy. (N. S. Wales branch) .. 50
N.S.W. Bush Mission 50
This made a total of £lB5O. That the
S. M. Herald didn’t mention such benefac-
tions out of an estate of a mere £569,062
suggests quite unnecessary modesty on its
part. As for the Telegraph, maybe it was
furious jealousy of the other family that
kept it from saying the great things a Fair-
fax had done.
IN A MAN’S WOULD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A hundred merry years ago ere Bonaparte was broke
And gallant ships sailed England’s seas that knew
not oil or smoke,
Good people stayed where they were bred and looked
askance at “those
Damned furriners” from distant France, where
swarmed old England’s foes.
The dark-skinned stranger at the Hall was served in
sullen fear,
And yokels asked, in wood and field, “What be he
doin’ ’ere?”
The merchant for adventure armed chose well his
sturdy crew
And no man journeyed far who had no business to do.
A hundred merry years ago no liner cleft the seas
W ith long decks strewn with idle squaws with books
upon their knees,
And smirking maids to do their hair and draw
their corsets tight
When they stripped off to show fat necks around the
food at night;
No sweating stokers shovelled coal, no stewards ran
for "tips
To do the foolish bidding of some she-ass with wide
hips;
It was a man’s world then, my lads, above decks and
below—■
I often wished I could have lived one hundred years
ago !
N.S.W.

High time it is for Wurms to turn.


THE VALUE OF MAGNESIA TO
DYSPEPTICS.
ITS ACTION EXPLAINED.
“Of inestimable value in all forms of
stomach trouble.” recently remarked an
eminent specialist when asked his opinion
upon the ever-increasing use of magnesia in
cases of indigestion, dyspepsia, heartburn,
flatulence, wind, etc.., but, of course, it must
be pure bisurated magnesia ; this is the form
invariably prescribed by physicians who
recognise that in many cases serious harm
might result from the use of other forms,
such as the acetates, citrates or sulphates
of magnesia. Most chemists no\y stock pure
bisurated magnesia in powder and tablet
form, and I would advise sufferers from any
form of stomach trouble to take half a tea-
spoonful of the powder or two compressed
tablets in a little water immediately after
each meal. This will instantly neutralise the
excessive acid in the stomach and prevent
fermentation of the food, thus ensuring nor-
mal digestion and freedom from discomfort
and pain.*

Browning misquoted to fit the times .
God’s out of His Heaven ;
All Hell's with the world.
The department over which Spence, M.P..
lately presided can achieve a feat when it
really gives its mind to it. On May 12 last
a Melbourne business-man sent a plainh -
addressed letter to “Col. E. Tivey, Militai ,\
Camp. Broadmeadows,” and awaited an
answer in patience. He got it when the
missive was returned to him through the
Dead Letter Office on October 2,). I ■ost-
marks show that it was duly posted on May
12, and reached Seymour on the 18th.
Thence it went on a sort of grand tour,
touching at Cairo on the loth Zeitoun
on the 28th, Cairo again on the loth Aug-
ust, and other undecipherable places there-
after. Nobody interfered with it; it simply
mooched around the world, casually and
leisurely. Finally someone considered it
had mooched enough, and returned it to
Melbourne, whence the genius who directs
these tremendous matters returned it to the
writer instead of sending it to Broad-
meadows, where Tivey had been all the
while.

The old gentleman had asked for some
stories and the returned soldier was obliging
him. “There was a sniper who was doing
a letter damage and I goes after ’im. Just
as I sneaks over the rise he lets go and
smashes me bay’net. Running down the
gully was about 20 Turks, and I
goes after them and drops a
couple before they gets clear. Then
I comes back and lumme if I didn’t
walk fair on top of me sniper.
Bet yer life I put the bay’net into
him ouick and lively.” “But I
thought you said your bayonet had
been shot away,” said the audi-
ence. Billjim lit a cigarette.
“ ’Ere,” he snapped, “you asked me for
stories, but what you want’s a blanky argu-
ment !”
“T. 0.1.”: There are two churches in
Windsor (N.S.W.) named after St. Mat-
thew, one Anglican, the other R.C. The
former has just celebrated its 98th birth-
day, and is the oldest church in Australia.
The foundation-stone was laid by Governor
Macquarie on October 11, 1817. That stone,
by the way, was removed during the night,
and the holey dollar, which was below, was
stolen. The stone was relaid, hut when the
second coin was likewise stolen the parson
got tired of placing valuables underneath.
In those days there were no collections —
the Imperial Government did the needful
in cash matters. George IV., of very pious
memory, presented the bell which hangs in
St. Matthew’s tower. The pipe organ was
the first landed in Australia, and is in use
still.

“H.M.W.,” from a warm spot:—
Recruiting results and the Gallipoli performances
of the Bananalanders ought to do a bit to knock out
the mischievous yarns about our hot climates breed-
ing only degenerates. Ask any experienced camp
commandant, and he will tell you that the men of
the most magnificent physique are coming in,
especially to the Light Horse, from the Outback
plains. Hot climates, I am more than ever con-
vinced, breed as big and robust a type as do the
lukewarm and colder brands of weather; and the
shibboleths about certain climates not being fit for
white men must soon be thrown into limbo. It can
be hot enough here, where we are not far from the
big western plains, and our last physical degenerate
who enlisted, aged 20 years and four monhts, stood
6ft. 3in. in his socks, went lOin. round the chest, and
weighed 2001 b. And we have more of him.
THE ECONOMIST.
“This dreadful war! They say me must all
suffer.”
“Yes, indeed; poor Fido’s chicken sand-
wiches are now made of rabbit.”
Norman K. Ewing’s appointment to the
Tasmanian Bench and the re-shuffling of the
Federal Cabinet cards in the same week will
remind old Westralian goldfields men of the
meeting of Ewing and Hugh Mahon (now
Minister for External Affairs) at Kalgoorlie,
in the consulship of Finnerty. Mahon, as
editor of the local paper, with a terrific
set on the quaint old Perth administra-
tion, used to launch hair-raising leaders at
the Forrest-Harper crowd who, up to then,
had never encountered anything stronger in
the way of criticism than G. F. Sala’s mild
comments in the Perth Daily News. Big-
john especially disliked Mahon’s slashing
style, and the first chance he got he went
for the pressman for criminal libel. So
-anxious was “Old Jack” to pot Mahon, whom
he dubbed “a d —d rebel,” that he dispatched
N. Iv. Ewing in place of the common or
garden Crown Prosecutor, because (1)
Ewing was then coming to the front as a
lawyer; and (2), like Forrest, he had a pri-
vate score to settle with Mahon’s paper. By
hook or by crook Mahon was to be “got.”
All sorts of rumors flew about the field ; it
was even said that the Crown tried to “pack”
the jury. But in those days to pack a jury
against a Goldfields’ champion was harder
to do than it would be to bribe Peter for a
free pass into Paradise. Mahon was acquitted
without the jury leaving for its usual drink
—a record in those days. Being himself a
Justice, the “d —d rebel” left the dock and
—stepped on to the Bench! The astounded
Ewing protested, whereupon he and his
friends were reminded that one Justice —-
under the then W.A. law —had power to
commit to gaol for “contempt,” and the hint
was passed round that if Ewing got really
savage Mahon, J.P., meant to order him
into custody. The idea of a Crown Prosecu-
tor being shot in appealed to all hands, and
if Ewing had not “tumbled” quickly, in he
would have gone! Ewing, very pale, for-
mally opened the “next case,” then handed
it and his papers over to a junior, and
cleared for Perth by the afternoon train.
* 45- * *
“Mujik”: Now this yarn about a Mel-
bourne Cup dream is absolutely true. A
month before the race, while sleeping off my
daily debauch of tea, I was visited by a
severe-loolcing person in clerical clothes, who
took me by the arm and led me to the win-
dow. “Look, brother,” he said, and I looked,
and there, suspended in the distant atmo-
sphere, was the figure of a girl clad in a
diaphanous garment, and as the tail thereof
fluttered in the breeze the ghostly wowser
slowly spelled out, in a deep, sepulchral
voice, the letters of a word printed on the
celestial nightie—“M-a-r-c-u-l-f-u-s.” Won-
deringly I turned to my visitor, who, putting
a large, bony hand to his mouth, whispered
hoarsely in mv ear, “The straight griffin.”
Then he pulled a couple of wings from his
tail pocket, pinned them on his shoulders and
flew off like a bird towards the airy maiden.
They were hiding behind a cloud when I
woke. Next night my rest was disturbed by
a horrible nightmare, in which, carrying
George Reid on my back, I made the ascent
of Kosciusko in inky darkness. “Mountain
Knight,” I panted, when bathed in sweat I
dropped George at the top for supper, and
woke to find it was day. “Must be going to
have a dead-heat,” solemnly said Puntersby
when I told him next morning, and he rushed
off to ring up Ikey. But just then Pickem
happened along and remarked that his wife
had received a letter from her cousin in
Westralia announcing that her husband’s
niece, who lives in Queensland, had a friend
who said he had dreamed with great distinct-
ness seeing St. Carwyne win by half a mile.
Pickem said he was going to have his bit
on that. Now when the race was run these
three calamitous prads fell down on the way,
and everyone whom I had told about the
dreams spoke in terms of advanced fright-
fulness. Me? Oh, no. I didn’t lose any-
thing ! I didn’t back ’em. I thought I’d
wait and see how things panned out; and,
anyhow, I tumbled to it that my shadowy
rev. visitor was out hitting up biz for his
mates, the bookmakers.

Soldiers are still returning who vehem-
ently declare that the strictures of Dr.
Springthorpe on the management of the
Australian Red Cross in Egypt were abso-
lutely correct and more than
wholly justified. The Bul-
letin was assured this week
(and by no irresponsible pri-
vate) that one battalion had
practically no comforts for
four months, and he asserted
as a solemn fact that supplies
despatched from Australia on
some ships remained unloaded
and were returned in the same holds.

SLOW BUT SURE.
JONES: “And he went out at twenty to one.”
OFFICER PUNTER (who hacked it) : “M’yes; and got hack at a quarter to two.”
“Tuppence Extra”: Whenever I come
across one of these exultant Yankee shrieks
about the financial supremacy of the world
passing automatically to the U.S.A., 1
wonder how much money without power
will be worth in the confused years that
will follow the war. The U.S.A. had the
luck to produce the man who wrote “The
Influence of Sea Power Upon History.”
9at it needs now is another genius to
write “The Influence of Sea and Land
Power Upon Finance.” The dollar is a
noble animal, but it can’t take care of
itself.
* * * *
“Wingen” Abbott, the aged New England
(jN.b.vV.) squatter and inveterate newspaper
correspondent, caught a tartar when
he wrote a fevered criticism of the Necessary
Commodities Commission to S. M. Herald
on October 5. “The Crown” fired a
Supreme Court writ at him inviting him to
snow cause why lie should not be proceeded
against criminally for libel. The case was
heard before the Full Court, and was in its
way a judicial farce. There were three
Judges, two Iv.C's and live lesser legal
lights present to look as solemn as if there
really was an intention to prosecute Abbott.
He handed in a humble apology, which was
gravely intoned, and this was followed by
the chanting of the pained affidavits of the
libelled commissioners. Then the inquisition
unanimously agreed to accept the apology
aforesaid on condition that Abbott paid for
everybody’s beer.

The war optimist’s creed :
I am an Optimist.
I shall remain an Optimist.
I clou t want anyone moaning in my car.
I don’t want jumbles of figures proving nothing.
I believe that our volunteers are better than tlicir
long-service conscripts.
1 believe that our improvised strategy will prove
better than their long-matured plans.
I believe our diplomacy will achieve more than
their bribes.
I won’t listen to long stories about our muddling.
I won’t read long stories about our criminal care-
lessness.
I will give no encouragement to the Jeremiah of
the tram or train.
I refuse to believe that all the Red Cross com-
forts and stores go astray.
1 want to hear bands and see banners.
I believe in the Fleet, the Army, the Silver Bullet,
and all the rest of it.
I believe, most of all, the speeches and statements
our politicians make.
1 believe and hope for the sheer comfort of
believing and hoping.
1 believe that victory is incubated by patriotic
fervor.
1 don't want to be reasoned with, because reason
is only dry dust.
I admit I have no great reason for elation,
but
I am not troubled.
I am Characteristic of the Race.
I am British—-
And none the worse for being so !
GOD SAVE THE KING!
And my liver !

COLONEL EDWIN THEY. D.S.O.
Kenneth M. 11. Solomon, son of the Syd-
ney City Treasurer, who, as lieutenant in
the lltli Gloucester®, died from wounds at
Gallipoli in September, was one of the most
promising of Australian poets. “Verses by
K.M.H.S.” showed the author to be a poet
with ideas, chaotic but original, and a tine
facility in expression. Ilis death, at the
age of 27. closes the promise of a brilliant
career. He was captain of Sydney Gram-
mar School, gained liis degree at Sydney
University, and was just completing his
course at the Temple when the war called
him.
Once when interpreting for Wi Peri in
M.L. Representatives David Francis (Ren-
ville Barclay, .iust dead, refused to translate
the last sentence of the speech. Wi. who
was violently protesting against the iniqui-
ties of a native land Bill, banged him on
the head with a volume of statutes and
roared at the Speaker in his best English.
"I say, ‘Go to Ilell, te pakeha” This
was in the old days. With the arrival of
Maori representatives like Apirnna Xgata
(barrister). Poinare, Hone lleke and Te
Rangihiroa the interpreter’s job became of
much less consequence. Stili Barclay, like
Jimmy Carroll and Bellamy’s and the' mace,
was an institution, and his death causes
widespread regret.

Professor Laby. the Australian science
expert, lias done a line service to the army
of his own country by joining with Pro-
fessor Masson to give it a respirator which
compares more than favorably with any
hitherto used. Laby, who is the latest and
longest addition to the Melbourne Univer-
sity teaching staff, has started on his new
job under the most favorable auspices, not-
withstanding that he has found himself the
subject of a lecture-hall couplet:—-
To lieai' me,' draw near me, keep still and rejoice,
I’m Laby ; like Gaby—a star without voice.
Ex-ITemier John Verran. miner and
“local preacher,” continues to hand out. to
S. Aus. Parliament his personal renderings
of Holy Writ. The other day he explained
in his own way how and why an Opposi-
tion member who had interrupted him was
no better than the ordinary crowd. “God
did not make you any different from other

It is ipiite possible that Vie. Attorney-
General Mackinnon may be beaten at the
next elections by a mere law-clerk. At
Labor’s recent pre-election ballot at Prali-
mn, Mackinnon’s constituency, there were
seven candidates, the successful one being a
young man named Parker, cm l ‘l■,vod in the
office of solicitor Joseph Woolf. Of the
seven Laborites in the ballot, two had
opposed Mackinnon at previous elections,
and the Liberal majority had shrunk each
time. Since the South Yarra and Prahran
seats were amalgamated, and Mackinnon
entered the Assembly on a minority
poll leading by a few votes, Labor
has put up a different candidate at
each contest. Parker being the seventh.
The first of these was Edward Pus-
sell. now senator and Assistant Minis-
ter ; and the second was Martin Hannah,
now M.L.A. for Oollingwood. The third.
McCarthy, was killed while playing football
two or three years ago. On paper, the
Parker-Mackinnon engagement: looks like
being a close thing.
Benjamin Lee, who represented West
Maitland in the X.S.W. Assembly 51 years
ago, and was afterwards police magistrate
at Bathurst and stipendiary at Sydney for
some years, celebrated his OOth birthday
iast week. Ilis father, also Benjamin, was
a private in a 1 )ragoon regiment, and was
all through the Peninsular War, and missed
Waterloo through being engaged in the
battle of New Orleans in America. He came
to N. S. Wales in 1829, settled at Parra-
matta in 1830, and died there in 1579, aged
91 years.
The latest of Adelaide’s vigorous patriarchs
to pass the SO-mark is Simpson Newland.
pioneer and pastoralist. Mis father, a farm-
ing preacher, settled in 1839 at Encounter
Bay. just outside the Murray mouth, and the
veteran of to-day has put many memories
of early times into a valuable though
rather stodgy novel, "Paving the Wav.” He
has been State Treasurer, and is now an
authority on all River Murray questions:
also he claims that S. Aus. would have had
its direct line through to Darwin long ago
if the Price-Peake Government bad not
frowned on bis plans for getting it done by
a land-grant syndicate. Among the Newland
sons are a leading Adelaide surgeon, an
Australian XI wicketkeeper, and a Rho-
desian settler, who had the chief hand in
arranging Roosevelt's big game tour.
AN • D. Murray, a unionist who was
rapidly making his way to the top of the
X.S.W. Labor tree, has had his climbing cut
short by a railway accident at Auburn'while
on his way home from a Sydney Labor Coun-
cil meeting. lie came from Belfast on
account of victimisation in that place, and
was able to supply Sydney unionists with
the Larkin viewpoint of the Larkin strikes ;
and ever since his prominence had been grow-
ing on council and conference, his straiglH-
out speech and firm adhesion to definite
Labor doctrines giving him the support of
the stalwarts. One thing he was very definite
about —his hostility to the introduction of
religious matters to political and industrial
affairs. He had seen the workers beaten
time after time by the good old business of
stirring religious differences in Ireland; and
he was a wholesome reminder to a party that
has been inclined lately to forget the old
principle.
"•T.R.S.”: The most numerous Australian
family, is itV (I>. 28/10/’l5). Well, it’s ai
Mount View, near (Vssnock (X.S.W.). The
father is Richard King, an Oxfordshire man.
who arrived here with his parents in 1840.
at the age of eight. In 1857 he married a
Somersetshire lady, by whom he has had
ill children. The mother died recently, but
the father and the rest of tin 1 family still
reside in the district. In 1911 a paper
offered a prize to the largest Australasian
family, and quoted an English journal’s
claim that the family of George Perkins, of
Hull —28 children —was the largest in the
Empire. After exhaustive research the
prize was awarded to Richard King and his
wife. Dick’s brother Bill, of the same
locality, proved himself almost as good a
recruiting sergeant.

Dead, as a result of an accident, Glenville
Barclay, of Wellington (M.L.). lie was
the eldest son of Captain W. de It. Barclay,
and for many years was Maori interpreter
lo the House of Reps. Probably there was
no one in the Land o’ Quakes who knew
more of Ilenare, his language, ancient lore
and traditions than “Pakare.” There are
many good translators—Alf Grace, for in-
stance —but very few of them can talk
Maori well. Glenville Barclay could do it
more than well: in the use of Ihe musical
language there are few Maoris who could
ha ve' equalled him.
Melbourne’s Art Gallery Trustees (lie
other day received a letter from an Austra-
lian sculptor suggesting that he should do a
bust of Henry Lawson In copper, bronze or
marble for the gallery. One of the trustees
promptly observed that he did not think “the
writer mentioned” had acquired such fame
that he was entitled to have his portrait in
the gallery. This was received with mur-
murs of approval, and the suggestion was
accordingly cast into outer darkness. Hav-
ing thus asserted the exclusiveness of their
Temple of Fame, the gallery guardians pro-
ceeded with the next business —a proposal
that Henry Gyles Turner, who acquired
fame by being general manager of a bank
that made a most disastrous smash in the
'!lo's, ruining thousands of people, and who
now happens to be chairman of the
Gallery Trustees, should be preserved
|n oils and given a niche in the
Temple. This was unanimously agreed to.
and Posterity, not being present at the
meeting, could not utter one squeak of pro-
test. Nor for that matter, could the Past —
the Past of the Busted Boom.
The first member of a N.S.W. Legislature
to receive pay was probably Hanley Bennett,
of Tamworth. He was elected for Liver-
pool Plains in March, 1872, and sat in the
seventh, eighth and ninth Parliaments. IBs
first election came about curiously. The elec-
torate was made a convenience of to provide
a seat for “Slippery Charlie” Cowper in
December, 1869. Cowper held it for a
year, and then went to London as Agent-
General. Lewis Wolfe Levy, who had
big family interests in the district,
followed him. But the people were
getting tired of being made a convenience
of. and decided to select a local candidate,
they chose Hanley Bennett, a man in very
humble circumstances, and illiterate. He
consented to run on condition that those
who nominated him also maintained him.
Several business people agreed to do it. and
to the surprise of some of them. Bennett
was elected by a clear majority. The finan-
cial support' dropped off after a year or
so. and Bennett, who was re-elected in
1-575, got further and further in the
mire and was made bankrupt in May.
’76. But he came back smiling after
re-election, and sat until 1880, when he was
given a job as gatekeeper on the railway.
AN hen he was too old for that he found his
way into a public institution and died there.
“THE NAUGHTY BOYS.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A famous African battalion, known as “The
Naughty Boys, ' which is composed of ex-prisoners,
led the assault. As usual, when a desperate enter-
prise is afoot, they sprang forward a few seconds
after the artillery lire ceased. Despite dreadful losses
the line never faltered. When once at grips with the
foe they soon finished off the Germans. The battalion
will be mentioned in Orders of the Day.— Cable.
I hoy marshalled from the prison yard, from convict-
gang and cell ;
they mustered in from hiding-holes, from many a
noisome hell
Y\ here shame can shelter its disgrace or failure its
despair.
\\ hen danger calls for daring hearts the naughty
boys are there!
It may be some are prodigals whom careless ways
condemn :
i-'onie rebels in a bitter world that made a war on
them;
And some the victims of mischance; the paths of
shame are thronged
With hundreds—nay, with thousands —who are far
less wrong than wronged.
Not only in the fields of France, or in its army’s
ranks,
Ihey muster to their country’s aid who do not owe
it thanks ;
'I he outlaws of its rigid laws, or codes of conduct
nice.
Prepared to seek redemption in a last, great sacri-
fice !
High spirits dial once .made their sins may shine
in gallant deeds -
'ill, tearsome is the reckless charge the social out-
law leads !
W bile others hesitate to leave their case or treasured
toys,
(he country will not call in vain that calls its
“naughty” boys.
( h, many a weary mother-heart ’twixt these twin
sorrows tossed —•
The grief for sinful waywardness, the sorrow for the
lost —-
Will learn, before the war i> dune, how in the field
of fame,
Ho naughty boy lias cleared the smudge from his
dishonored name.

This bank didn’t bit hard enough when it
reduced its capital ; and afterwards it appar-
ently hadn’t the courage to hit again. So,
though there are nominally reserves of some
size, which ought to mean that all the capital
is intact and a good deal besides, the public
has always been able to buy the shares* at
several points under par. Had the capital
been cut down harder, a 7 p.c. div. would
have cost no more than one of 41 or 5 p.c.
does now; and there probably wouldn’t have
been such pressure put on the management
to raise the dividend another T p.c. Fur-
ther, it wouldn't have been necessary to stuff
profit away in order to replace capital lost
but not written off. However, it is not much
use talking now, though it is a fair tiling
to remember that the present reduction of
dividend is really in a measure due to the
former neglect to do the writing-down
thoroughly. Of course it wouldn’t have
saved the hank from showing diminished
profit. Had there been no war. Queens-
land’s exceedingly serious drought would
have cut into earnings. The remarkable
tiling is that it hasn’t cut more than a
couple of thousand pounds out of them.
And on top of drought has been the inter-
ference of the war with the meat-export
trade. It was unquestionably the proper
thing that the Governments of the Allies
should have first call on our meat surplus.
That, however, shouldn’t have meant any
serious interference with exchange business.
But as things were arranged, the Govern-
ment played into the hands of one or two
banks—and the Royal wasn’t one of them.
Ministers had, in short, to deal with matters
<>l which not one of them had any personal
knowledge, and they had their levs pulled,
just as Hughes has had his pulled over the
metals business.
But if the profits are not so good, the
balance-sheet figures show the bank to be
stronger:—

Liabilities thus total £1,698,77!) and liquid
assets are £868,927. There are thus £829,852
of deposits, etc., not represented by liquid
assets : and against this lot there is no less
than £1,393,325 in advances (book value)
and £80,475 in premises. The margin is so
wide that it should give the depositor a very
comfortable feeling. Ou the other hand, it
isn’t calculated to give the shareholder much
profit. A bank with less than £3 of the
public’s money for every £1 of its own, and
more than half of that held liquid, can't
expect: to make its shareholders very fat.

The half-year just ended took in the winter
months; and that partly explains the
shrinkage in the profit from the figures of
the previous term. But there is no visible
reason why the profit should be loss than
one-half of what it was. As a matter of
fact the £172,773 of the previous half-year
looks a good deal like a freak.
Wore the directors' pursuing a cunning
policy, it would he a most inopportune time
I > show a bloated return at the rate of over
•“3 p.c. p.a. on the actual capital, as was
done six months ago. It isn’t so easy to say
what a management’s policy ought to he in
a case, of this kind. And (what is of more
practical importance, perhaps) it isn’t so easy
to say what it is. For instance, there are
serious contingencies in this business. The
trade was never faced with so man? dan-
gers. Even if the worst of them are over-
come. the victory will cost a lot of money—
and for a good deal of it there won’t be any
receipts given. If. as seems highly nr.j-
bable, pubs are closed at six or seven or eight
o’clock, irrespective of their character, the
rent and capital value of hotel properties
will be cut very hard. Is the co. nroviding
for this? Is it stuffing a few tens of thou-
sands of profit up its sleeve, so that it may
presently write down the assets without
drawing too heavily on the profits of the
future? Or will the future have to carry
all the trouble, supposing trouble comes?
Until you know these things you don’t know
very much about this co. The published
accounts look all right; but they really leave
a lot unsaid, and in the nature of things
the management is not at all likely to sup-
ply the omissions. There is this to be noted,
certainly. Three or four years ago £350,000
of contingency reserves were brought to
light and capitalised, and the existence of
these hidden reserves shows that the policy
had been to provide for contingencies out of
undisclosed profits.
It has been a wonderfully lucrative busi-
ness. Look at this :

►Still, as suggested just now, this isn’t a case
of the past governing the future. The future
is going to govern itself. And the leader
oi: the opposition is no longer a dear old lady
of uncertain sex who demanded impossible
things and was encouraged to keep on
demanding them and thus make quite sure
of getting nothing.

It is when you come to sit down quietly with
an old-established Australian co. like this
that you begin to realise, if you have never
done it before, bow splendidly the Australian
spirit has grown during the past 12 or 15
years. The United is 53 years old; and if
you look at the table you will see that, in
spite of a transfer of £20,000 to capital, the
loserves are now a good deal more than twice
what they were in 1902. Or this shows it
even better :—•

By 1900, after 38 years of life, the co. had
reached a premium income of £58.499. In
the subsequent 15 years it lias added £129,788
to those figures. So in the past 15 years it
bas_ got 21 times the new business it had
achieved in all the previous 38 years. And
tins hasn’t been due to any thorough reor-
ganisation. 011 the contrary, things with
the United have gone along in much the old
leisurely way. General-manager Tinley,
who died during the year, had been with
the co. for 52 years. Bartin Haigb, who
steps up one, was- secretary 20 years ago
and how many years before that this paper
doesn’t know. And George Judah Cohen and
1. 1. Knox and Richard Binnie, directors to-
duv. were directors in 1895. Legislation
lias no doubt done something for this and
other cos.; employers’ liability and accident
insurance have become very general. But
more important than all that in bringing
about these remarkable changes in the
figures is the growing confidence in Austra-
lian institutions; a growing belief that you
can t make Australia rich by making divi-
dends for someone to spend elsewhere. And
this war is going to push that truth still
further home.
incidentally, the growing disposition on
the part ot. Australians to advance Austra-
lia by dealing with Australian institutions
is bound to be exploited bv adventurers
This makes it the urgent duty of the Aus-
tralian Parliament to legislate so that wild-
cats may be kept out. Things are being
done at this moment iu the insurance field
which amount to a scandal. Any adven-
turer can put up a brass plate in Sydney
and call himself and his equally impecunious
friends the Perennial Insurance Co., and
make vague references to a million capital
and on 110 real capital whatever take insur-
ance risks running into hundreds of thou-
sands of pounds. People actually are doin<*
this thing in N. S. Wales at this moment,
mostly, of course, in country districts, after
caging a few well-known men to act as
decoys by offering them a handful of free
grains. With luck these people will pull
through, and may. in fact, make a lot of
money. Without luck, they won’t of course
lose anything, because they have nothing to
lose, but hundreds of cookies and others who
thought they were insured will find their
policies a delusion and a snare.
. first thing to look out for in con-
sidering a proposal to insure is safety Will
you get your money supposing you have to
make a claim? .Now. the security which an
insurance co. offers depends primarily upon
the proportion of its funds to its risks; and
, e i' lsks you can judge, in a general way,
by the premium income. Therefore con-
sider first what proportion capital and re-
serves combined bear to premium income.
CaSe ,°l, the United paid capital is
±120,000 and the reserves £231,780 after
current div. is paid; total £351,786 Pre-
miums were £188,257. So funds were 187
p.c. ot the year’s premium income. Anv-
thnig over 150 p.c. is good; anything over
uo p.c. very good. Anything less than 100
p.c. is bad. If a co. can’t show that it has
actually in hand an amount equal to the
previous vear’s premium income, it ou°lit to
be turned down. Actually in hand—it is well
t<- note that. The amount mustn’t consist
pothetical assets, but cash deposits,
public securities, mortgages and properties.
I'lie safer the co. the more plainly will it
sot out its assets: this one, for instance,
shows deposits £111,005, various Govern-
ment stocks £87,642, mortgages £36.060.
freeholds £<2,244. and so on.

4he profit is shown net "after providing for
depreciation, etc.”; but the amount of that
provision isn’t mentioned. Really therefore
it isn’t any use taking much notice of a
single line in the foregoing table: the only
safe thing to do is to take a general sulvey.
The most the figures do is to indicate the
trend of things. For the past two terms
it looks as though the directors want to
indicate that things have been distinctly
better. They certainly could afford to be.
Before that, apparently, the 7 p.c. dividend
had for several years been barely earned. A
10 years’ comparison makes things look even
less satisfactory. As at September 30 1905,
the co. owed only £2840 and had £43,950 in
cash and public securities. Now it owes
£4073, and practically the whole of the nest-
egg of cash has been put into ships, which
ought, of course, to be earning a lot more
than the money was bringing in when it
was on fixed deposit. Certainly profits are
better than they were, on the whole, 10 years
ago; but the difference isn’t -striking. This
clearly isn’t one of those fortunate ship-
ping cos. which can gradually builu up a
fleet out of undivided and mostly-undisclosed
profit. Possibly it may be doing a trifle
better than the table indicates; but if it had
been doing very much better, it wouldn’t
have had to use up all its liquid assets to
pay for the additions which have been made
to the fleet. As far as one can see. there
is bread-and-butter ahead; but The Bulle-
tin can’t find any reason to expect cases
of champagne to wash the bread-and-butter
down.

The directors put it in the director’s usual
involved way :■ —■
This balance-sheet is by far the most satisfactory
submitted since manufacturing commenced in 1886.
The larger surplus shown has been derived by reason
of the greater demand for the qo.’s goods, brought
about to a considerable extent by the abnormal
restrictions in the way of importers obtaining their
usual deliveries, and not from any inflation of prices.
The shareholders get 5 p.c. extra on their
capital, the employees get a 5 p.c. bonus on
their wages, and (according to this report)
the public pays no more for its goods, if
the directors can prove this point as to
prices, they will have got hold of one of
the strongest practical arguments in favor
of high Protection ever available in these
parts. For if “restrictions in the way of
importers obtaining their usual deliveries”
can add 5 p.c. to the return of capital and
5 p.c. to the return of labor, without adding
anything to the cost to the consumer, plainly
this is the thing the country is looking for:
and those restrictions can be imposed just
as well by a high tariff as by a German
submarine. The thing is quite feasible.
Apart from labor unrest the great trouble
of the average Australasian factory has been
the irregularity of the demand anil the high
cost of marketing and distribution. This
latter drawback has been even more pro-
nounced in Maoriland than in Australia. If
the Australasian market were secured to
the Australasian product, the manufacturer
could afford to specialise, with advantage to
himself and his customers; and the specialist
would get the business iu his particular line
without chasing it at the present very heavy
cost. The consumer would benefit in regard
to price as well as quality, because competi-
tion would inevitably disturb a concern
which was showing 25 p.c., as this one did
last year. What we want to aim at is a
condition which will allow a manufacturing
co, to earn a steady 9or 10 p.c. or so. With
our present tariffs a co. lias to starve its
plant horribly in order to pay -1 or 5 p.c. one
year, and a few years later, there being no
internal competition to speak of. up rush
the profits to 30 p.c. when foreign com-
petition is temporarily shut off. That the
lean years of this co. have been much more
numerous than the fat years can be judged
by the circumstance that, after just a third
of a century, the reserves amount to less
than £43,000. though., the dividends have
often been mighty poor. The Bulletin
could name a dozen .concerns which either
cut up land and sell it or handle imported
goods which have done far better than that
in one-sixth of the time.

That table < <>v< is practically all the life of
the present co., which was built on the wreck
of a predecessor. At that time it was still
uncertain how the balance of the old pro-
perties on hand would pan out, so when the
assets were revalued the capital was fixed
at only £90,331, or £2(i.4SS less than the
total net valuation of the assets. That
surplus was accordingly available for
reserves. Instead, however, of this fund being
drawn upon to make good any losses on the
sale of assets at prices below the written-
down book value, surpluses were realised,
and the reserves were thus added to. For
some years the surpluses realised were
shown as profits on sales,, but this line has
of-late years disappeared from the accounts:
and, as it is simply incredible that the pro-
perties sold have realised exactly hook value,
either the profits have been lumped with
other earnings or an invisible or contin-
gency reserve has been established. As the
properties are now practically all sold, this
fund may be now brought into the light:
on the other hand, it may be kept up the
directors’ sleeve to provide for any losses
that may bo made on securities which may
in future fall into the co.’s hands. Hidden
reserves don’t, of course, sound well; but in
practice they turn out all right. Without
them depositors would certainly often he
quite unnecessarily ruffled, and tiie credit of
a thoroughly sound concern would be in
danger of being impaired.

Capital meantime has gone from £96,351 to
£128,000. The deposits are still quite mod-
erate. Deduct the cash from the deposits,
etc., and you get net liabilities of £71.449:
and the advances represent 31 times that
amount. Further, they are no "doubt all or
nearly all advances on building society
terms, which means that solid repayments
have been made since the period of excessive
values two or three or four years ago; so
that even if excessive advances were then
made the security should be quite good now.
As for profits., the 7 p.c. dividend is earned
and a fine margin besides. But how it is
done is a bit of a puzzle. It almost looks
as if some of that undisclosed profit on sales
previously referred to has been dropping into
the general profits of late years, though the
accounts don’t admit it.

The war teaches us that it is a wise plan to
encourage our own manufactures. At the same time
we want to buy that which is good and reliable.
The engineer cannot be bothered with belting that
is not reliable, no matter where it is made. But he
has not to look to the outside world for a good
belting, because he knows that “Pioneer” Australian-
made leather belting is equal to the world’s best.
“Pioneer” is obtainable from the only manufacturers,
J. 0. Ludowici & Son Ltd., 117 York Street, Sydney :
and Queensland Machinery Co., Brisbane; 11. Sum-
mers, Howard Street, Perth, W.A.; J. McKell,
Timpson Street, Port Adelaide.
ALUMINOFERBIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.*

Old Burra copper mine (S.A.) is up for
.ale and although this tamous copper
bonanza produced £3.000,000 worth of cop-
pe! A isn’t likely to realise a Inghpnce
The co. formed in 1901 did 110 good, the old
workings proving wet in the deepest levels
and rather barren. The Burra from 1840
to 1877 produced 234,648 tons of ore, much
of which assayed 20 p.c. copper. Costs
were heavy, especially m the very early
da vs when the crude ore Avas carted 100
miles to Port Adelaide. Still it paid hand-
somely even then. Mining men generally
consider the Burra to be a basin-like deposit
formed by intersecting lodes. Geological
reports are emphatic about lode channels,
•md hold out considerable encouragement to
a mining co. that is prepared to spend money
in sinking. # »
The C’ooronga Oil Lands Go.’s prospectus
reads like a chapter from “Monte Cristo.’
Pour pages are full of AA’hat Kockotellei,
D’Arcy and Tom Whaley have done, but
precious little about Avhat the Cooronga Co.
is going to do —except as to the distribution
of "the money it hopes to receive from the
public. Out of 125 shares for sale at £lO
the vendors are taking £750 cash as well as
getting £2OOO Avortli of scrip. That leaves
£5OO for what the prospectus naively
describes as “the expenses of flotation and
the usual brokerage.” The. Cooronga Co.
does not even mention precisely where its
areas are, though it hints in the fifth para-
graph that they are “within 10 miles of a
splendid port,” and it. seems proud that it
is in the vicinity of Petroleum Concessions,
Petrolia. North Petrolia, Austral Conces-
sions, Murrabinea Oil Concessions and
Kingston Petroleum Territories, all similar
companies holding prospecting search
licenses for oil. Why don’t all these small
syndicates amalgamate and thus raise
cash to put down a bore? If they Avon’t do
it people are bound to say that the pro-
moters have more faith in getting something
out of the public than out of the earth.

“G.D.M.”: The Australian iron industry
needs a scientific series of Customs duties, and
when we get that Ave’ll make our own fencing
wire, pig, bar, hoop, ingot, plate and sheet
iron, pipes, tubes, rails, fishplates ; also our
own guns and rifles. And to the Mount Lyell
ought rightfully to belong the duty of making-
iron and steel at Burnie (Tas.) from the
Blythe River deposit. The creation of
another great steel-making corporation will
need a big man and a big capital. Mount
Lyell Co. possesses both. Why should it;
not undertake the task? Blythe River may
not have the 21,000,000 tons of iron ore iu
sight at the Broken Hill Prop.’s Iron Knob
(S.A.), yet its compensation is that near by
are coal for coke and big beds of limestone.
Bowes Kelly ought to make this the crown
uf his life’s work. In no way could he do
more for Australia.
“Weston”: “A Bulletin man at Wes-
toiiia” (B. 14/10/T5) states that the

Deeps, by forcing the water out of the coun-
try rock and filling the cracks with cement,
have been successful elseAvliere. Will he

One of the largest unsecured creditors has
acquired Chaffers (W.A.) from the liqui-
dator. and proposes to first let it on tribute
and then try to form a neiv co. and give it
another go. Less than a year ago, when the
“wild dog” management Avas in full swing,
Vail (Lake View and Star) reported on the
property for his co. After au exhaustive
sampling he came to the conclusion that there
were 93,162 tons of ore in sight, worth 295.
per ton. Working exes, and cost of develop-
ment he reckoned would average 275. per ton,
and losses in treatment an additional 35.;
so that for every ton of ore mined and treated
he figured on a loss of Is. That
summed up the mine in a few words. He
admitted, however, that a considerable ex-
penditure on development might enable a
profit to be made on 295. ore, but there was
no certainty about it, nor was it at all sure
that the expenditure could be recovered.

Payne’s Find (W.A.) still perseveres.
The last half-year’s accounts show a profit
of £155 on a revenue of £2594. Liquid assets
are a mere £866, including £2OO stores.
Against this there is a debt of £6296. It is
true the co. has a calling power of £7223 and
7000 reserve shares which are given a book
value of £3500, but neither of these assets
would count for much if the co. got into diffi-
culties. Something is said in the manager’s
report about ore in the shafts assaying from
30dwt. to 30z., and that there “should be
1000 tons of 25dwt. stuff in one particular
block of ore,” but no definite estimate of
reserves is given. Chances are not good.

Black Range Co. (W.A.) is still turning
its Sandstone mine to profit. Last month’s
surplus was £lBBl. There is estimated to
be 1400 tons raised and 50,000 tons in sight
below the surface of its newly-acquired
Pinnacles show, and the erection of plant is
about to begin.
* If * *
Alec Peacock signs the Edna May Deeps
circular with regard to raising
but he isn’t the architect of the scheme. He
is too canny to want to jump up a register
from 60,000 to 100,000 shares, if only be-
cause of the extra office work it means.
Most Victorian no-liability concerns when
needing money simply increase the amount
callable on each share. The Deeps Board,
instead, goes for 40,000 new shares divisible
pro rata among present holders if they care
to apply. This looks like trying to hit up
the bears. If no calls can be made on pre-
sent shares the bears cannot use such calls
to straighten out their position. All the
same, they will recover cash over the rights,
and they know also that once the last 9d.
is called upon the current issue, 20,000
paids will come in as good delivery scrip,
making 60,000 shares available to square
deals instead of 40,000. The market apart,
there isn’t much to be said in favor of the
new issue. If ss. per share will cover all
outgo till the co. gets on to profits, the five-
bob new scrip will rank equally for divs.
Avith the old 10s. paid contributing shares.
The men who put up 10s. each on the orig-
inal float Avon’t like that, the more when
they see the new shares called first in the
market. If there should happen to be a
knock-out, the old holders will be on to the
loAvest plane before they get the right to
deal. Manager Stuckey estimates £7500 will
erect plant and sink the Deeps shaft as far
as he wants to go. So far his scheme for
cementing out the water has acted, but he
has 90ft. of fractured ground to get through,
and it would take a palmist to say what
will happen on the Avay.
Lancefield (W.A.) looks like being turned
to profitable account by the Kalgoorlie mine
managers’ syndicate which recently acquired
it. Returns for August and September
aggregated £17,000-odd, and the profit over
working exes. Avas £5OOO. A big surplus is
still required to recoup the syndicate for its

to be opened up in the bottom levels, this

A tale of Western sin:—
The present sporadic activity of the Goldfields
C.I.D. in scoring- convictious against gold-stealers
from the mines holds up to ridicule the system of
search. The underground men, who have to go
through a methodical examination, probably during
a Avhole year’s work never see a color, though the
stone may be pretty rich. But the workers in
treating plants have an opportunity of getting away
with the stuff in lumps, yet through some quaint
provision do not have to submit to the indignity of
examination. In the “good old days,” when there
was plenty of free gold showing in the face, how
many wore the schemes devised to collect some of
the curse! The best “joke” in our mine AA-as per-
petrated by a chap avc used to call “Grunter.” He
was a very unsociable cuss, and to any question,
civil or otherwise, his only answer Avas a grunt.
Hence his name. The searcher, a garrulous old
Cousin Jack, tried vainly to get a “pitch” on Avith
him, but AA'as always answered in pig’s language.
One of his Avorking mates, after some months of
this, bet his friends a fiver he Avould make
“Grunter” articulate. When meeting him at crib-
time that night, all his advances and taunts re-
ceded the same answer, till he connected “Grunter’s”
jaw Avith a dirty left. That loosened “Grunter’s”
tongue all right. It also loosened from its hiding
place a 4oz. slug of gold, Avhich prevented his free
speech and also explained . his taciturnity.
“Grunter” had reckoned that, if he never opened his
mouth to speak, nobody would suspect anything
when he kept his mouth shut to hide something.
Fred Tricks’s old mine, the Npav Loch
Fyne, of Matlock, near Woods Point (Vic.),
is to enjoy a second advent under John
Friel, the man who helped in the resurrec-
tion of George Dickenson’s Al. This time
the Loch Fyne will don the tartan of James
Mackay’g Queen-street, clan. It is vouched
that, “since the geological features at the
mine are similar to the Al and Rose of
Denmark mines, Gaffney’s Creek, there can
be little doubt that, as in these mines, the
ore occurrences remain permanent and will
maintain values at a depth.” This is about
as clear as mud. As the record of the New
Loch was 118,000 tons for £304,000 of gold
and £109,000 divs., the old Tricks crowd
wouldn’t have given up the show if the ore
occurrences had remained permanent. They
quitted because they couldn’t lay hands on
any more payable stuff above No. 4 adit,
and there wasn’t enough cash floating around
to take the dyke in bulk and shove it
through the battery on a huge scale. Want
of support also kept them from going-
deeper, and gave John Friel and his brother
the chance to pick up the plant and mine
for an old song. Seeing how the Al has
developed, James Mackay has every reason
for giving the Loch Fyne another run,
though he mustn’t talk about permanent ore
occurrences when nearly every vein or floor
has independent existence.
The whisper goes round that George
Dickenson, flushed with profits from the
Al, is shaping the old Morning Star al
Woods Point into form for a post-Cup car-
nival float.

iu another live head of stamps. Even with

2dwt. lOgr. ore —a remarkable achievement

at least a dwt. better.

Mount Shamrock, in the Maryborough
district (Q.), is to have another trial, a local
syndicate having taken it on. This show
has had several tests, and has produced a
good deal of gold, but the ore is refractory,
containing bismuth as well as gold and sil-
ver. It was at one time the only place
where telluride of bismuth was identified,
though possibly now it does not play a lone
hand in that respect. There is a 10-head
battery, with Wilfley tables and cyanide
plant, and a ton of concentrates recently
obtained from 100 tons of ore returned llOoz.
gold, lOoz. silver, and 10 p.c. bismuth, w’hich
is valuable just now. ~ If every 100 tons of
ore would do this, and the supply was equal
to the demand, the resurrection of the Sham-
rock should bring credit and renown, also
cash, to its instigators. Dunstan, Govt.
Geologist of Queensland, was at one time
interested in the property and held a very
high opinion of it.

The little Corolla group, near Cloncurry

Here driving south at 300 ft. is showing
pyrites over sft. wide in a silicious gangue,

Assistant-Geologist Hills holds out little
encouragement for the much talked of molyb-
denite shows on the north-east coast of
Tasmania. He thinks that one patch at
Mount Stronach might be worth prospect-
ing, but he is not optimistic about even that.
On the other hand he thinks that at the
price of 555. per unit offered by the Federal
Government, the wolfram deposits round
Ben Lomond and on the Upper Scamander
are worth developing on a much bigger scale.

# -X- * *
The deepest gold mine in the world, the St.
John Del Rey, in Brazil, has just paid 10 per
cent, on its capital for the year, a consider-
able sum being held in hand till the share-
holders decide whether they are going to test
values below “horizon 20.” They have a fine
large way of alluding to different levels in the
Sf. John as “horizons,” and “horizon 20” is a
bit over a mile and a Sheffield distance
down. This is a long way to drop cash if
“horizon 21” proves a duffer. It is fre-
quently quite unnecessary to go so deep to
lose money.
