A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, November S, 1915.
My Dear Ari.vi. —
This is from Carrie Swain, whom every
theatre-goer in Australia knew 20 years ago;
After all this time, talking to Australia again—-
it seems so strange, so unexpected. Yet I had to.
Every day from rny home here in Paris T go, like
most Avomen who have the time, into the hospitals.
Yesterday I saw a red cover, so familiar; it
brought me running to it. Behind it was as
splendid a Cornstalk as ever lifted a idfle to an
enemy, and I knew he was my brother, and straight-
away sat beside his cot and handed over all the
cigarettes I had. Then we chatted and laughed, and
were as happy as two children out of school, living
over again our days in Australia.
How bravely he and the other big brown men have
been fighting! What splendid manhood, what fine
courage!! I am proud of them, and long to give
that personal encouragement that counts for so much.
Cor the wounded among the brave soldiers of the
country which has been my home for 20 years 1
have given many concerts, and have convalescents
with me at my house iu groups, and then I am
indeed happy at their sweet, honest appreciation,
and we part as though we had known one another
all our lives and would soon meet again. God
grant that we may!
France is not the bright, happy land it was. You
rarely see people laughing. Only the English
soldiers’ seem merry and gay. The whole French
population appears to have suddenly become serious.
Even in appearance; everybody has laid aside light
and gay colors. The men have discarded even straw-
hats. On the national fete day there was something
sublime in the quiet dignity with which the French
passed their annual holiday. No music; no singing;
no dancing; no shouting; no noise. Only the occa-
sional displaying of flags and the quiet crowds of
families in the streets.
During the last few days a new feature
has come upon tl.e city. Thousands of sol-
diers from the firing-line, after nine months’
fighting, have been given four days’ leave
to return to wives and mothers. Brown and
war-beaten men with torn and faded coats every-
where. There is something in the lines of their
faces that tells you they are determined to win.
They seem to have grown so old.
How could the city but be stern? To-day I saw
five soldiers together with only five legs, and some
of the wounds are beyond description.
I was overwrought and needed a few days’ rest.
But where to go? Finally I thought of Jean and
his wife who, after 30 years’ service with one family
who are my friends, had put their savings away in
a little house near the sea where they take in a
guest or two. The good couple’s pleasure in greet-
ing me was almost pathetic. But Avhere was their
hoy, just 20 —-their one happiness and hope?
Promptly on the call to arms he had gone to the
Front with his battery, and every ten days some
brief, humid scrap in pencil came home to tell them
all Avas well. Months passed in this accustomed
regularity. Then the ten days lengthened into 20,
and finally a month without any tidings....
After a while the father went to an old desk, and
with trembling hands handed me an envelope. It
was in their boy's handwriting and said: “This is
my wish. Dear Parents, I desire this envelope not
to he opened except in ease you Avill be without
news from me for more than a month or you have
heard of my death. Charles.”
“Read within,” the old man said ; and was there
ever a more touchingly beautiful farewell message
from a son to his devoted parents
“These lines are my last thoughts and my supreme
adieu to my dear parents whom I adore. 1 have
always loved you more than I have been able to
tell, and if at times iu the misguided moments I
have strayed from your love I admit it to-day and
ask your forgiveness. Rest certain, my dear and
beloved parents, that if I die I will fall bravely for
France and our beautiful country. Receive from your
son who loves you fondly many kisses and remember
these two words which I send in embracing you—Du
Courage!—Charles.”
To-day I am back from a pilgrimage to Mailly on
the Marne, and there we left all the Union Jacks and
french flags we could get on the little mounds of
the dead soldiers —for maybe among them was many
another Charles.
The first orchestral concert here under
conductor Verbrugghen had to be housed in
the ToAvn Hall. The Conservatorium is
much too small for a great occasion. A
bulging eroAvd Avaited for the opening bar
of “God Save.” Verbrugghen struggled
through the platform, tossed a mop of hair
that seemed to be strands of electric Avires,
bowed to the Quality on the vice-regal
patch, and let it go. Our dull old National
Anthem brought a rare thrill. The Bel-
gian Anthem —a joyous lilt —shook a storm
of applause from the house. Then the small,
lithe figure in the conductor’s pen settled
down to serious business and Beethoven.
Verbrugghen conducts with a long, wand-
like baton, an empty hand and every nerve
in his body. He cajoles and inspires his
•orchestra. Light and shade are as deli-
cately Avorked out in sound waves as the
(color scheme of a Avihl rose. At the close
of Beethoven's big Symphony in C Minor
the Belgian had to return to his stand to
avaa’c an exhausted arm toAvards his fiddlers
in ans Aver to “hurrahs” from ail over the
hall.'

It was rather a poor part in the U.S. play.
But the Australian girl practically raised it
into “lead.” Florence is one of the few

a semi- toothless, begrimed fright. In a
rusty black frock, bursting open in front,
with a grubby apron, and a cap about the
size and color of a turnip that has fallen in
the coal-box, she was more like the London
slaveydom of Dickens’s days than any pre-
sent product of Woody Wilson’s country.

Lottie Dearn and her eldest sister Allie
are back from Europe. They tvere round-
ing off their music in Leipsic when the
enemy showed his claws. They got intact
to London, where Lottie resumed work
under the mane of Mark linmbourg. In
spite of his technique Mark used to slam-
bang the piano too heartily for most people’s
taste. Of late years he has adopted a more
soothing attitude. Mnvbe he found the old
style too expensiA r e. They say a piano used
to last him only three months.
Australian-born Justine Kong Sing Avrites
that she has been holidaying in the Bronte
country amongst Yorkshire moors. Her
hostess Avas Miss Kemp—the lady who has
turned out some solid books concerning her
wanderings in the East. Justine had a
slice of Yorkshire pudding, besides, under
the roof of Mrs. Claude Roe, an'Australian
Avhose husband is at the usual address in
Gallipoli.
The Benevolent Society’s appeal for help
this Christmas is voiced in its pamphlet.
“Yule Tide.” This granny of N. S. Wales
charitable institutions is 103 years old, and
has found out quite a lot of good ways of
spending money.
“The Govenor gave a lunching yester-
day,” the Telegraph tells us. It must be
the D.T. that always talks of the Blue
Mountings.
A daughter of “Jim” Gannon, K.C., has
just given a Waverley man, Ilarrie Dvcav.
the right to call round in the evenings
Avithout making excuses.
Artist Ruby Winekler, now in Boston
(U.S.), has just turned the corner after
bolting away from appendicitis. The Sydney
girl’s gossamer illustrations have caught the
fancy of W. Wilson’s subjects. She is
steadily climbing the ladder that leads to
dollars.
Mrs. Pain, Avife of the Gippsland bishon.
has come back to look at her lambs. Before
leaving Sydney Parson Pain used to hand
out the text in St. John’s, Darlinghurst.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, November 8, 1935.
Mv Dear Yarrje.—
This C.up may have been a success as a
mere race meeting, but socially it was rag-
tag. First of all there was no vice-regal
circus; the Fergusons just rolled along to
the terrace steps in a limousine and made a
quiet, plain-clothes entry. Seen in the
official pen bereft of her carriage furs, her
Fx. was dressed in a soft smother of grey,
with puffy white tulle and lace about the
bodice and big grey feathers in her hat.
Governor Barron, of Westralia, yawned
with them. He wore an eyeglass and a
grey suit. The Pudukotas were close by.
The Also Ran-ee looked weary. Her very
fair hair was groomed smoothly back under
a shady hat, and she wore a dress made of
a curious Indian silk, with gold flowers
woven into its soft grey folds. Her other
things were already packed and down at the
mail-boat bound for India. The skipper and
most of the passengers saw the Cup run and
then fled for the wharf on the stroke of six.
Governor Stanley, in a comfortable grey
crush lounge-suit, vice-regaled away the
hours in the birdcage with his trusty Gent-
help at his elbow.
Mrs. Patrobas Widdis was in neat navy
tailorings with a dream of a hat made up of
buff and white and blue. The Widdises
refused to drink the noble brute’s health in
champagne, but sent the wine to the hos-
pitals. Russell Clarke and his missus (she
used to be Miss Mackenzie of Tasmania)
were there. She sported a particularly mag-
nificent series of furs, which reached from
chin to boot-tips. They were off some flossy
brown animal which had no further pse for
them. Roma Clarke, having exhausted the
possibilities of ordinary clothes, put in a
bid for publicity in a hideous yellow and
black suit which terminated not far below
her knees. A sister attracted some notice
with a tiny parasol tip-tilted at one side
like a broad-brimmed hat. One woman,
evidently a Best Dresser, walked with her
head in a filmy blue-bag; the veil disguised
her effectively. She incautiously sat down
on the terrace wall and her knee-short navy
silk skirts looked like a ballet dancer’s tulle
frill. Mrs. Leslie Stuart had a pale blue
stripe in her neat khaki suit and balanced
her duck of a hat on her nose like a juggler.
Hocking, from Westralia, was here and
there. He has been buying new horses,
and threw up his hat when one of them
won the Cup Steeplechase. The Manifolds,
from Camperdown, were in force as usual.
The womenfolk, being representatives of a
racing district, stuck to sensible tailor-
mades.
One Gippsland farmer-lady had a small
and early bet on Patrobas for the Cup.
When the colt passed the post ahead of the
mob, she gasped “Oh! I’ve won £140!” and
straightway fainted all over the crowd. Poor
creatures that we are, our racing minds are
so used to working on a half-crown basis
that a shower of gold stuns us ! If I ever
won more than 7s. Gd. in one lump I would
have to knock the G.-G.’s grey belltopper off
his head or die.
Mrs. Widdis, owner of Patrobas, is the
only woman who has ever gained immor-
tality with a Cup winner. And she and
Mrs. Alawa Buchanan are the only two who
have owned a Derby winner.
Federal Government House was a quiet
place this Cup-time. There was no free
entertainment for anybody beyond a heavy
dinner or two for mandarins of the first
grade. As soon as the Cup was emptied,
alt the Excellencies rushed away from the
big, grey house as if some evil soul had put
ferrets into it.
During the Flemington horse scramble, a
big grey Wolseley motor Avas run out on to the
promenade lawn. It was a raffle car donated
to the Australian Red Cross by Mr. Gar-
land, of the Dunlop Tyre Co. The climax
of the gamble is timed for November 26, and
the tickets are fi\ T e shillings each. Do you
want one?
The second patriotic dinner, eaten at the
Savoy on the Wednesday night after the
Cup, folloAved the first too closely to be a
big success. The Best Fed still‘had their
appetites; but they had been engaged far
ahead for the horse season. The Bowes
Kellys, Widgiewa Falkiners, George Fair-
bairns and Barlows AA'ere hosting. Elderly
lawyer Bowman, from Sydney, Avas at a
party given to his fiancee, Miss Rhodes.
Swedish Consul Aon Goes ran another groan-
ing board. Eventually the proceedings
became frivolous; the tables were shoved
aside and dancing began, \vhen the revelry
Avas fairly started a young squatter-visitor,
avlio had been dining quietly upstairs,
waltzed in Avith something particularly chic
in black. The matrons thereupon put up
their glasses and eyebrows and shoulders
and noses, and presently the poor young
things became aware of the change and
drifted out again. The auctioning results
did not stagger or delight the charitable.
But there are promises—or are they threats?
—that the next blow-out will be very care-
fully inflated beforehand.
Ancient history telleth us that Sassiety
got some excitement out of the cafe ehantant
at Raveloe —the Emmertons’ house on South
Yarra hill. It was really a big race-week
frivol circumspectly dressed in Red Cross
disguise. Entrance was by expensive
tickets ; but every care was taken that the
passports fell only into well manicured hands
or perfectly-fitting gloves. It was an even-
ing affair and the clothes were gorgeous.
The Hodges, Grices, Fairbairns and Clarkes
were all “helping” with the raflles and so
forth, and the song programme was just like
one of the old-time expensive At Homes of
Cup week. The Bowes Kellys, Dalrymples
and Fannings were to the fore, and Lady
Helen Ferguson shed a brilliant vice-regal
blessing over all. Mrs. Emmerton and her
daughter, Mrs. Norman Brookes, received
her Ex., who was a vision of grey embroi-
deries and pink roses, and made a stirring
Red Cross speech. Miss McAdam and her
fiancd received congratulations, and there
was some confusion as to which of the
numerous khaki boys had just become en-
gaged and which were brand-new bride-
grooms. Marriages are so hurried and
quiet nowadays that one can’t keep track
of them all without a card system.
Steeplechase Saturday at Flemington
closed the greatest week of the 52. A storm
of rain came just before the big race ; till then
the heavens were supported on a pillar of
dust. It was curious to see every tidy man
whip off his hat at the first patter of rain-
drops and give it 25 lashes with a big hankie
or spank it by hand. Women flew to cover
like barnyard ebooks, the frilly variety hav-
ing a particularly lively time. One smart
young dame who had very short skirts
showed extra-plump calves in tight white
silk stockings as she scuttled for the crowded
stand and flew straight into a thicket of
umbrellas.
Daisy Jerome was very smart in grey
satin, with a grey cloth cape and grey velvet
bonnet. Melba’s daughter-in-law, Mrs. Arm-
strong, was startling because of the size of
her Puritanical cape-collar of white muslin
over black taffetas. Everard Browne was a
symphony in autumn-leaf shades and took
life easily as one of a man-party. Justice
Cussen walked modestly in spite of the
Aveight of the thanks of both Houses which
had been heaped upon him during the week.
This was for re-stuffing the horsehair
sofa of the Law, which was down on its
springs. Lawyer Casey wandered about
looking thoughtful—perhaps because brother
Dick is still in the hands of the surgeons.
The G.-G.’s collection had scattered off to
Sydney and the visitors had gone off to their
various homes, but Stanley and a Man Fri-
day or two managed to get along to see the
end. Mrs. IT. S. K. Ward looked really noble
in all-grey tailorings, and Mrs. Rankin, from
Maoriland. was a vision in soft white, with
a black Gainsborough hat. The lonely
Fenner girl partied with some Rutherglen
friends. She was befrilled from neck to
toe with stiff glacd silk, and was forced to
beat down the fluttering waves as the wind
flung them at her face. The handsome Bail-
lieu girl wore a sleeveless navy suit (there
were short sleeves of net, you know) and a
flat pancake hat with flattened ostrich fea-
thers. _ Mrs. Arthur Richards (who used to
be Miss Rowe) walked the laAvn in the
cutest, dress of frilled Avhite glace. Her hus-
band is at work in India. Mrs. McEvoy
swathed her neck in a black satin choker
and wore plain muslin frills up to her nose;
the rest of her clothes were biscuit-color and
the hat Avas a bleached straw bird’s nest.
Adelaide’s despatches:—
The Medina smoked over the horizon with long
decks dotted with neighbors. W. J. Isbister, a
Avell-known barrister, is bound for Red Cross work
in Egypt; Mrs. Edgar Brown follows her doetor-
husband, who has already gone per transport to
No. 1 Base Hospital; Mrs. Carew Reynell, widow
of the late Lieut.-Colonel Reynell, is going as far
as Egypt; Mrs. Longbottom and her daughters are
bound for somewhere else; and Mrs. Humphrey
Makin will probably fetch up in Egypt, where her
husband went. Meanwhile the sea has given back
to us the Guy Makins and R. W. ITa'ckett. The
latter put in a dreary time in a German internment
camp. Another arrival is Mrs. Wright, widow of
the late Captain Wright, who was killed in France
in the early days of the war. He aviis A.D.C. here
in the Bosanquets’ time. She is on a visit to her
people, the T. E. Barr Smiths.
Two more old Prince Alfred College boys—T. E.
Cleland and R. S. Davey-—have got commissions in
England. Archdeacon Samwell’s only son is on his
way to shell-fire from Sydney.
Lieut.-Colonel’s Miell’s estate has been sworn at
about £23,000. It goes to his vndow and children.
The financial “feeders” for the forthcoming Gov-
ernment House fete are now bidding for cash. One
of the tributary shows is a small exhibition of the
pictures that are to furnish the art section of the
Australian stall. Lady Galway looked in during the
afternoon, and a good many of the canvases were
sold.
The engagement is proclaimed of William, the
third of the William Gilbert sons, of Pewsey Vale,
to Pauline, the younger of the T. L. Brownes’ daugh-
ters. Last week Margaret Hughes, a cousin of the
leading lady, wedded Roy, the eldest of the \V. L.
Davidsons’ sons.
From England comes news of the marriage of Mrs.
Cordon Ayers to Mr. E. Boddam-Wetham. She was
one of Adelaide's smartest and best-looking young
women, and he was here on the Challenger some
time back, lie is a brother of a one-time A.D.C.
to this State's Government House. Another peaceful
engagement is that of Harold, one of the H. B.
Hugheses, of Atlielney, to E. Margaret, the second
daughter of the late E. C. Gwynne, of Payneham.
The “Hut,” built by the Cheer-Up Society for the
use of soldiers, was given its final coat of paint and
its last polish during the week, and then declared
ready for use by the Governor.
Allan Morey, who has annexed a Military’ Cross
for gallantry at Loos, went to England as a Rhodes
Scholar not long before the present turmoil com-
menced. He volunteered to cross open spaces
between the lines to get information, and went some
distance after being wounded to report to his com-
manding-officer.
Mrs. John Downer has booked her passage, and
that of her small son, for Egypt. She will stay
there awhile and then go on to England, but she
talks of returning to S.A. within a year.
The Way Campbells, who have the travel habit,
are off again to England. That will be once out
and once back since the war commenced.
Mabel Marryat and Joan O’Halloran Giles have
come back from England and Scotland and the
Seven Seas, including the Spanish Main.
God Save the King!
Family-solicitor Edward Rigby is this
week being installed as one of the eight lay
canons of St. Paul’s Cathedral. It is a rare
office —a sort of spiritual V.C. —and is the
subject of a certain amount of dignified com-
petition when death removes one of the elect.
Dolly Castles is leaving for the land of
the star-spangled dove at the end of the
month, and is spending her last weeks in
Melbourne in a private hospital, after a
slight operation. She is, however, progress-
ing favorably and declines to be down-
hearted.
Mrs. Brookes, who used to be Ivy Deakin,
has gone off to Egypt to join husband ll> v
bert. lie is one of Adrian .Knox’s helpers
in straightening out the Red .Cross tangle.
Louise Mack made a bolt through Mel-
bourne to Sydney and a rest, but not before-
she had been one of a party to plant wattle
trees at the Base Hospital and attended
a luncheon party with a strong Parlia-
mentary flavor which was given in her
honor. Andrew Fisher and the Pearce pair
attended, and the party afterwards went
along and listened in the newly-arranged
House while Fislier was congratulated on
giving himself a new job.
Young Dr. Frank Keane, who used to
heal Glenferrie, went to France at the begin-
ning of this gunpowdery year, and was a
minor in No. 5 General Hospital to starl
with. Now he has been hoisted to the posi
of Assistant Surgical Specialist at No. 1
Hospital, Rouen. He had a bout of illness
on his own account some time ago, but won
it on points.
Ex-Gaud Mayor Henry Weedon’s younger
brother, W.J., who lately enlisted in the
A.1.F., left the other day for the Front as
a private.
The Frederick Blackwood lordliug, who
left Melbourne at the first jump for the war.
has been invalided to England from the
fighting ditches in France. He is Major
Blackwood now; in the Boer War, in which
he won a D. 5.0., he was a lieutenant in the
Lancers.
Pretty Dollie Parker, daughter of the ex-
judicial Parker, of Westralia, remained at
home when the family moved east. Now
comes the news that she has accepted young
Alec Macleod, who is one of the irresistible
wounded heroes.
Mrs. Ted Mitchell has hurried off to Eng-
land to comfort the eldest girl, whose hus-
band has a naval job. A younger daughter
goes with mother; the other remains to see
that the K.C. gets his breakfast on time.
A cable from Frank Carse saying that he
had been invalided to Egypt sent his little
wife off at top speed in the Medina.
Our warriors in England are as prompt
at the altar rails as they are in the trench.
Aviator Foster Rutledge has been to church
with Mildred Manifold, daughter of the
Willie Manifolds, of Purrumbete, Camper-
down, and popular Freda Shaw, another
member of the squattocracy, lias said “Yes”
to Ronald Pyman, a likeable English youth
who was connected with the oil industry
before he went to war.
The latest arrivals from Germany are
Elliel Liggins, the Sydney singer, and Mar-
garet Edson. the pianist. They were domi-
ciled in a Berlin pension when the lid blew
off Europe, but sat tight and said nothing-
till the first burst was over. About the
time the Germans were digging themselves
in on the Marne they packed their grips and
slipped over to England. Unluckily they
had to leave behind them a piano and a
varied assortment of luggage. You can’t fly
with a piano.
Colonel Tivey’s wife, who belongs to the
house of Robb —Malvern wing—has started
a little association, composed of wives,
mothers and other interested relatives of
her husband’s fellow-officers. The amiable
objects of the new association are (1) tea
drinking; (2) keeping one another’s spirits
up; (8) looking after the interests of every
soldier in the brigade (Btli Inf.) while he is
away. If the thing is done with thorough-
ness and tact it should help quite a lot.
Henry Barnet, of the St. Kilda Council,
and probably the most effective parks-gar-
dener about Melbourne, led a deputation up
to Mayor Hewison the other day. kowtowed
three times and asked that the Y.M.C.A.
might be allowed to build a rest-house for
soldiers on the St. Kilda foreshore, with
enough room to grow a few sweet peas on
the sunny side. The Mayor’s reply, con-
cealed in polite phrases, was understood to
be favorable; and now .Tews and Gentiles,
the local Patriotic Committee, the Shrimpers’
Association and the rest are working to give
the Christians their hearts’ desire. The
rest-house is to be a neat, commodious shaek
ac the back of the Palais du Danse, with a
free lawn and sea-breeze available for every
soldier.
The women of the Overseas Club, who
want a shipload of vegetables for our legion-
aries in the trenches, gave a c-afe chantant
last Friday night in the Club rooms. There
was every known contrivance for getting
money out of the public ; but what is wanted
is something like a milk-shake machine
which will seize and rattle the visitors till
all their money falls out of their clothes.
It cost only lialf-a-crown to get in to the
bushranging. Some of the preserved vege-
tables and fruits were on view and made my
mouth water. The Club cleverly coaxed
Professor Osborne down from the University
and got him to explain the high food values
of vegetables and fruit. The flock of even-
ing-dressers looked quite intelligent for a
while; but they soon tired of hearing about
the gastric juice and began to yawn
politely. Mrs. Jansen attended to the music
and Mrs. Barlow ran a cigar stall. Miss
George peddled flowers and sweets, and
a platoon of girls sold programmes indoors
and outside in Fiinders-street. Mrs. Secre-
tary James, a gracious figure in lilac, told
everybody just what the Club was doing
with the bottled vegetables. Mrs. Dyer
came along in the good cause of cabbage
with a sunburst of gold embroidery on her
black dress. Mrs. Eggleston, wife of the
lawyer, sat in a bead cloak, and Mrs. God-
frey and her daughters were on hand in
clothes which defied the Finger of Scorn.
Mrs. Senator Pearce in soft dove-grey duds
was with daughter Dorothy, and her man-
o’-war came along later with Andrew Fisher.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
THE UMPIRE.
FOOTBALLER (suddenly hearing the familiar
sound of a whistle): “Ah, gum! Wot
for? I never touched ’im!”
“Itan”: Perhaps the greatest resort for
wild fowl in Queensland is Lake Buchanan, 40 miles
from Aramac. Not far away is Lake Dunn, also a
great bird resort. Buchanan, during a wet season,
is 20 miles in circumference. Birds frequent these
places in the breeding season in millions —ducks,
geese, swans and others. Lake Dunn, even when
brimming, is so full of bird life that the water is
useless for stock. Buchanan is uecter in that
respect, as the water is deeper. In the nesting season
the eggs lay around in millions, but as small settlers
are beginning to spread out into those parts, most
likely they will interfere with the sanctuary. If
possible, some means should be adopted to conserve
the birds, too few now in Australia.
“Tibs”: He has been passed out already
four times on the same charge, but is still game.
“ ’Lllo, woo-woo-woo-where you going?” “I’m going
to enlist, Bill.” “Wa-wa-wa-wait a bub-bub-bub-bub-

“Overlander”: I have seen no mention of
the supple-jack or vine tree, which is the best and
most even heat-giver on the high Downs, where fuel
is scarce. Next to it in value are the knubblv roots
of tlie currant-bush, more prized because found where
no other fuel is available —out near Connolly’s Water-
hole and Bessie’s Bush, on the track between Camoo-
weal and Anthony’s, where, failing it, one has to
fall back on the last resource of the cattle-man, viz.,
“buffalo chips.” The drawback to a damper cooked
with these is similar to that baked on dogwood of
New England. They flavor the batch. On the coast
the roots of the mangrove are No. 1 for an even
heat. The red rnulga is good in the “corner” about
Clondilla Downs. Absolutely the worst timber to
light in Australia to my knowledge is the peppermint
of New England. You can always light a fire in
stringy-bark country even when it is raining. Strip
a sheet for shelter, use the middle dry bark for
kindling, and you will soon have hot tea, which is
the compensation for wet saddles when the cattle are
moving.
* * * *
“Mulga” : The monkey-vine mentioned by
lick (B. 14/10/15) probably has no superior as
a bush reservoir; but, unfortunately, it is indigenous
only in coastal areas. In Central and Western Queens-
land, however, there are two trees from which bush-
men can replenish their waterbags in drought time. One
is the bottle-tree, which needs only cutting into a
few inches and a bountiful supply is obtainable. The
other is the kurrajong, the surface roots of which, if
cut into short lengths and hung up over a receptacle,
will trickle out a good helping. Like the liquid
from the vine the fluid has a resinous or gummy
taste, but it is a welcome thirst-quencher when there
is nothing else going.
* * » *
“V.”: Vanilla-growers in Tahiti suffer a
sc\ ere disability through the lack of co-operation on
the part of insect life, of which there is practically
none in the island, barring wasps and mosquitoes.
As a consequence the fertilisation of the flowers has
to be done by hand. The male portion of the flower
(I do not know its Sunday name) is shaped like a
tongue and the female like a cup. When the flowers
arrive the tongue has to be cut off and placed in
contact with the feminine part to secure fertilisation,
this operation is a delicate one, and has to be per-
formed on the first day the flower opens, otherwise
it is too late. The native, being a born procrasti-
nator, hates blossoming time for this reason, but he
has to get the job done to pay the interest on the
mortgage, generally held by John Chinaman.
*****
-Le Babarantiko”: According to news
biought from the Gilbert Group, Bob Correy and
family have drifted away into the Pacific, while
sailing in a cutter from Maiana to Tarawa, and are
apparently gone for good. That makes the second
time the patriarch has drifted away from Maiana.
A good many years ago he and another trader were
whisked away in a dory, and eventually
brought up on one of the Marshall atolls
not much the worse for the grisly ex-
perience. The distance from Maiana to
larawa is but 18 miles, yet the crossing
involves considerable risk, owing to the
mighty Equatorial Current which usually
runs to the westward, and there have
been many drift-aways. Usually they
bring up in the western Marshalls, the
Carolines or the outlying islands east of
the Solomons. (A cargo boat which
drifted away, empty, from Ocean Island
brought up on the coast of New Guinea.)
Many perish on that stretch of from 240
to 1000 miles of ocean. The most re-
markable case during my residence in
the Gilbert Group was that of the late
AVilson, trading on Nonouti. Just to
show everybody, he, with a native crew,
sailed out of Nonouti Lagoon bound for
the-few-miles-distant Tabeteuea Atoll.
Outside the lagoon they were becalmed,
and the craft promptly drifted away.
Days dragged by, and when they were on
their last cocoanut they sighted Baker
Island, a worked-out guano dot, 600 miles
due east from Nonouti, but were unable
to reach it for the current. Here the miracle hap-
pened. Burdett-Coutts’ yacht, the Laurel, happened
along, took the famished men on board, and towed
the boat back to Nonouti.
* * * *
“S. S. Severn”: Nearly every Papuan vil-
lage has its albino, blue-eyed most of them, with
rose-pink skins and garish yellow curls. The gild-
ing is too acute; it has the aura of poison weed.
The freaks appear to be throwbacks to some long-
since white strain. In Motu-Motu, a very huge
Tauripi folk village, I saw a beautiful child, with
agate-hued eyes and golden hair that rippled to her
knees, sitting under a palm-tree with her whole
soul engrossed in hanging a garland of frangi-
pani flowers about the neck of a village pig. The
Samoan missionary when questioned about the girl
shrugged his fat shoulders, and said: “Proper
native; no can white.” Which, interpreted, meant,
though the skin was white, no peroxide on earth
could bleach the personality.

“D.F.”: A white seal came ashore at
Burnie (Tas.) the other morning. For a day or two
it disported itself quite happily about the mouth of
the creek at South Burnie, and then some destruc-
tive idiot knocked it on the head, alleging as an
excuse that he thought it was going to bite him.
I wish it had bitten him to death. There are
millions of fools around, but white seals are rare.

“W.G.8.”: Re the common belief that
alligators use the claws or feet for grabbing their
victims. I have seen three horses that have been
attacked by the devils, and none of them had been
touched with the claws, but all showed marks of the
teeth. One know-all insisted that claws had torn one
of the horses on the head till the vet. pulled out half
an alligator tooth that had broken off. Even when
feeding the reptiles do not use their claws to tear
the victuals.
* * * #
“Paroo Joe”: Not the kurrajong but the
mulga is the champion cattle fodder-tree of Aus-
tralia. The far west of N.S.W. has just emerged
from one of the severest droughts on record, yet
a recent consignment of six-year-old bullocks, from
Darling River country, realised thirty pounds per
head at Homebush. The Sydney agents stated that
the cattle were fed upon hay, but I’m game to bet
my new swag-strap that they were not. Further,
I’ll bet they had not seen grass for three years, but
were fattened exclusively upon mulga.

“Werrimea”: Quirindi (N.S.W.) Gazette
(8/10/’l5) offers an explanation as to the great
disparity between shearers’ tucker bills this year.
“In our own district this season the mess accounts
have varied from as low as 16s. to as high as 305.,
which is somewhat remarkable. But the men have
only got themselves to blame. When they get a
quid a head for voting for a cook if he gets the
job, they must know that he will fleece them for it
afterwards. And there are several easy ways of doing
it, too—spiriting away the stuff that comes from
the store and charging up stuff that never comes at
all being the most common dodges. In the latter
case, of course, the cook and the storekeeper are in
the game, and cookie generally winds up with a
watch and chain or a spanking new suit.” This is
the first time I have heard of a shearers’ cook buying
votes. As he gets from Is. to 6s. per man per week,
he would require a long-running shed to make it pay.
Of course, he would buy only doubtful votes, but
even a week’s pay out of a month-shed is a lot—
unless a pushing storekeeper helped him. Then the
shearer’s tucker bill would be swollen by more than
he got for his vote.
“Con Lea”: Allow me to inform “Sas-
safras” (B. 2/9/’l5) that on the upper Tweed River
districts I have heard the lyre mimic the scrub-
turkey frequently ; in fact it seems to be the prin-
cipal part of his performance. I have even run
across the “wappett” (turkey) in close proximity to
a mimic, no doubt under the impression that a
cobber was about. But “Sassafras” is quite correct
when he says the lyre does not imitate the wonga.
I’ll add that I've never known him to imitate any
of the pigeon species. Why, 1 don't know. And old
"hands can't give me any satisfaction.

BAR-BIN’ DOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the big machine’s been busy the best part of
a week
And punched a dozen “twentv-foots” around the jut-
ting peak ;
When Powder Monkey Murphy has ’em loaded and in
trim
(An old hand at the game is he —we call him “Care-
ful Jim”),
Along the wires the eager spark upon its mission
goes
To rouse the latent demons to mad, mountain-rending
throes.
And when the row is over and cleared the dust and
din
Of several hundred falling tons—well, that’s when
ice come in !
It's treach’rous sort of country, among these tumbled
rocks—•
Last year a hundred tons came down and crushed
poor Davie Knox.
So ere the men may start below a gang of us must
work
Along the “face” to clear the spots where hidden
dangers lurk.
And this our occupation—it's one of small renown—
To make things safe and tidy. They call it “Darrin’
Down.”
With bar in hand adown the ropes we take our
careful wSy
To where the loosened boulders hang; our mates
above must pay
The ropes by niggard inches out as we may make
demand,
While scratching footholds in the “face” whereon to
take our stand.
Perched like the chamois on a ledge, a careless
glance we throw
To where the grim rocks wait for us a hundred feet
below.
With judgment sure, and practised hand, we prize
the lurkers loose
And send them roaring to the place where they may
be of use;
And if there’s any breeze at all the boulders as they
fly
Start up a cloud of gritty dust that hits us in the
eye.
But blinded eyes or slipping feet must not to fear
excite,
For we’ve to keep our wits awake, stay cool, and
hang on tight.
Though we’re not strong on souls to save, still we
allow ’tis true
Fa eh has a precious carcase that’s of use to me or
you.
We toil until below it’s safe as in a street in town —
And then we’ve well and truly done our job of
barrin’ down.
ff.S.W.

“Billy T.”: Breaking the news is really
a fine art. Two years ago Richard had started taking
gloomy views of life. At 28 lie agreed with the
ancient who found that existence was vanity and
vexation. Dick talked little, and when he did talk
his favorite theme was the relative merits of various
methods of shuffling off or “snuffing it.” The gun,
tne rope, the river or the poison bowl —which?
Dick’s relatives said: "Ah. gain, you’re all right.
Dick! Dick decided on the rope. Tom Harvey
found him hanging in the barn. The rope method
had proved most effectual. Tom Harvey called Holt
and Cameron and Dick’s brother Ernest. Holt
climbed up to the rafters with a tommy-axe to cut
Dick down. He was a little too precipitate, and
Dick fell on Ernie. Ernie took this in very bad part.
He lay quite still. It was necessary to carry both
or them home, and Holt was sent on ahead to break
the news to Dick’s mother. Holt arrived all out
of breath. “Please, missus, somethin’s ’appened," he
said. “Ernie's hurted.” "What is it!” cried the
stricken woman. “Tell me! Tell me!” “I dumo,
missus. On'y a few broken ribs, I think. They’re
bringin him up.” Holt went away, but turned back
from the gate, and put his head in at the kitchen
door again. “This is how if 'appened, missus.” he
said. "When we was ontiin' Dick down he fell on
Ernie.”
Following its trout-fry distributions,
A.S.W. Fisheries Department has been transplant-
ing fresh-water lobsters. Several crustaceans were
lifted front one part of the Alurrnmbidgee at Bin-
gagee and liberated in the Uinaralla, which begins
its ramblings near the junction of the Monaro and
Couroek ranges. Another batch began its new life
in the Nepean at Penrith. The ’Bidgee is a fine
stream to produce fresh-water Fa ( men. The
I maralla, Molonglo, Queanbeyan and Yass rivers
trickle in on the right bank, and the Naas Creek,
Cotter, Goodradigbee and Tumut dribble from the
left; and in-all of these there is abundance of feed.

“Pick”: The speed at which a fence can
be run up (It. 14/10/To) depends entirely upon
(1) kind of fence, (2) depth of post-holes, (3) nature
of the sinking. In soft, sandy country, where a
toncer can shovel out a post-hole in 3-1 digs, such a
line of posts as “Mnlga” quotes for the champion-
ship might be erected between jackass and mopoke.
1 have, in fact, heard of cove (though 1 never
met him) who fenced so far in a clay that it took
him three days to walk back to his coat. With
post-and-rail fences, however, and in hard country,
the going is different, and as a performance that
calls for bettering, i want to mention 176 panels of
two-railed fence put up during half a round-about
of the planet, at Rose Vale, near Bungonia (N.S.W.),
by old Amby Carter and young Amby his son. The
latter did the digging and ramming, whilst dad
adzed and fitted the rails, and the yakker was for
a man who stood no slumming.
-* ■* * *
“Hamer”; The rabbit has been carrying
out a vigorous offensive in the north-eastern regions
of N. S. W ales, where a few years ago it was practi-
cally unknown, and the stock inspector has warned
the coastal districts that the country is becoming
slowly but surely infested, One of the indications
of the advance of the rabbit is the retreat of the
hare, which invariably disappears when the swarm-
ing relative comes down on the fold. The country
along the Northern line was well-stocked with hares
some time back, but you need a 2-h.p. telescope to
see signs of them now.

“Moana”: Maoriland says that recently
when 10 of its men dug 572 post-holes in 3,1 hours, a
record was established. The necessity behind the effort
was the getting ready of the course at Cambridge
(M.L.) for a race meeting. One of the workers was
over 60, but he succeeded in outing everyone but
the gun, who beat him by a couple of holes.

“Milo”: In most things connected with
ihe Burke and Wills expedition, Burke’s journal was
noi clear, but his records leave little room to
doubt that the expedition did reach the Gulf. One
of the entries refers to some of the party catching-
sig-ht of its waters. Within the last four
years ample evidence has been obtained to
establish that the expedition accomplished
what it set out to do. The track from Nor-
manton to Burketown crosses the Little
Hynol, about a mile and a half inland from
the limit of the tidal waters of the Gulf. It
forms part of the delta of the flinders, and
beside this stream, close to the limit of the
tide movement, a coolibali tree grows. Some
time ago the manager of Magoura station,
-Mr. Graham, noticed on this tree what looked
like an old surveyor’s mark. It was over-
grown with a thick growth of new wood,
which Mr. Graham removed; and under-
neath he found the letters “B. & AAL, 1861.”
I’hc work appeared to have been done with
a crude instrument, but the letters are
plain. The thickness of new growth indi-
cates that it was very many years since the
initials were cut, and such a growth could
not have developed had the marks been made
since this part of the Gulf Country was
settled. On another tree Mr. Graham found
traces of initials, which are believed to be
those of a subsequent explorer who had
been searching for flic lost expedition. I
have been unable to ascertain what these
initials arc, but the date is 1862. Another
tree, which grows some distance from the
Duchess, in Western Queensland, also bears
the letters “B. & AAV’ These were probably
t u;i de by the explorers, because the party’s journal
indicates that they travelled through this country.

“Romney”: My troubles about the breed-
ing of eels. Here's the way to cook them. Skin
in the usual way, until the abdominal cavity is laid
bare. Remove contents, and replace with a mixture
of butter, breadcrumbs, anchovy paste, pepper,
thyme and parsley or onions. Also smear the body
with these, minus the breadcrumbs. Replace the
skin, and hang deceased beak upwards in front of a
fire, giving the string an occasional twist; or place
him on his back among the embers. Cooked that
way an eel is a feast for a. king, or even a book-
maker.

“S.T.S.": Wha.t does “ging" mean? Or
what did it mean in Sydney in 1801? The new
volume of the Historical Records of Australia shows
that Marshall called Macarthur “a Ging rascal,’' and
was at once challenged to a duel. Henley’s wide-
raking slang-dictionary does not seem to know any-
thing’ of the offensive adjective. As a guess, it may
have been “G—-ing.” The blank might stand either
for some simple word like “grinning,” which
Governor King’s official reporter happened to write
indecipherable, or for some verb he thought too
shocking to write in full.

“Whosit”: A beak at Little River, near
Christchurch (M.L.), fined two defendants a fiver
each the other day for collecting black swans’ eg"-
They had several barrels full of the fruit in a can'
and as the Law says that the fruit is
the magistrate had to lie rude, lint the black swm
is becoming Something of a pest in Maoriland. Lake
Lllcsmere, where these two men got the eggs i-
heavily stocked with the fowl, which collar ’the
mod oi the local duck and help clean up the crops
thereabouts. The other day a party with guns
wiped out swans in thousands to have them frozen
and sent to the starving Belgians, hut still left
enough to be a nuisance. The statutory protection
afforded the animal is under an Act in which black
swans are described as “native game." What has
Westralia to say to that?

“Itan”: Re "Pick’s” monkey-vine water
supply (B. 14/10/’lo), has anyone noticed a scrub
tree growing on the east coast which drips water
from knots on the branches? 1 remember one or
these in Maclean, Clarence River, when the town
was a mere hamlet. It grew in a small scrub. On
several of its branches the moisture exuded in a
lrothy state, and as the small globules
cohered into drops they fell to the ground. I have
been about the bush a good deal, but have never
seen another of the same description. The water
which fell from it was as clear and sweet as drops
from a thunderstorm.
HIS FIRST FAG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a very little boy.
He sat upon a log.
His smudgy face was reft of joy.
A mixed-up kind of dog
Sat at his feet, and seemed to be
A deal more woebegone than he.
The small boy was a bilious white,
Which is a sort of cream ;
And in his fingers, still alight,
He held a “smoke.” A dream
Of ancient horrors seemed to fly
Across his weak, blue, sodden eye.
“What ails you, Thomas John?” I said;
“Why does your spirit sag?”
oil. wearily he shook his head,
And offered me the fag.
“Please go,” he gasped, “and send a. cart —
I think it must be smoker’s heart.”
VICTORIA.

FATHER'S COMPREHENSIVE DRINKINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Poor father drank !” the flapper sighed—
She’d nothing else to say.
The sad and silent caller eyed
The clock that ticked alway.
“Yes,” said the maiden thin of shank,
“I thought you lcneio that father drank!”
“Oh, no!” he groaned, “I never knew!”
She squeaked a faint “Go on!”
And from its dusty cupboard drew
i The fam’ly skeleton ;
Then made the darned thing reel and leap
To keep her visitor from sleep,
if
He listened helpless to her tale,
And licked unmoistened lips ;
His poor tongue tasted dry and stale....
And father had had whips!
Thirsting and coated in the hall —
“Well, good night! Did he drink it allt”

THE DECANTER.
From the Office Curate: —
A curious side-issue of the war has been the con-
troversy which has raged in the Cold Country as to
the appearance of angels at Mons. Briefly, the story
is that when the British troops were retreating-
before the German cavalry in August, 1914, and
when it appeared as though they would be cut to
pieces, a- squadron of angels materialised out of the
air, and, frightening the Germans’ horses in the best
Old Testament manner, drove the Huns back. No
one seems to know who first started the yarn, but it
spread like a bush-fire and found its way into the
press all over Britain. In no instance was it told
by an eye-witness, but was always given at second-
hand as “the story of a wounded officer (unnamed)
to Miss M., daughter of the well-known Canon M.,”
or something equally vague.
Still, thei-e was an uneasy feeling in pious circles
that it would be just as well to produce one eye-
witness, at any rate, to confound the sceptics ; and
the demand found the man. Private Robert Cleaver,
of the first battalion Cheshire Regiment, invalided
home from the Front, modestly admitted in his
Flintshire village that he had seen the angels;
anyone could, and many did, hear his first-hand testi-
mony in the local pub. When the gossip reached
the ears of the Squire, who is a J.P. and a church-
warden, he sent for Cleaver and got him to make an
affidavit with which to confute the ungodly scoffers.
Cleaver confined his sworn statement to the sentence
“I saw the angels at Mons”; and in the amplifying
remarks which he made to the J.P. under cross-
examination—and which the J.P. at once sent to the
press—he was admirably vague. “Suddenly the
Vision came between them and the German cavalry,
lie described it as ‘a flash.’ I asked him if the
angels were mounted or winged. He could say no
more than that it appeared as ‘a flash.’ ” Even this
vague testimony was greedily seized upon as giving
a knock-out to the sceptics. But someone was base
enough to suggest that the heroic Cleaver had never
been at Mons; and Squire, to refute this calumny,
wrote to the record office of the regiment for par-
ticulars. In return, he got a cold official reply which
proves that Cleaver was only mobilised in Chester
(Eng.) the day before the battle of Mons began,
and did not leave England for France till the day
after the retreat from Mons to the Marne had ended.
But a mere detail , like that won't .discourage the
pious faith of ~the* good ToTks wEcFbelievVthat God
is English—a Cheshire man, maybe—and the tale of
“the angels at Mons” will for generations be told
as a convincing “proof” of the special divine favor
which Field-Marshal French enjoyed.
The veteran James Ashton, father of Will
Ashton, has been re-elected president of the
Society of Arts in S. Aus.. and 11. E. Powell
remains secretary, as he has been any time
these 20 years. At the annual meeting of the
society it was resolved to cut the loss lately
made by having two exhibitions each year.
The ordinary July show is to go in with the
‘'Federal,” in November; but the society
will console itself by showing some
“sketches” in May. There was a brisk debate
on an official proposal to fix a minimum
price of a guinea for a “sketch” and five
guineas for a picture; but eventually this
was thrown out. as being on the lines of
what someone called “Trades Hall Tyranny.”
Incidentally, Will Ashton, whose work has
been bought by many galleries, admitted
having sold things for as little as half a
crown; still, in liis experience, “the bigger
the price the better the chance of a sale.”
“Chug,” in protest:—■
Why this eternal reference to the Germans as
“Huns” ? The Huns were a mob of Asiatics who came
near making a mess of Europe, but the Goths were
a mob of Germans who did make a mess of the
Roman Empire and with the assistance of the Van-
dals, Lombards and a few others put back the clock
of civilisation a thousand years. Why doesn’t the
old name do for the Kaiser’s crowd? Goths they
are, and the name carries as much stigma as any
other. But even that shows a frightful lack of
imagination. Can’t someone invent a plain title
that is less than a thousand years old ?
Well, why not call them Germans? You
couldn’t make it worse.
In the intervals of procuring peace among
the nations, a little coterie in Melbourne
takes its daily peck at a Swanston-street
cafe. There is a penny-in-the-slot electric
piano in the place. It is not a large estab-
lishment, mostly depending on the steady
custom of a few dozen faithful feeders of
all shades of political opinion. At one table
an M.E.A. is accepted as a sort of chairman,
and he discourses in a booming voice on the
war-situation, going back to the days of
Itufus to prove that things could be better
if they weren’t worse, or words to that
effect. Rut the assemblage has its remedy.
“Here, Bill,” says one, reaching down in his
pocket for a copper, “bung this in an’ let’s
get on with our dinner.” Bunged in accord-
ingly. “And so,” says the M.L.A.. “the end
of despotic rule” (crash of melody from
the piano) “downfall of the oppressor
and” (up the scale and down again and
“Rule Britannia!”) “era of peace and
prosperity, with the people no longer bound
to the wheels of” (series of hurried arpeg-
gios, “Sons of the Sea,” more arpeggios, a
salvo of thunderous chords, “God Save the
King”—and half a dozen diners each grop-
ing for another penny).

tea. Christmas toys and jobs. the only

“Bill o’ Costs”: What a fine old phrase it
is—“the precincts of the court”! It. has
flavor and dignity about it, like dry sherry
and side whiskers. When I read that a
surprised suitor has outside the court room
ventured to express his opinion of the other
party or his lawyer, and has been hauled
back and charged (preferably before Madden,
C.J.) With contempt, then am I filled with
great joy. “Your Honor,” says the out-
raged complainant, “he threatened to pull
my nose within the precincts of the court!”
At the mention of that phrase all the judi-
cial horsehair stands on end, and the rash
person w’ho thought that when once
outside the court he could freelv express
himself, SV .'wfW
description of his offence. Then he learns
that Contempt of Court does not merely
mean offermg cheek .to the beak where he
sits, but includes taking liberties anywhere
“within the precincts,” a vague territory
over which the Judge sometimes steps or the
bailiff spits. Some of these days someone
will raise the point, “What is a precinct?”
Then the old chestnut as to wdiat a “place”
is will be tame and dull by comparison,
because, you see, a, precinct isn’t anywhere,
It is merely adjacent, so to speak; and if
you cannot locate a place, which must be
somewhere, a fortiori, as we say in the law
—but, there, there, what’s the use! Any-
way, I am thinking of hiring someone to go
into the street outside Madden’s Court and
make rudely disparaging remarks about it.
and when my agent lias been lectured for
10 minutes and fined 2s. (id., I shall appeal
to the Supreme Court and the High Court,
and hold a brief in the ensuing arguments,
and my reputation will be established, even
if a precinct remains as nebulous as it was
before.

“Sniggs”: Our destroyer Derwent has had
its name altered to Huon, because there is
Derwent in the British Navy. The
‘ x ~ . TT
change is most appropriate, as Captain Iluon
was one of the discoverers of the Derwent
River, which was named in 17113 La Rivifere
du Nord (River of the North). Four years
after La Perouse had called at Botany Bay
in 1788, the French Government sent an
expedition to look for him under the com-
mand of Admiral Bruny D’Entrecasteaux,
There were two ships, the Recherche, in
charge of the Admiral, and the Esperance, in
joint charge of Captains Huon and Iver-
mades. In 1792 they named Storm Bay.
D’Entrecasteaux’s Channel, the Huon
River, Esperance Bay and Le Grand Bay
—the last after the sailor who found it.
Captain Huon was a brick of the royal
Bourbon house, lie settled in N.S.W., and
his descendants have intermarried with
famous Australian families like the Humes,
Ilovells and Mitchells, all pioneers and
explorers.

A VEGETABLE THAT REDUCES
FLESH.
“I recently made the astonishing dis-
covery that a vegetable can reduce excessive
fat, arid for the benefit of obesity sufferers
1 give the following facts. About five weeks
ago I met a friend from the south of France,
who. knowing I suffered from obesity, ad-
vised riie to include salith leaves in my diet.
She told me that these were extracted from
a harmless sea plant found off the south
coast of France, and said it was quite well
known among the natives there who main-
tained that it possessed exceptional health-
giving qualities. Though sceptical as to
its success, I decided to give the treatment
a trial, and on inquiry found that my
chemist already kept salith leaves in a con-
centrated and most convenient tablet form.
For the first week I did not notice any
difference, but at the end of the second I felt
undoubtedly better and my clothes (parti-
cularly round the hips) seemed much easier.
Scarcely five weeks have elapsed now, but I
have already lost 91b., and feel ever so much
more healthy and vigorous. I only took two
tablets before each meal.”*
Overall, McCray, Ltd.
Bald Rock,

POLITICAL
POINTS
JUST AWAKE AGAIN.
The water supply of the Northern Suburbs of
Sydney has again failed, and in some parts house-
holds have had to depend on water cars. — News item.
RIP VAN WATERBOARD: “Well! Well! Who
would have thought it!”
Part of that benevolent jest, the Nobel
Peace Prize, will this year go to the pur-
chase of cannon, shells and warlike sundries,
for £1125 will be deducted as Swedish defence
tax. Probably it is the first bit of the prize
that ever did anything for peace,-seeing that
purchase of the machinery of war is a surer
way of promoting harmony than the writing
of hundreds of essays to encourage some
people to remain defenceless while others get
ready to walk over them.
The war dwarfs so many things that a
recent peaceful incident of tremendous conse-
quence was scarcely noticed. A man sitting
in an office in America exchanged words with
another man in France —actual spoken words
- —and an operator in Honolulu beard the
talk. It is little tilings like this that ought
to make somebody wonder what public works
are permanent. And the outcome of the
wonderment ought to be a sinking fund every
time.
The acrid protest of N.S.W. Liberals
against the Broken Hill Trades Hall Exten-
sion Bill, which gives the Barrier unions
another slice of land in the middle of the
city, would have sounded a lot more genuine
if it hadn’t been the Liberals who gave the
first slice. That was long ago, when the
two big parties of the time were fishing for
the Labor sprat. Henry Parkes, who helped
the business through, subsequently laid the
foundation-stone of (he ball. That night
somebody overturned it and stole the coins
underneath ; but the old man proved an alibi.
There are some people who can’t learn
the truth. The W.C.T.U. of Victoria has
passed the following resolution at its annual
convention without a dissentient voice:
“That the Union advocate the closing of
liquor bars permanently at six o’clock, in-
stead of temporarily at half-past nine
o’clock, and that ‘shouting’ be made a crim-
inal offence.” The W.C.T.U. produces no
figures to show that the 9.30 early-closing
lias been even a partial success. So far, all
the available figures indicate a rank failure.
As for the suppression of “shouting.”
Australia lias been advised of only one soli-
tary conviction for this offence under the
new English law—which seems to dispose of
the efficacy of that measure. This fooling
around with proven fallacies gets the Union
nowhere —and meantime the liquor trade is
putting new r mirrors in the private bar and
at 0.25 p.m. is doing a roaring traffic in
buttled beer to he drunk on the kerbslitone
in the shweet by-hic-bye.
We are capturing somebody’s trade, any-
how. Imports to Australia for nine months,
January-September:—
1914 .. £58,416,742 | 1915 .. £50,752,724
Exports of products other than gold during
the same periods :
1914 .. £47,080,370 | 1915 .. £41,725,958
Gold exports:—•
1914 .. £1,996,407 | 1915 .. £8,091,926
So, with all the fevered borrowing, it is
clear enough that Australia hasn’t borrowed
hard enough to keep her credits on the right
side, and is now, in addition to importing
foreign capital in the shape of goods, ex-
porting Australian gold at a greater rate
than it is produced from the mines. There
can be only one end to a country which goes
along on those lines; and the depreciation
of the paper currency will mark its arrival
at the half-way house.
A wail went up from Sydney Telegraph
the other day that the Northern Territory
refused to go ahead in spite of “there being
no constitutional restrictions to its complete
development by the most up-to-date Labor
methods.” Its condition certainly is nothing
• for Labor to crow about; but what about
the anti-Labor crowd? . They had control
for 50 years or more, and when the Com-
monwealth arrived in 1901 the result of all
the “development” along anti-Labor lines
was a white population of 4073. And it
wasn’t because the Telegraph’s pet black-
and-tan mixture was prohibited. There was
a time when Chinese over-ran the place. That
was when tlie Telegraph’s nigger policy
went to pieces, for the Chinese didn’t develop
the country worth tuppence: they simply
gouged tlie eyes out of its mining fields, and
then cleared, leaving it every way poorer.
The Telegraph’s crowd simply smudged
tlie page. The Labor crowd, if it hasn’t
done much, has anyhow kept the page clean.
The S. Aus. Assembly lias resumed its
Bible-class manners. Over a discussion on
a Teesdale Smith contract, arranged in a
hurry to give work to unemployed, Public
Works Blundell said: “I publicly admit that
the Government did not do so much as it
might have done. Tlie Government did make
a mistake in not going to the extent of
advertising for offers to have the work
carried out.” On which this lady-like dia-
logue followed:—
Peake: It was a loose business arrangement.
Works Blundell: It was. I have already admitted
that.
Peake: There seems to have been no check.
Works: There was no chance to get a check, in
the circumstances.
Peake: Oh, well, I feel that the iirice paid in that
respect was a fair thing. Your heart was sound,
but your head was dicky.
Works: If the lion, member can get any pleasure
in putting it that way, I will not complain.
Tlie Sunday-school certificate which the
Assembly had been imperilling with its
“Scoundrel!” and its “Come outside!” can
now b'e restored. Both sides have “got
religion” again.
Late Minister for Home Affairs Archibald
in reference to O'Malley, cheerfully remarks
“I have no doubt I shall be a Minister when
he is in Hell. This is no personal matter
witli me.” Certainly not; but it seems a
fairly personal matter for the Kink.
THE END OF THE SHOW.
GLOOMY ACTOR: “ WHat's the mater this morning, Albert?”
GLOOMIER MANAGER: "Baby Briggs is getting married, and the show's queered."
Said Asquith —•
But I challenge anyone to prove there has boon
anything of sloth, indifference and self-complacency
to face unpalatable facts, or to conceal the truth.
(Cheers.)
A few minutes later, among other charges,
Edward Carson brought this against the
Government: —
So far as he could sec when he was in the Cabinet,
there icas no staff at all.
And a little later on, Charles Beresford told
the world this :
Hitherto there had been no proper plan of cam-
paign. The naval authorities had never been con-
sulted regarding the Dardanelles operations, but
simply told to “do it.”
And still, as far as one can judge, Asquith
the Challenger didn’t turn a hair. What are
these political “challenges” worth?
There was a lot of loose talk in the S. Aus.
Assembly the other night over the rate of
interest which it is permissible for the State
to pay. The Government asked to be
allowed to offer up to 5 per cent, for the
conversion of a loan of two millions just
falling due in London. Peake, the Opposi-
tion leader, offered to allow the Government
to go higher. As a matter of fact, any
rate of interest is merely nominal, unless the
loan is issued at par. In this case the loan
was actually issued for five or seven years
at 99; in other words, you get £99, and pay
£lOO, and on a five-year loan this adds
nearly a quarter per cent, to the nominal
rate of interest. Add underwriting and
various concessions, and the actual rate paid
by S. Aus. is not far short of 5| per cent., in
spite of all the loose Assembly talk above-
mentioned. The quotation of a fictitiously
low rate has the bad effect of making a
borrowing policy agreeable to a lot of people
who would have much more serious doubts
about it if they were told the blunt truth.
From a detached Tasmanian onlooker: —
Our experience so far suggests that the propor-
tional system of representation, with preferential
voting, promotes rather than restricts party strife.
Under the Hare system neither political party in a
fairly equally-divided Parliament need fear a crush-
ing defeat at the polls. There can be no such thing
as a party debacle , so the personal risk to any
sitting member is reduced. Consequently there is
a tendency to ride the party moke recklessly at
all obstacles. Neither the moke nor the rider would
rush the fences if the falling places were not so soft.
A deputation that waited on 1 rank T .
to protest against the 100 per cent, me ‘ 7
of Customs duties on motor-tyres go ,
right answer. Tudor said that lie nite
to do everything he could to prevent '
many from getting busy on Australia a
the war; and tis the local manufacturers,
who had established themselves under the
old tariff and had kept the prices from being
pushed up, could expand, and as crude rub-
ber could he obtained from Papua and the
Northern Territory, lie considered the best
plan to block German trade was to keep tlie
business for Australians. But why doesn’t
Tudor apply the same sensible conclusions
to a few more industries?
A LITTLE GAME OF BOWLS.
Another £11,010,000 was added to the public debt
during last year. The total now amounts to
£127,735,000. — N.S.W. Treasurer’s Budget Speech.
The Sydney city railway was to have been
started before this, but operations have been
suspended until the beginning of next year.
The official explanation is that so many
draughtsmen have enlisted that the Works
Department is unable to prepare the work-
ing plans. Lack of cash —of course not!
The only chance that any N. S. Wales job
has of being held up for lack of cash is the
absence from the earth of any person from
whom the Government could borrow.
MAORILAND SPEAKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here to the courts of tlie Empire I am come. Let
my voice be heard.
Ye have named mine a little people. Hearken to
this my word.
Untilled, alone, unfruitful, a forest of mighty trees,
An unstrung, unpolished jewel in the swirl of the
Polar seas,
They found me, and straightway labored in stress
and travail sore,
Cut and polished me, forged the links binding me to
your shore —
Cut and polished unrestingly, as it was only meet,
That I of the setting he worthy, that your girdle
might he complete.
When the sword leaped from its scabbard, did my
people falter or weep ?
From the North in the sun a-dreaming, through the
Straits where the wild winds leap,
To the Bluff like an icicle gleaming, where the
sullen pole tides creep,
The message rang- and they heeded. In their man-
hood’s strength and pride,
From the North to the South they rallied, and
sprang to the mother’s side.
They are a handful of people on the outer rim of
the earth,
Where the arts of the old world nations are still in
the throes of birth ;
of color, and song, and laughter life lias doled them
a scanty share,
But has filled the day with labor, and dulled the
night with cave.
Yc own a hundred cities ablaze with time’s purple
and gold,
Right it is that your sons should fight for the
wealth and romance ye hold.
My sons fight for tlie Future, holding it ns their
bride,
With courage deep as the ocean; their souls as the
skyline wide.
And when the task is over, with love in their hearts
they will sweep
Over the mighty waters, over the vasty deep,
Back to the mist and the sunshine, to tlie sting- of
tlie icy breeze—
To the emerald they have wrought me agleam in the
Southern seas.
Ye have named mine a narrow people—the fires of
my heart are stirred.
1 have spoken, Mother and Sisters—my far land
awaits your word 1
JIAOMLAND.
JOHN EGAN.

A NEW INVENTION
The “Rawson Moody”
Patent All-Steel Fence
There are three distinct stages in the evolution of the fence —first
the stone period, second the wood, and third the steel.
The stone fence was too expensive to quarry—too weighty to handle,
hence it soon gave place to wood.
The wood fence has also had its day. There are too many objections
to its use. Being subject to quick decay, it serves as a breeding
place for vermin. It is rigid, and lacks flexibility or springiness.
It is also very susceptible to destruction by fire.
The “RAWSON MOODY” is the last word in fencing—THE PATENT
SELF-LOCKING, ALL-STEEL FENCE.' It is as superior to wood
as an automobile to a buggy—a man-power to a gas-engine.
The remarkable flexibility of the “Rawson Moody” enables it to
stand the severest strains, as the whole fence resists the strain put
upon it at any given point.
The “Rawson Moody” fence has long passed its experimental stage—
being now the most popular and most successful fence made. Used
by landowners all over Australia, and by the New South Wales
Government.
The “Rawson Moody” is a complete fence, comprising straining posts,
standards and droppers—all of which are supplied separately if
required.

For Sale

recently was the most up-to-date of its
kind in Australasia, being modelled on latest
American practice.
The main items are two Babcock Wilcox
Water Tube Seilers, one 3008.H.P. Itobey
Drop Valve* Horizontal Engine, largo and
small Band Mills, Four Block Log Carriage
with Trout Power set works, and gun-sliot
feed. Five-saw gang edger, 10-saw trimmer

logging trucks, steel rails, haulers, etc.
Full inventory will be posted on applica-

1015.

A HINT TO THE SWEETHEART.
To the thousands of sensible young
women who have men friends serving in the
ranks at the Front and who desire to send
some article that will he of real _ benefit,
instead of some silly, useless trifle, it might
not be amiss to suggest sending a bottle of
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Fills.
A bottle of pills may seem an amusing or
unusual thing to suggest; but, seriously,
could anything of equal size or cost be sent
that will be so necessary or useful? _ It is a
well-known fact that the men require such
a remedy. The irregular life causes the
system to get out of order, and it is this
getting out of order that is responsible for
such an enormous loss from sickness. The
shot and shell of the enemy are only a part
of the danger the soldiers face. To be an
effective soldier good health is far and away
the most important single item.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are too well
known to the public to waste space telling
what they will do. The little amber bottle
in which they are packed will secure them
from all kinds of weather conditions, and
they can be carried by the men on their
person at all times without inconvenience.
10,000 bottles of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills were offered and accepted by the Mili-
tary Authorities during the early months of
the war. From letters received from Egypt
and other advices, the gift of these pills
was highly appreciated, because they met
the everyday requirement and kept them in
perfect condition. See that a bottle of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills is included in any
parcel being sent to a friend at the Front.*
SPORTING
NOTIONS
The moral of the recent Melbourne Cup
race is that the best of the three-year-olds can
always be expected to make bold bids for it if tlio
most kindly-handicapped of their seniors are not
equal to a two-mile journey. Last year the three
placed horses in the Derby-—viz., Oarlita, Aristaeus
and thru—ran in the Cup without a hope of win-
ning’, for some of the light-weighted older nags were
stickers, and the pace was exceptionally fast. Car-
lita, the best of the youngsters, finished ninth. This
year, with Bst, 41b. up, the same mare finished a
good third, two of the Derby placers being in front
of her. From which it is evident that Carlita, not-
withstanding her notable wins in w.f.a. races, is
not a great mpre, but merely a good one. The colts,
Patrobas and AVesteourt, were better at the weights.
Patrobas won by a neck because he was very fortu-
nately circumstanced as compared with the workman-
like looking AA'estcourt, who is quite likely to develop
into a good Tin over long distances. Patrobas drew
the Mo. 1 position next to the rails, whereas AVest-
eourt got No. 24 position, outside of the field, and
had to cover quite a lot more ground than the
other follow before he got near the rails.
Other bits_ of luck for Patrobas were the break-
down of Naxberry (Bst. 41b.) —a carefully-prepared
four-year-old with some credentials as a stayer—and
the accident which disposed of Marculfus (7st. 41b.),
not to mention Mountain Knight and St. Carwyne,
'Oon after the start. Marculfus, like Naxberry, had
been specially-trained for the Cup journey. Lambert,
who had the mount, was as sweet on his chance as
Bracken, who had been previously engaged for it.
Marculfus was expected to get busy when the other
nags were stopping. But lo! something fell in front
of him and brought him down. So the long prepara-
tion was a waste of time. The luck of the game!
The book shows that Derby horses, though they are
usually in good condition and are honest battlers
(not having begun to lose their enthusiasm for
racing), are very uncertain matters of calculation
in the big Spring handicaps. There are the years
when they shine, and the seasons when they are on.
of it. Prince Foote won Melbourne Cup in 190"
because he was a first-class animal with no good
light-weight elders to beat. Alawa, the runner-up to
Prince Foote, was a doubtful stayer with 9s t. 31b.
in the saddle, and Aberdeen, the third horse, was
too infirm to be thoroughly trained. Lord Nolan,
the three-year-old who won in 1908, carried 101 b.
less than a Derby winner’s weight, and had nothing-
much to beat. Poseidon —a great horse up to 12
furlongs, but not a really good stayer—struck a
year when the opposition was weak, and he won
the Cup from the colt (Antonious) that had stretched
his neck in the Derby. The fresh and sprightly
tliree-year-olds had things made easy for them thm
year. It was the same when tlio young stable com-
panions, Clean Sweep (7st.) and Malster (7st. 111 b.).
finished first and second for the Cup, with a com-
moner named .Mix in third place. When Derby
winneF’Merriwee won the Cup in 1899 another three-
year-old, Dewey, finished third; and Dewey had pre-
viously won the Caulfield Cup, a race in which the
less handy Patrobas this year could never get to the
front.
The case of Amberite of yore shows the great dif-
ference between one season and another as regards
the Derby horse’s chances in Melbourne Cun. Amber-
ire in 1897 won both Derbies and the Caulfield Cup.
the same as Poseidon did before adding the Mel-
bourne Cup to liis trophies. But in Amberite’s year
tlie late “Bill” Forrester had his two good things—
Danins (7stl 81b.) and The Grafter (7st. 71b.)—in
the Cup, and they ran clean away from the field.
Gaulus won by a head from The Grafter, who next
year, with 9st. 21b. on liis back, was good enough
io win the two-miles race from AVait-a-Bit, the
Derbv winner. Cocos finishing third. Now, what
chance had am' average top-notch three-year-old of
winning the Melbourne Cup in Gaulus’s year? Not
Buckley’s !
The foregoing remarks go to exnlain why racing
tipsters, without execution, didn’t • greatly fancy
Patrobas for the Cup before he had won the A.R '
Derbv, and why not many of them picked him after-
wards. In the first, place, there was no line of evi-
dence bv which Patrobas could be proved better
than Cetigne and AVesteourt (not to mention the
exalted AVallace Isinglass) before lie bad met them.
And in the next place it was clear that he had a
poor show to beat sueli as Lnvendo and AVillinm the
Silent if they were going to be in better nick
Flemington than they were at Caulfield. Silent
Bill, Reputation and Garlin, who finished in the
first quartette for the Metropolitan, had confirmed
their form by finishing in the first five for Caulfield
Cup, with Lavendo ahead and Patrobas behind
them. The two imported horses and the dandy
black from Maorilahd had only to do as well a
Garlin in the meantime:to be pretty certain to beat
that proved non-stayer in the Melbourne Cup. But
whereas the consistent Garlin repeated himself—
being right in the front rank, and failing onlv i"
the last 100 yards—Lavendo and AA’illiam the Silent
never got near the front. AA'ant of condition for a
two-miles’ struggle might have beaten the infirm
aliens on the pinch, but they never, arrived at the
pinch. Lavendo was badly left at the post, and
couldn’t sustain the effort of making up so much
leeway. AVilliam simply wouldn’t gallop with his
former dash. Circumstances favored fhe Derby colts,
and that’s all about it. Had AVallace Isinglass been
nearly the colt that public fancy painted him—his
owner supplying the paint—-he would have won the
Cup.
Patrobas, being a true son of Wallace,
will take all the hard riding he can get; Hence the
luck of Bobbie Lewis in getting the mount in the
Cup. Smart had ridden a capital race on Patrobas
in the Derby, but he would have had to put up
some extra weight in the two-miles event, and he was
not under contract to ride at catch-weights. Lewis,
for hi.s part, had not been booked for Wallace Isin-
glass, and after the Derby failure he was full up of
that thick-winded deceiver. So he, beHng- the oldest
and strongest jockey who could go to scale at 7st.
61b., was snapped up for the job of pushing Patrobas
in the Cup. Smart couldn’t have done it better.
And the extra weight would have kept Patrobas
from winning.
* * * *
“Bobby” Lewis, who added a little to his
pile by riding the Cup winner, is far and away the
wealthiest of the jockeys now in training. “Bobby”
must be worth more than 25 times his weight in
gold.
*■.***
It was rumored at Flemington ou Cup
Day that William the Silent was strolling around
the birdcage with a muzzle on him, talking loudly
through his nose the while. King Marco, another
imported pony, was mistaken for the better-behaved
William. Marco and Mr. Brennan’s little horse are
much about the same size and color, and with their
legs swathed in bandages either nag might be casu-
ally mistaken for the other.
* * * *
During the Melbourne Cup meeting only
three horses won twice (Patrobas, Marculfus and
Traquette), and only one owner (Mills) entered
more than one winner. The most auspicious trainer was
P. T. Heywood, who was represented by five firsts.
The most successful jockey was Lambert, who rode
them. The winnings list was headed by Mrs.
Widdis, the owner of the Derby and Cup winner,
Patrobas.
* * * *
Mountain Knight earned his oats —over
■£ll,ooo in stake money—before cracking his shoul-
der in the Melbourne Cup; so his wealthy owner,
Watt, of Ilawkes Bay (M.L.), has not much to
complain about. Queer how good luck and bad
comes in patches to men and horses. Mountain
Knight was an exceptionally fortunate horse for
over a couple of years, for though not a first-
flighter he struck seasons when top-notchers were
scarce, and began to look like one. Then. trouble
came in hunks. At the A.J.C. meeting he contracted
some mysterious sickness variously attributed to
dope and to a thistle poisoning his tongue, and
cracked up badly, letting down with a heavy thud
the crowds who. had steadily backed him for Caul-
field. But he brightened up later on, and was
backed some more for the big handicap at Fleming-
ton. It was a hoodoo bet, for Fate, or something,
had him dead lie goes to the stud, with a
good record, lie wasn’t a champion, but he was a
pretty good sort of an ’orse, all the same.
Two brilliant wins by Marculfns in the
Veteran Stakes (12 furlongs) on the Thursday and the
V.R.O. Handicap (14 furlongs) on Saturday must have
convinced the backers of Patrobas that they were
lucky when three horses came to grief in the Cup
race. Marculfns was one that fell, and it now
seems that he only needed a fair run to have got
home in front. Last Saturday the -fine son of
Positano carried his Cup jockey Lambert, plus 221 b.
of dead weight, and galloped the 14 furlongs in
record time for Flemington. On Thursday he had
won the Veteran Stakes in fast time, with Bst. 21b.
up. And he was thrown into the Cup with only
7st. 41b.! On paper Marculfus, the 20 to 1 chance,
was a real good thing for the 16 furlongs’ journey
had Mountain Knight not happened to fall in front
of him.
* * *• *
Carlita, in winning the C. B. Fisher Plate
(12 furlongs) from Cetigne, confirmed the impres-
sion that she should have finished a closer third in
the Cup, though the mare may not have been quite
good enough to win. In the Cup she was giving
Patrobas 121 b. In the C.B.F. Plate, run over the
Derby course, she gave Cetigne 141 b. and beat the
colt by a length. Well, Patrobas beat him more
easily in the Derby.

horses was one of the features of the Cup meeting
six out of seven starters for the C. B. Fisher Plate,

they ail finished in front of the one Victorian rep.
As the Plate is a w.f.a. event the fact is the more
surprising. The winner was Carlita, and as she got
away with the Williamstown Cup on Monday, carrying
9st. 41b. at 10 to 1, her stake earnings run into
about £12,500.
