“T.F.”: A while ago The Bulletin
mentioned a number of cheap-priced gee-gees which
had won big events. Two more for the list.
Carlita, winner of Williamstown Cup (Vic.), on
Monday, cost only 125 guineas; Bascasse, Queens-
land Derby winner, was exchanged for 100 guineas.
Thus far, the season lias been a triumph for cheap
yearlings—something which will make purchasers
of young blood think heavily.
Steeplechase form is a lot more variable
than the most in-and-out running on the flat. It
is only sometimes that the toughest old ’chasers are
equal to a severe test, though they are kept in
work nearly all the year round. And the average
quality of the jumping fields varies like the fashions
in hats. For instance, last year old Coradjill
‘ lOst. 111 b.) finished last, and a bad last at that,
in the Victoria Steeplechase, won by Swedish Lad
in 6min. 29sec. This year Coradjill, carrying
list. 81b., won the same race in 7sec. better time,
two old stagers that beat him last year being simply
out of it from start to finish this time. In these
races over the big sticks the opponents (or their
jockeys) seem to take turn and turn about. At one
time one lot of gees appear unable to put any pace
on. Three months later they form the vanguard,
and the former lot of leaders run hopelessly in the
rear. A field of 12 for a steeplechase at Fleming*
ton in the “off” season usually means that one of
four horses will surely win unless the whole quartette
topple over. The other eight starters never get near
the leaders.

* *- * *
Thus “Vagabond,” of the K.F.A., settling
down for his second winter at the Flanders Front: —-
The latest sport in Flanders is field-gun sniping,
and you can read that both ways, for the field guns
are the snipers and the sniped. It is a sport really
for g-unner subalterns, although occasionally majors
are tempted to indulge in it when plenty of birds
are about. The Forward Officer of a battery gets up
as near to the German lines as he can. He is armed
with a telescope and a telephone, with a battery tied
to the other end. He keeps a careful and compre-
hensive look-out over the country at the back of the
enemy lines —well back usually, because the front
line is squatting well down in dugouts or under
stout parapets, and doesn’t offer a target worth
heaving a shell at. But back a few thousand yards
the inhabitants are more careless, especially if they
are below the eye-level of the front parapets, and so,
as they fondly imagine, out of sight. Particularly
good sport is to be obtained when a fresh lot of
Huns come up into the trenches, because, not having
learned the ground, they may at any time wander
out on to a patch on which some Forward Officer
has glued his e.ye. Working parties, too, in the
innocence of their hearts, imagining that they may
labor safely without being shot at a mile or two
back, gaily get to work in broad daylight; or a
strange transport driver may take what he thinks is
a short cut. It often is—on a longer journey than
he had expected.
When the Forward Officer spots one of these
pleasant pictures lie makes hurried calculations and
hasty remarks down the telephone to the battery.
Then it's “Fire,” and he claps his telescope to his
eye to view the performance. If he has calculated
angles and ranges and elevations correctly the next
thing the enemy knows is that a disturbance has
been arranged and is due to take place thereabout
within the next few split-fractions of a second. The
game adjourn the meeting hurriedly, and without
waiting for any formality move towards the nearest
cover. If some of thorn have to go away on a
stretcher the Forward Officer enthusiastically shakes
hands with himself and telephones congratulations
to the gun.
One of the best bags he can make or hope to
make is of an opposition Observing Station, with
Forward Officer complete, planted by a battery to
play a hand in the same gun-sniping game. On both
sides a keen watch is kept for any clue to a For-
ward Officer’s hiding-place, and when one is found
a round of high explosive or shrapnel is immedi-
ately unloaded on it. A very young subaltern may
poke his telescope out of his tree and so give the
game away. At times a haystack, which ought to
lie a rigid non-smoker, is observed to be emitting
little blue clouds from its top store}'. Or there may
be a movement in a dilapidated cottage roof which
lias no right to move. There are finer points in the
game; but these are trade secrets in the profession,
and not to be given away.
Occasionally this intelligent sport of gun-sniping
degenerates into a general scrap, when the batteries
on one side, annoyed by some hit scored by the
other, foam at the' mouth and proceed to distribute
eruptions. The other lot hit their hardest back, and
so the scenery bubbles with lyddite and high explo-
sives. During this- period the Fonward Officers, the
genuine gun-snipe sportsmen, keep their heads (etc.)
as best they can, and pray hard that a chance shell
won’t lump in on them. They don’t mind so much
being beaten fair and square at gun-sniping, but
they feel annoyed if they are outed by a blind shot
in a kick-bite-and-scratch. It hurts their profes-
sional dignity (as well as other parts), just as a
boxing world’s champion would be who might get
slugged over the head with a bottle in a rough-
house argument. But, on the whole, gun-sniping
can be recommended as an interesting and exciting
sport, even if an Observing -Station cannot be con-
scientiously recommended as a health-resort.

“S.T.S.”: I happened to he there when
old Grace’s great keenness, combined with Ins know -
ledge of the technicalities of cricket, made him '
about the strongest thing within the law ever se.
on a field. He was fielding at point, on Benning-
ton Oval, sometime in 1884, against the Australians.
The ball, bowled to Murdoch, got up awkwardly on
a lump in the pitch, and was put away a few varc s
to leg. The wicket-keeper went after it, a Quick
but safe single was run, and Grace moved up to
the wicket to take the return. The incident seemeo
to be closed. While the wicket-keeper was coming
back to liis place tlie batsman who had to take
strike, Sam Jones, went out to look at the lump and
pat it down; on which Grace put down the-wicket
and appealed for run out. And out it was. Aon see
the ball having been thrown in, and there being no
question of another run, Jones supposed it to >e
dead in the wicket-keeper’s hands; whereas it was
in the hands of Grace, who was not wicket-keeper,
though lie was standing at the wicket. That is,
Grace had not fired on a flag of truce; lie had merely
shot a man who was coming along unarmed under
what he thought was a flag of truce, though technn
cally it was of tlie wrong shade. There was a lot ot
talk about it. The legend at the time was that the
umpire said pointedly: “If you ask me, sir, I must
give it out” ; hut he went into print to deny thaL
saying that lie never made speeches when giving his
decisions. Grace’s own simple, sole and sufficient
argument was that rules are Rules. The man was
out of liis ground, the hall was not really dead, and
his duty to his side required that he should giet a
man out whenever lie lawfully could do so. 1
took the news of the incident to George Palmer, laid
up at the hotel with a sprain. “The old villain, ln-
said. “If I had known lie would do a thing like
that there is a trick I could have played him several
times. He backs up too soon when you are bowling
from liis end. and you could put his wicket down
instead of delivering the ball. Next time I will do
it.” But I never heard of the old man actually
getting out that way.

“0.K.”; First-class cricketers are. not
first-class insurance risks. Ilazlitt has gone close
after Victor Trumper, whose old opponent. Grace,
died only a fortnight ago.., Even “W.G.,” who out-
lived so many of liis contemporaries at the game,
was not so very old when he died. Yet: he must
have often felt in his 60’s that ho had missed the
hearse, so to spak. One thinks of the famous
cricketers of 30 years ago, and remembers only
the dead. Murdoch, A. G. Steel, Alfred Lyt-
telton, Bonnor, Shrewsbury, Johnny Briggs, floran
and others have passed out. Of the team that Lord
Sheffield brought to Australia —when the show was
advertised by the name “W. G. Grace” in large caps
on the posters—how many are alive now? The throe
stars—Grace, Stoddnrt and Lohmann —are deceased,
anyhow. Evidently tlie life of a representative
cricketer puts too much strain on the constitution in
most cases. A man can live longer by sticking
strictly to bowls or golf.
Ilccent N. S. Wales enlistments include
three top-notch X.S.AV. oarsmen —Alma Cox, ‘‘Gig”
Smedley and Alf Felton. Cox had a share in all
sorts of Tasmanian sculling championships, includ-
ing six senior eights and two champion eights, and
latterly was a N.S.W. amateur champion. Smedley,
as coxswain, holds the unique record of only losing
one race for Sydney Rowing Club in six years.
Alf Felton is the professional sculler who would have
made a front-ranker if he hadn’t flown too high early
in his rowing life.
# * * *
Light heavyweights Harold Hardwick and
Harry Reeve battled over 20 rounds at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday, and Reeve got the verdict.
Tt wasn’t much of a scrap. Hardwick outpointed the
Englishman on the first part of the journey, but
made very little use of opportunities for doing
something more emphatic. Towards the end he
tired badly, and Reeve, who is at any rate a stayer,
easily wiped out the score against himself. Hard-.
wick looked like a man who had overtrained. At
any rate, ho shaped poorly, missing often and hitting
without much sting. This suited Reeve very well.
Reeve is plucky and quick-footed,, but that he should
be somewhere near top among the bigger men is a
rather pathetic circumstance.

“S.S.”: I was at a fight the other night.
Ernie v. Arthur. “Fight ’im, Ernie, fight Mm!”
advised a fierce ringsider. Arthur replied in a scorn-
ful aside: “Fight me! Him fight me! He can’t
keep me employed. Somebody get me a bit o’
Knittin’?” This irritated Ernie, and he socked
Arthur on the iaw. When Arthur came to he was
most bitter. “The cow!” he said. “Fancy him
takin’ offence at a little thing like that!”

Westralian Soccer footballers are doing

the nation and 32 have been wounded. N.S.W. Rugby
Union’s tally last week had reached 1519.

THE GOOD CAUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The good old lady at the Cup
Was seeing all they had to show.
She put her horn-rimmed glasses up,
And took a long survey of Mo ;
And Moses yelh-d his lungs away,
for this was .Tudah’s busy day.
The lady saw his frenzied eye,
She gazed upon his foam-flecked jaws ;
Then said she with a puzzled sigh:
‘‘No doubt it is a worthy cause,”
And added to the bookie’s swag —
She put a penny in In's bay!

The society in its present form dates from
1900, when it cut off half its capital to pro-
vide for accumulated and prospective losses
oh properties which had been thrown in.
Tilings turned out a lot better than they
were expected to; and in the past seven
years the society has got back over £10,009
of the money which it then thought it had
lost. This so-called profit has been passed
to reserves. However, there isn’t much more
to come from the same source, as the old
foreclosed properties have nearly alb gone.
This shows the position in that and one or
two other respects :
July,’ol. July,*l4. Jan.,’ls. July,'ls.
Liabilities ..£37,015 £45,813 £40,766 .639,587
Advances . . 19,998 121,991 119,881 120,095
Foreclosures .. 72,305 10,576 8,481* 7,912
Cash, etc. .. 2,778 165 990 .1,412
Thus foreclosures have been worked down
from £72,805 to £7912. This money, along
with the accumulated profits, has been put
into advances; but there hasn’t been enough
doing in that way to entice the co. into
borrowing to lend out again. Liabilities
are therefore actually less than they were
14 years ago. Considering the agencies
through which the public can get house-
purchase money nowadays, the society's
earnings are astonishingly good; and (lie
management will be very fortunate if it can
go on doing as well. A society which at
this business can make well over 8 p.c. on
capital and reserves combined, without tak-
ing the risk of handling more than a trifle
of other people’s money, certainly seems to
know its job.

.Toe Gardiner. Ltd. (Sydney), formed to
take over various boot-retailing shops as
from August 1, 1911 (the deficit Jto begin
with was made up of goodwill £0777 and
flotation exes. £1188) :
Julv, Profits. Dividends. To Reserves. Deficit.

This is a trading co. pure and simple. Of
its capital and reserves £5899 is all that, is
represented by freeholds and leaseholds. The
balance, with the liabilities to the public, is
practically all in stock (£69,978). That a
co. with a capital of only £44,813 and liabili-
ties of no more than £21.570 should hold
stock of that size is only possible through
the small investment in premises combined
with a cash trade. Including prepaid charges
the sundry debtors amount to only. £2BOB.
The co. is thus able to handle a business of
remarkable size in proportion to the capital
invested. But if the co. hasn’t its own
capital invested in premises it must have
somebody else’s; and with certain qualifica-
tions the value of the premises is preference
capital which doesn’t figure in the balance-
sheet. There is this! however, to be said:
supposing business falls away, the hired
pi emises can be left and the capital thus
represented ceases to be a burden. On the
other hand, capital represented by premises
doesn’t carry a fixed maximum interest, since
the rent Is liable to go up as the-business
becomes more profitable. Apparently this is
wluit lias been happening. At any rate,
whereas the gross profit on trading in 1914
was £55186 and the rent, taxes and operat-
ing exes, were £41,780, in the past year the
gross profit was £53,896 and the rent, taxes
and operating exes, were up to £45,788. No
doubt there were other factors —increased
wages and taxes, for instance. Anyhow,
there is the fact —gross trading profit was
£1290 less, while rent, taxes and operating
exes, were £4OOB more; and the result is
shown in a severely cut net profit. Even
now, however, the margin of profit over the
124 p.c. dividend is equal to 7 p.c. of the
present capital of £44,813. Both principal
and profit depend very largely upon the
stocktaking. No independent check of that
lias been made this year; but 12 months ago,
for the satisfaction of shareholders, an out-
side expert was called in, and he reported
all well. As to the future, the trade is in
goods which will be called for even in less
prosperous conditions. In fact, under such
conditions, the co. is likely to attract a cer-
tain amount of custom which went in other
times to shops that wouldn’t think of put-
ting a familiar name like “Joe’" on their
signpost.
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : Re the Melbourne-
optioned Olympic at Linden. Records show
that at a depth of 40ft. payable stone was
extracted. Values materially improved at
75ft., and from a width of 3ft. the owners
dug out and crushed 156 tons for an average
of 2oz. 17dwt., and obtained an additional
4oz. per ton for the tailings, or 6oz. lSdwt.
in all. It is now proposed to sink a further
50ft. and open out.

Whim Well (W.A.) has got its reconstruc-
tion approved at meetings of debenture
holders, unsecured creditors and share-
holders. All that now remains to do is
to get the sanction of the Court and tjie
British Treasury. Meantime the receiver,
manager and general promoter of the re-
organisation—one Campling—-claims to have
got hold of people who have faith in the
show and pluck enough to fiijd the funds
necessary to re-start it on a sound basis.

There must be a Reason for the fact that the great bulk of the
motor-car owners of the world are Ford owners—more than 850,000 of them.
We believe that one reason for that is the lightness of the Ford—the lightest
motor-car in the world. And that lightness means economy in tyres, in petrol,
in repairs, in general serviceability. We ask no better proof than the legion
and the tribute of Ford owners.

Messrs. Davies & Fehon Motors Ltd.
Hunter Street, SYDNEY.
Dear Sirs, —1 bought my Ford from
Mju in January, 1912. it has covered
22.000 miles since then, and has cost
me practically nothing for repairs. It
runs on this dead, loose soil on the
average of 25 miles to the gallon of
naphtha, on hard red country nearly
30 miles.
It runs as well as a new car, and a
man who knows it well offered £l4O
for it lately, but I want it.
You know there are no roads here,
only waggon tracks, and they are
bumpy and heavy. Other cars bought
about the same time have tumbled to
pieces and are now scrapped, or ought
tp be. I have had no troubles other
than punctures.
A tyre runs about 5000 miles.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) C. B. BLYTHE.

condition, none of the engine bearings

kind to the car-—the petrol consump-

gallon. In mud “Fords” have no equal;
they float over bogs heavier cars sink

was from Mildura to Broken Ilill
between 0 a.in. and .12 p.m., over 300
miles, thence into the heart of Aus-

and I have yet to learn what a break-

with a capable, experienced driver, you
can go anywhere.

BEFORE THE MIRROR.
w - By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeaftnette,” tins 'well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of " T^„ e „ b^accmn-
wHl be pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Mirror,

These are undoubtedly days oi exceptional nervous strain foi tbe
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its f j°' an)0ll ,
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few the mo : : tll a little
us—can avoid worrying over past or prospective events, we na , « notice-
care and forethought, succeed in preventing the resuit of worry fiom be
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat w * . to ii et
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real-need of ll known
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto
method of retaining, or regaining that beauty of face and figure *ch :« fhebutu
right of every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned be found to
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist nil usua j
have a small quantity in stock.
Freckles and How to Cure Them.—lt Is always
those with the finest and fairest skins who
freckle most easily, but this knowledge is pool-
comfort when the face is disfigured with these
nglv little blemishes. To prevent the forma-
tion of freckles, a wash should be applied to
the face which will form a shield from the
rays of the sun. A simple and inexpensive
unc can be made up at home by dissolving an
ounce of cieminite in four tablespooni'uls of
hot water. Shake the bottle, and when cold
apply to the face and allow to dry on the
skin. If this is done every day, renewing the
application whenever the face is washed, and a
little raereolised wax rubbed into the skin
every night, freckles will not make their
appearance, no matter how hot the sun. To
remove freckles which have already formed,
apply the wax every night, rub it well into
the skin of the face and neck and leave it on
lill the morning. Then wash off, using Pileuta
soap and warm water.
The Hair—To Increase the Growth. —To arrest
an undue falling of the hair and increase the
growth, scalp massage and the application of
a good tonic is absolutely necessary. Part the
hair in the centre, and starting at the forehead
massage for at least ten minutes. Then apply
a tonic. A simple and inexpensive one can he
made up at home by mixing one ounce of
boranium (which can be bought at the chem-
ist’s) with a quarter of a pint of bay rum.
Add the boranium to the bay rum, allow to
stand for half an hour, then strain and add
sufficient water to make half a pint. Dab
amongst the roots of the hair with a soft
sponge.
Blackheads, Knlarged Pores, ete.—Blackheads
are unusually troublesome things during the
summer months to those bothered in that way.
The extra warmth makes the skin “give,” and
it looks more greasy than usual, and conse-
quently the blackheads are more noticeable.
To remove them and close up the pores of the
skin dissolve a stymol tablet in a glass of hot
water, allow the effervescence to subside, then
dip a soft sponge into the liquid and bathe
the face. After a few minutes, dry the face
with a soft towel, and the blackheads will be
instantly removed.
To Reduce a Double Chin. —To reduce a double
chin, give hard, firm massage every night with
mercolised wax, stroking the flesh firmly back-
wards from the chin towards the lower portion
of the neck. Do this every night, and in the
morning bathe with cold water and iii')
the skin a little parsidium jelly. JJ"* 1 ®„ (l
ment will strengthen the flabby slolia iiv
restore the contour of the face, t <■ • k 0 - ,
a double chin is due to stooping oieibooh..■ ■
work, but generally it is the result of put tin.,
ou weight. When the latter is t he ‘ a^! a , vlu
elynol berries, eaten regularly eieij da •
greatly assist in reducing fhe si pei tiuoi (
flesh. A glass of hot water should be sippeu
after each meal.
Superfluous Hair.— Dark-complexioned women
are far more frequently. ";? übl q , 1 ,.s wi t
growth of superfluous hair than those
fair skins, and at the first sign of tlie*e objec
tionablfe hairs steps should be taken to remoie
them, or they will develop into a stioi g
growth which will take a very long time to
destroy. A little powdered pheminol applied
to this growth will remove the unsightly blem
ish. destroying it permanently if the haiis be
but few. Two or more applications may bt
necessary if the growth be unusually strong.
but about one ounce of pheminol should m
sufficient for the most stubborn case. Phemi-
nol can be obtained at the chemist s and a
little powdered alum should be obtained at tlit
same time for dusting the skin before applying
the pheminol.
Grey or Fading Hair.—To restore the color to
grey or fading hair add one ounce of coneen
trate of tammalite to four ounces of bay rum.
Apply to the roots with a soft sponge everj
night until the desired result is obtained, then
use the lotion two or three times a week. Alter
three or four days a decided improvement will
he noticed, the hair turning gradually to its
original color, An iron tonic will be found
most beneficial when the greyness is caused by
general debility.
Beautiful Hair—How to Shampoo.— Whether
the hair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the hair in good
condition it should he shampooed once m
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing,
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful oi
stallax granules ill a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which the ends
of the hair can fall, and shamnoo the stallax
mixture into the scalp in the usual way.
Rinse, and dry by fanning with a palm fan. 01
in the open air. if the weather permits. Should
the hair be naturally very greasy, the oB
massage should be omitted: [•?

“F.H.” : There is a law in Victoria against
soothsaying for cash, but not much is done
by the police to deal with the professional
soothsayer. The credulous, discontented
woman is still the prey of scores of charla-
tans who are prepared to prophesy trips to
Europe or new husbands for a consideration.
Once in a blue moon the police-force dis-
guises itself with big feet, enters the dim
mystery of the prophet’s parlor, and presently
testifies in court against the prophet; but
not much ever comes of it. On the other
hand, it is a suggestive fact that one of
Melbourne’s newest, most lavishly-rococO
and nouveau-decorated “mansion homes” is
the property of a lady whose business it is
to communicate vague, high-priced nothings,
full of canny comfort and cunning advice, to
a faithful circle of chuckle-heads.
“Alamon” ; All Northern Territory hotels
came under Government control a week or
t"-o ago, and one of the suddenest shocks
that customers got was a demand for cash
down for all drinks. If you’ve ever been in
the N.T. you’ll know how easy-going the
old system was. Another facer was the abo-
lition of Sunday trading, and many an old
stager now spend a long, hot, dry Sabbbath
praying for Andy Fisher’s welfare. Private
Enterprise is forbidden to sell, attempt to
sell, offer to sell, or supply liquor, and first
offenders have to face a fine of £3OO or 12
months’ imprisonment, or both. Hence, sly-
grog selling- is dead —for the present. As
booze can be lawfully supplied only by the
Government store, and sold only by State
hotels, probably the N.T. will become more
temperate, if not teetotal, in the future. The
hotels at Katherine and Brock’s Greek are
closed, and the three in Darwin and one. in
Pine Creek are run by employee*. - whose job-
depends on observing the law. A mail might
as well be at the war!

1913; A special work has been the removal of the
Kelp Rock at Currie Harbor, for which a contract
was let in February, the amount being £5OOO.
1914: The contract for the removal of the Kelp
Rock is progressing as well as can be expected, con-

complete, and in every way fitted for this important
work..

Poor, hard-up little Tasmania is finding
out, like the rest of them, that wheat-specu-
lating isn’t the game it is cracked up to be.
When it couldn’t get what it wanted from
the mainland it turned to America, and the
Yankee unloaded a lot of inferior stuff. The
Government reckoned it wouldn’t be safe to
get only just enough to last till November,
because something might happen to this har-
vest too; so it has a pretty big stock on
hand which it can’t afford to sell for less
than about 75., while mainland stuff can be
landed for 4s. 9d- There are only two things
to be done. It can either keep the price
propped up, or take the natural price and
call the difference a loss. In either case
the public pays, in the first case in its
bread bill or in the alternative in its taxes,
In either case the Yankee scores.

Mackinnon, Victoria s Attorney-General,
mumbles his reply to the demand that the
borrow-and-boom policy should stop:—
We must not shut down all round, dismiss people
and create a panic by wholesale stoppage, but weather
the crisis without interfering with the lives of the
people - -
Of course, nobody wants the absurd person
to create a panic. All that is asked of him
is to cease wearing out his boots on the way
to the mont-de-piete and get out of his skull
the demented notion that by pawning the
people he is helping them, instead of sowing
tares and docks and alligator’s teeth.
Thus a man in the business
The action of the Federal Government in suspend-
ing the trade mark of “aspirin” (German drug) in
favor of two Victorian chemists may be all right,
provided that after the war German rights are not
re-instated. If they are re-instated, then Hughes
will find that he could have devised no better way
of keeping the nest nice and warm for the Hun
than by thus preserving in the public mind the word
“aspirin.” One condition is that the Australian-
produced “aspirin” must comply with the tests for
acetyl salicylic acid, British Pharmacopeia. If
aspirin answers to these tests then it is acetyl sal.
acid, and acetyl sal. is aspirin, and no patent
rights should be granted. Is this Hughes’s idea of
collaring German trade?

“Mil”: A proof of the humanness of
Caucus selection lies in the fact that it
reflects the mistakes of the electors. Kink
O’Malley, who hangs on shakily to the Dar-
win seat, got himself elected as Minister
despite his record as a puller-down of Labor
Governments, thereby proving that all the
thoughtless people are not on the West Coast
of Tasmania. Archibald was not the limit
in administrative ability, but the principal
objection to him was that, in a time of finan-
dal stress, he did not give way to every
demand for more nay or less work. The in-
teresting thing ahead is the watching of
O’Malley attempting to redeem promises to
the ca’ canny section of the unions, and
contemporaneously putting into action those
fine business ideas he learned in Amurrika
—ideas consisting mainly of The substitution
of red ink for black, and designed to save
everything except life and money. It’s a
good reflection on Australia that the Caucus
can’t find an Australian-born hot-air mer-
chant equal to O’Malley. I’ve seen a dozen
O’Malleys on Broadway selling jewellery at
a dollar a quart. Yet' he is chosen from a
party including Watkins, Bamford, Page and
other good Australians. But none of these
five at the Trades Hall or belong to the
Underminers’ Union, neither are they any
good at rolling a log or pushing their own
barrows.
An Opticians Bill is promised by
the N. S. Wales Parliament. It is
time. Only a week or so ago a person was
advertising for “Yg men, good add., no prev.
exp. necy.” to “work” a district for the sale
of spectacles: and, of course, a cash pay-
ment was required in return for a “guar-
antee” of £5 per week over expenses. What
may happen to the eyes of people who
are treated by yg. men of good add. and no
prev. ex. can be told by hundreds of victims.
The Legislature should have jumped years
ago on a business that is not only plain
robbery but eye-murder in addition.
“Orion,” back from S’Africa:—
A Dutch parson named Von Horst, who was a
candidate for Parliament at the recent S’African
election, distinguished himself by using the gag that
the Almighty could trust General Hertzog in the
dark but not the British, and that was' why He
never let the sun set on the Empire. After the count
of votes, which gave Botha an easy majority, the
predekant forgot to say that evidently Heaven had
lost confidence in Hertzog; nor did he explain why,
when a word from Hertzog could have stopped the
preparations for rebellion, he never spoke it. Now
poor old De Wet and a crowd of others are in
Johannesburg gaol, while the real instigator of the
riot sits grinning in Parliament.
“Rainbow” : The British Board of Trade
has decided to alter the “wool” test in the
color-vision examinations. For years the
aspirant for a certificate as a mate has had
to pass what is known as the Hofmgj'en
examination. Given a collection of wool
skeins of every hue except black and white,
he has had to match those picked by the
examiner. If he could decide by day a crim-
son lake from a burnt sienna he was ad-
judged capable of telling a green light from
a red one by night. Science long ago de-
nounced this system as imperfect, and ad-
vised the adoption of the obviously correct
test of real lights. The object of the test is
not to find out if a sailor is “color
ignorant,” but whether he can distin-
guish a ship's lights. - After lengthy per-
suasion the British Board adopted a lantern
test in addition to the wool test, with many
misgivings and shakings of its grey old head.
So successful has it been that the Board
announces the abolition of the wool test als
from January 1, 1916. After that date
aspiring masters and mates will be examined
only by means of red, white and green lights.
V “Glen”: Before the war the Port-Mel-
bourne rifle-range was visited on Saturdays
by a fairly solid crowd of regular attendants,
year in and year out. When bloodshed
began a swarm of learners came along. At
that stage one had to play a patient wait-
ing-game to get a whack at a target. Then
came the voluntary drill-movement, wifh the
rifle clubs as organising centres.. That fairly
clogged the range with shooting-students;
and numbers of these trainees enlisted. in
due course. But, with every club clamoring
on Saturdays for two or three targets, there
was an inevitable lack of shooting facilities.
Now, with the need for trained shots as
pressing as ever, the attendances are slack-
ening, and beyond doubt the shortage of
targets is the reason for it. For
a year or more an agitation for an
open range to the east of Melbourne
(so far served by miniature ranges only) has
been engaging the bored attention of the
Defence Department; but, beyond the in-
spection of one or two possible sites, nothing
visible has been done. The present is no
time for procrastination. Either this busi-
ness of learning to shoot is necessary or it
isn’t. If it isn’t, why the Port Melbourne
range? If it is, why this apparent content-
ment with an inadequate system after 15
months of war?
Assisted by private donors, the Vic. Edu-
cation Department has built a semi-rural
school for poorly-nourished city children.
It is at Blackburn, a bush-and-orchard dis-
trict 11 miles out of town, and the pupils
come daily by train from Melbourne’s
closely-settled areas. Stretching a point,
the Railway Department charges only
a shade over a shilling weekly for each
child’s fare, while for the tucker supplied by
the. Education authorities another shilling
or so is asked. Both these payments are
made by the parents of the children. Mrs.
William Keast, whose husband is M.L.A.
for Dandenong, started the agitation which
led to the establishment of the school, and
most of the cash for the building was col-
lected by her. The experiment has not been
conducted long enough to establish results;
but, since the Education Department seems
to be taking no great financial risks in the
matter, the good work will probably be
allowed to go on.
An art note (more or less) from Colies,
the black-and-white man:—
Ever since landing here in Gallipoli I have been
under intermittent fire; practically all the time.
But it wasn’t until a few weeks ago that I found
myself involved in an argument that lasted continu-
ously for one day and through the night and into the
following morning. It was a case of advance and
counter-attack and counter-counter-attack, and a lot
of my good Australian brothers fell. Just about dawn
I was one of those relieved. We were then occupying
captured ground, and from the characteristic maze
of shrubs, hillocks, pits, abominable stenches and
other horrors I was glad to clumsily drag away to
where a narrow track began a precipitous descent
into the gully.
Next night, feeling chippier, I was having a sea-
bathe, when a "chap in the water near me
mentioned Manly. He was, it seems, one of the force
I had met going up to their baptism of fire. With
a lurking hope, I asked after my Sydney artist
chums. To my surprise he told me that there was
a chap named Cecil Hartt in his company; but, he
added, he was one of those who’d gone out in the
charge that morning and hadn’t come back. “But
see that bloke up there,” he continued, pointing to
a group further up on the beach-—“ask him. He’s
in the same platoon.” I went and accosted a ser-
geant. “A long streak, ain’t he ? Struth, yes 1 He’s
all right. He came back with me —wounded in th’
thigh an’ shoulder Yes, he wuz sent off fer ’is
’oliday—but quite all'right!”
So, in the same disturbance which provided my
initiation, artist Cecil Hartt made his dSbut and a
sudden but honorable exit. It was hard luck to thus
just miss meeting him. But there’s plenty of time
yet; there’s tons of encores in this show —and < we
could do with a lot more performers.

IN THE SUPREME COURT OF
QUEENSLAND, TOWNSVILLE.
1915 No. 90.
MATRIMONIAL JURISDICTION.
MR. JUSTICE SHAND.
Between JOHN BAILEY Plaintiff and
MARIA MARTHA BAILEY Defend-
ant and JOSEPH LEBER Co-defend-
ant.
To the Defendant Maria Martha Bailey
wife of John Bailey of Mount Molloy Ada
Cairns in the State of Queensland Teamster.
TAKE NOTICE that this action Avas on
the twentieth day of x\ugust 1915 com-
menced against you and the abovenamed
Joseph Leber and that the Plaintiff by his
Writ of Summons claims a dissolution of
his marriage with you the said Maria Martha
Bailey and by his petition filed in this Hon-
orable Court on the 20th day of August 1915
the plaintiff alleges that you the said
Maria Martha Bailey committed adultery
with the abovenamed Joseph Leber on the
15th December 1899 at Port Douglas and
also between that date and the 31st Decem-
ber 1908 at Port Douglas Sydney and Port
Melbourne and elsewhere.
AND TAKE NOTICE that the Court has
by Order dated the 29tli September 1915
authorised service of the said Avrit and peti-
tion on you by insertion of this notice not
less; than twice in each of the following
newspapers: The Age (Melbourne, Vic-
toria), The Australasian, the Sydney
Bulletin and the Cairns Post.
AND TAKE NOTICE also that a t ou are
required Avithin thirty days after the last
insertion of this advertisement to- cause an
appearance to be entered for you at the
Registry of the Supreme Court of Queens-
land at Townsville and that in default of
your so. doing the plaintiff may proceed in
this action and that judgment may be given
against you in your absence.
Dated this twenty-ninth day of October
1915.
CHAS. S. NORRIS, Registrar.
Connolly & Slithers. Solicitors, Townsville,
, Town Agents for E. B. Steele, Solicitor
for Plaintiff, Cairns.
IN
BANANA LAND
Dropped dead in his office at Brisbane on
November 2, Thomas William Connah,
Auditor-General for Queensland since 1907.
He was born at Birkenhead (Eng.) and
arrived in Brisbane 49 years ago. After a
term as accountant for Gibbs, Bright and
Go. he entered the Audit Department in ’72,
and had climbed up to be Under-Secretary
ac the Treasury in 1901. He was a year or
two over 70 when the books were closed.
“A.T.E.”: Bananaland Judges are on a
soft wicket. This is one of the latest dis-
coveries made by inquisitive young Fihelly,
the Assistant Minister for Justice, who has
acquired a nasty habit of making inconveni-
ent “discoveries.” The Beaks, it appears,
regulate their own expenses by the simple
device of drawing exactly what they want
Nobody but themselves knows how the
money is spent, as they simply draw a
cheque to bearer and no vouchers are sup-
plied to the Dept, to show how, when,
where or why the cash goes. Fihelly re
marked that “the system was susceptible of
improvement.” Not at all! At least, not
from the point of view of the Judges. Also
why should business methods be introduced
into Government Departments? In any case,
only public funds are affected, so why worry?
“F.H.”: Captain Parry-Okeden, the
Queenslander mentioned in lan Hamilton’s
just-published despatches, spent a long time
in the Melbourne Domain, prior to leaving
these shores, as a lieutenant with the Divi-
sional Ammunition Park under Colonel Tun-
bridge. He is a very tall, large, capable
young man ; and he had the luck to be at
Gallipoli when most of the D.A.P. was
bailed up in England. At the Front he was
early given a provisional captaincy. By the
way, Tunbridge, who has only lately got out
of England, was one of the Australians in
the scrap at Eland’s River, when a few
mixed troops stood off six times as many
Boers for a fortnight or so, from behind a
rampart of very dead horses, in “the most
distinguished action of the war.”
A Brisbane daily, descanting lately on the
agricultural industry, quoted the names of
several well-known Queenslanders as having
been that State’s most • successful planters.
But the most spiendidlv-cffjcibik planter of
all was never mentioned. ' Fifty years ago
Gore, of. Yandilla, set in his -garden a little
spiny thing, and gave orders to his gardener
to water it carefully, as it was a tender
specimen. The gardener forgot it, and was
sacked. But the plant grew on, and to-day
30,000,000 acres of Queensland are covered
with prickly-pear descended from the pam-
pered pet of Yandilla homestead. As a
“successful planter” Gore, himself a sprig of
an old and prolific Parramatta family, beats
all Australian records.
“J.F.IL”: Any pioneer settlers in the
Gulf Country ever heard of an early ex-
plorer called Benjamin Woodrow? He has
passed out at Baxter, Derbyshire, England,
at 74, and left behind him a tidy £65,760.
accumulated as chairman of Woodrow and
Sons, Ltd., the well-known Stockport hat
manufacturers. The obituaries allege that
he “went to Australia in his early days, and
took part in many exploring expeditions,
being among the first to penetrate the wild
regions, in the vicinity of the Gulf of Car-
pentaria.”
Home Secretary Bowman, speaking at the
Ipswich Workshops:—
Britain and her Allies would come out on top, and
lie had little hesitation in saying that the forces
behind Britain and her Allies would secure success
for their arms. (Loud applause.)
If the public men of Australia would
frankly tell their audiences, that the Austro-
Gerrnan may easily “come out on top” un-
less Britain equals the enemy in effort and
sacrifice, the result would be healthier for
all of us.
OBVIOUS.
BIG ’U N: " How is it that I do twice as much work as you and only yet the same pay?”
LITTLE ’ UN: “Brains, of course !”
A good word can be said even for the
drought. It is the most deadly enemy of
that beautiful pest—the water hyacinth. In
dry times swamps and backwaters vanish,
and the hyacinth bulbs perish, while the
salt water coming further up-stream kills
the floating pla.nfs ifi thousands. But to
prickly-pear drought is a matter of sublime
indifference.
Brisbane Worker announces that “with
►Socialism would come the reign of universal
Well, it all depends. If the same
brand of Socialism were universal, peace
might be universal, too. But, if it were not
so dead, ancient Peru, the most Socialistic
State that ever was, could say what is liable
to happen to a country that has no defence
except its Socialism so long as there are
unsocialistic brigands in the world.
In camp at Enoggera, Arthur Leslie Mer-
rotsy, who has put up a record by journey-
ing from the heart of Australia, where he
was with a survey party, to put another nail
in the German’s coffin. The first 180 miles
was per camel, the next 500 by horse, and
then 140 from Pine Creek by train. He
started on August 12 and got to Darwin five
weeks later; and eight days after that a
steamer dropped him at Brisbane.
The real problem of Bananaland is the
prickly-pear. Two years ago a keg of
cochineal insects were imported from
Colombo and dumped on to it magnificent
growth near Charters Towers. To-day that
pear is a wreck, and selectors are howling to
the Lands Department that if any more of
the vegetable is destroyed they won’t have
stock food during droughts. If the present
< 'V sn<dl continues, pear poisoners like Dr.
Jean Bell and Oliver Cromwell Roberts may
have to clear for their lives.
Geo. Rylands, once M.L.A. for Gyrnpie
and the deadliest enemy the Parliamentary
Refreshment Bar ever knew, is again to the
fore. After being defeated for Gyrnpie George
was made Director of Lands and Forests in
the Northern Territory, and achieved dis-
tinction by coming out on strike and doing
picket duty at the call of the A.W.U. He
aspires to-day to fill the shoes of Andy
Fisher at Wide Bay, and is up for the party
nomination.
“Tom” Chataway, ex-Senator for Banana-
land, who was politically drowned in the
flood which obliterated the Liberal' Senate
bunch last election, is at present driving a
quill in the Transport Service.
Captain Thomas Bennett, an auld Scots
engineer who began to apostrophise the
firemen about 70 years ago, lias obeyed the
Last Order at Bundaberg. He served his
apprenticeship on the Clyde, and then be-
came “guarantee man” for Lairds. His
first job afloat was aboard Queen Victoria’s
gift-yacht when it was sent to the Pasha of
Egypt. After that he had a turn .as chief
engineer on a mail-boat running between
Barcelona and Minorca. He landed in
Brisbane in 66, had charge of the engines
in Gibbs, Bright’s Hero and the alleged
warship Cerebus successively. Jobs as chief
of an E. and A. boat and of dredges working
in the Burnett and Fitzroy Kivers fol-
lowed. After a spell as chief engineer of
the 860-ton flagship of Queensland’s toy
navy, the Gayundah, Bennett joined the
Marine Board, and stuck to it till it was
reconstructed. _ He obtained the rank of
captain by virtue of being engineer-com-
mander when he retired from Bananaland’e
navy. The log showed 90 when the “stop?*
was rung down.
In Khaki.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As boys in khaki stepped the street
With trimly-shouldered rifle,
What meant that hurried bosom-beat.
That thought I could not stifle?
What meant that question in my brain
That out me ever deeper:
“If Trueman fights and I refrain,
Am I my brother’s keeper?”
i saw the trenches in my dream,
The boys within them straining
To catch the word at which they stream
To death-grips uncomplaining;
T saw them fall in twos and threes
Before the ruthless Reaper:
They never doubted once, did these,
They were their brother’s keeper.
And once again the vision changed.
The Turkish sea-line faded,
And back again my fancy ranged
To creek and cottage shaded ;
The children reft and fatherless,
The widow wan, the weeper
Gazed up and asked in pale distress:
“Art thou thy brother’s keeper?”
And whensoe’er I hear the drum
Roll out fits fateful measure
Or watch the lads whose hour has come
Take leave of them they treasure,
Within my soul there rings a cry:
“What aileth thee, O sleeper?
How canst thou ask while brethren die :
‘Am I my brother’s keeper?’ ”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, November 6, 1915.
My Dear Thargomindah, —
What do you think. To-day at the races
his Ex. actually interrupted the proceedings
after the third race to deliver a recruiting
speech! On the sacred racecourse! My hat,
this war is undermining our grand old tra-
ditions ! Lady Goold-Adams, who encour-
aged her husband in this break-away, kept
faith, however, with the tradition which re-
quired her to decorate the winner of the
Derby with the blue ribbon. In return for
this compliment the ungrateful beast en-
deavored to devour the vegetation on Ihe
vice-regal hat.
The froeking on the lawn was mostly of
the airy, fairy, summery type. Lady Goold-
Adams was in blue. Mrs. Jack Thomson,
the best-dressed woman present, paraded in
floral ninon over pink, with a rose-crowned
hat. Mrs. Isaac Moore, Mrs. Wliittiiigham,
Mrs. Boss Munro and others of the squatto-
c.racy appeared in white. Mrs. Gilbert
Wilson, in pink and white striped voile, was
a living denial of the current idea that
stripes are not the right choice for the tall
and slender.
Mrs. T. W. Bouchard paid for afternoon
tea at Rowe’s Cafe on Monday, in honor of
her daughter, who will soon forsake the way
of the bachelor-girl. There were about 60
bearers of congratulations at the teacups.
Dr. Doyle and his handsome wife have
forsaken Queensland for Hobart’s milder
summer.
Another medico who has deserted us —to
take a longer journey—is Dr. Costello Sliqw,
who is in Melbourne, on his way to the big
Mix-up. His wife and daughter are staying
in Melbourne until he gets his marching
orders.
The Brisbane Liedertafel provided a con-
cert on Tuesday night for a very large and
music-hungry audience. Lena Hammond
was the she soloist, and her fine contralto
voice was at pure-tone level. She warbled
in a frilly lavender gown, relieved with
black —a gown that her grandmother might
have worn, and certainly would have
worn if she had seen how fascinating her
grand-daughter looked in it.
If there are any German toys on sale this
( liristinas they seem to be carefully disguised,
i’he displays in the shops are mostly
.Japanese samples of work that Australia
should be doing on the spot. Incidentally,
toy-making ought to be a good industry for
returned soldiers. Has anybody 'got a
barrow-load of tariff bricks handy?
Colonel King, who has been trying to get off
lo the war ever since it began, has at last
attained his wish. He has been given com-
mand of the No. 2 Australian Remount
I nit. ihe residents of Gowrie and there-
abouts farewelled the fine old soldier the
other evening, and passed him some material
evidence of their regard.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

How to Get Rid of Dandruff.
This Home-made Mixture Removes Dan-
druff and Stops the Hair from
Falling Out.
The following simple recipe, which can bo
mixed at home or put up by any chemist,
will quickly remove dandruff and stop the
hair from falling out.
To a half-pint of water add loz. of bay
rum, one small box of Orlex Compound and
}oz. of glycerine. These are all simple in-
gredients that you can buy from any chem-
ist at very little cost, and mix them yourself.
Apply to the scalp once a day for two
weeks, then once every other week until the
mixture is used. A half-pint should be
enough to rid the hair of dandruff and kill
the dandruff germs. It stops the hair from
falling out and relieves itching and scalp
disease.
Although it is not a dye it acts upon the
hair roots, and will darken streaked, faded,
grey hair in 10 or 35 days. It promotes the
growth of the hair and makes harsh hair
soft and glossy. 17
IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as- possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Hough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
“.Taques”: The Newcastle (N.S.W.) col-
liery proprietors have many worries, and one
they would like to be relieved of is how it is
that every sailing vessel laden with their
product arriving in South America from
Australia discharges from 10 to 100 tons
less than the quantity shipped and weighed
by Government scales at Newcastle. The
vessel’s holds appear to be in the same con-
dition when she arrives as when she left,
yet the Chilian and Peruvian weights say
she is always short. Does any Bulletin
mystery-solver know if climatic conditions
interfere with the weight of coal?
* * * *
“Warre” : You know how a “gun” works
the three broads. He generally goes to
gaol. The State employs people to nab him.
Well, this is how another sort of game is
worked, and this gun keeps a motor-car.
Cocky consigns a truck of prime chaff to
his agent in the city. The day’s yarding
comprises the usual amount of medium and
inferior stuff. The prime lots are sold “pri-
vately,” say at ss. per cwt. The medium
and inferiors are auctioned, with top price
about 3s. 9d. The agent who sold the prime
stuff for ss. sends an intimation to cocky
that he sold at 4s. 3d. per cwt., having
managed by “selling privately to get an ad-
vance of 6d. per cwt. on the top price of
the day, the yarding comprising a large
quantity of prime chaff.” Cocky looks up the
auction sales in the press and is satisfied.
He got 6d. more than top price all right—
and the agent snares a cheap advt. and 9d.
per cwt. on a six-ton truck. When will the
fool-producers learn to co-operate?
* * * *
“Fax” : A few sanguine Victorian citizens
may have saved a quid or two this Cup time
at the instance of M. P. Adams. That ver-
satile young man (he is just now conducting
a lightning tour of Bailey’s “On Our Selec-
tion” Co.- —27 country towns in 3S days)
was lately managing Irma Caron’s Concert
Co. on the Gippsland circuit. During a
pause in the programme Adams, who is a
conjurer, unfurled an umbrella on the
platform and gave the audience a prac-
tical demonstration of how the thimble-
rigger and the three-card man deal with their
prey. He could generally induce somebody
to try. his hand at picking the pea; after
which he would give a touch-of topical in-
terest to the turn by addressing words of ex-
hortation to any misguided young men in
the audience who might be going to Mel-
bourne for the races.
* * * *
“Hori”: “Jaques” (B. 7/10/T5) is right
when he says that the claim to be the oldest
steamer afloat lies between the Stormbird
and the Edina, both built and launched in
1854. The Stormbird left the Broomielaw,
Glasgow, for Melbourne on .Tune 17, 1854.
She was consigned to Graham, Sands and
Co., Collins-street, and was intended for use
as a tender to the steamers Sovereign (after-
wards lost in the South Passage. Moreton
Bay) and the Prince of Wales (converted
into a coal hulk at Panama). These vessels
were to have run a mail service from Mel-
bourne to Panama, but the Crimean War
upset the scheme. Just when the Edina was
launched and sailed for Australia I don’t
know, but that date would help to settle the
argument as to which is the older boat. The
claim of the British steamer Ceres may be
worthy of support for second place, biit a
sailing ship turned steamer can hardly be
treated as having been a steamer all the
time. Another English claimant was the
Collier, built in ’4B ; but for many years she
was only an auxiliary steamer. The French
built the 1200-ton liner Orient in 1855; that
was their pioneer. The Stormbird is still
going strong, apparently as sound as' ever,
in the Wanganui-Wellington trade. The
Edina has retired from business.
* * * *
“T.D.”: Only a very reckless prodigal
would expect the fatted calf these days. At
Burnie (Tas.) a Durham bullock fattened
by the V.D.L. Co. for the Melbourne Royal
Show (which did not come off this year)
.was sold for £l2O. This is claimed as a
world’s record and is a big rise on the Aus-
tralian record of £7B held by a beast sold a
few weeks ago at Newmarket. The weight
of the V.D.L. bullock as he stands is nearly
30001 b., but, even so, the price works out at
about lOd. per lb. live weight.
* * * *
Angaston, a quiet little fruit-growing dis-
trict in S. Aus., is rippling with joy. Its
marble is to be used in Australia House in
London. It owns a first-class quarry, and
three or four thousand feet have been
ordered from it. Since Parliament House in
Adelaide was enlarged the industry had been
starved; but now six-ton*- blocks are being
turned out, and soon Andy Fisher will be
passing up staircases, and between columns,
and through doorways that have come from
home.
THE PANACEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a special meeting of the Canterbury (N.S.AV.)
Council consideration was given to the construction
of 1835 J chains of road works, at a cost of £60,000,
which it is proposed to borrow.... The proposal was
unanimously approved. —News item.
Folly, war and drought
Have almost knocked us out—
The Ma State staggers under killing loads ;
But suburban councils still
Can increase our little bill
By raising sixty-thousands pounds for roads.
In the olden days
The rates they used to raise—
A course at variance with modern modes ;
And a practice we’ve outgrown ;
Nowadays we raise a loan
■ To repair our worn-out, unproductive roads.
Or to build new, costly, mostly-useless roads !
Of course, it wouldn’t do
For us to buckle-to,
And lay the things ourselves past our abodes.
No, no! Or peace or war
We will borrow (as before),
And Posterity shall pay for our new roads—
Our futile and inutile little roads.
And good enough for ’em, for what’s Posterity
but a lot of useless and ungrateful little
toads!
N.S.W.

Each of us has a certain supply of ner-
vous energy. Every act, even every thought,
uses up some part of this energy. During
rest ahd sleep the supply is increased by tiie
blood, which gets the needed elements from
the air we breathe and the food we eat.
Your nervous energy is like a bank
account. If you use it up faster than ybu
add to it you overdraw your account. Neu-
rasthenia is the name given to nervous bank-
ruptcy. It means that the power to recupe-
rate is gone.
The bl7)od can be built up so that it will
increase the supply of needed elements to the
wasted nerves, and this is the only way that
the nerves can be reached. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills are a tonic that especially builds
up the nerves, because they supply to the
blood the elements that the nerves need.
Many nervous disorders, sometimes chronic
ones, have yielded to this tonic treatment
with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills when other
methods failed to give relief. They are cer-
tainly worthy of a trial in every case of
weak nerves and thin blood.

Cause of Much Trouble.
Constipation is more frequent in women
than in men. It is almost always present in
old age.
Many results of constipation are attri-
buted to something else. Constipation causes
pimples and eruptions. By pressing on the
veins it causes cold ankles and feet and pro-
duces varicose veins. It may cause neu-
ralgia. Constipation often makes children
delicate and feeble.
The remedy is to stimulate the liver by
the use of Pinkettes, the gentle laxative pills
that do not gripe or purge, but simply assist
nature. Taken regularly for a time, they
really do correct constipation.

The Inky Way.
‘'Fax" : Lindsay Russell (of “Smouldering
Fires”), who is now Pattie Scott, writes
from London about her literary doings
I have signed a contract that will run my annual
income into four figures. 1 get £5OO as deposit
alone on two Lindsay Russell books a year, and 1
have contracted to bring’ out two K. H. Quinn
books. I’m said to have the biggest sales of any
“colonial” author now, and certainly my (10,000
copies of one book alone startled me when 1 saw
the - .record. I have indeed been lucky. I've ju-t fin-
ished “The Zandsee”—it goes into press at once, and
will be in Australia in November. This comes out
side by side with “The Gates of Silence," also
“Flickering Flame,” the best thing 1 have ever
done or will do for a long while, it is the story of
one who came back from the Dead for three more
years on earth.
Somebody called Lindsay Russell the Aus-
tralian Corelli. But she can write better
lines than Marie Corelli was ever paid big
money for. She's a poetess who thinks in
melodrama.
‘“Hois” : lie Kink O’Malley (8.21/10-/M5).
One very good reason why his O’Malleyisms
are so generally disregarded is that they
have been found out. For instance, take
the sample quoted: “The three greatest
organisations the world has ever seen are
the R.C. Church, the Standard Oil Co. and
the Germany army." You’ll find that one if
you look in a book called “The German-
Amerienn Plot,” by F. \Y. Wile (page TO).
“The Incubus”: The death of .1. A. Millar.
Maoriland's ex-Minister of Railways, re-
minds me of the time I followed him to the
West Coast of the South Island. There were
two reporters in his entourage, one for the
Liberal and one for the Conservative rags,
and Millar was supposed to be inspecting
the railways and coal-mines. We made a
happy party, the pressmen halving the
speechifying and working amicably together,
'flic people of the West Coast, where the
big mining rushes used to take place, are
hosts who shake hands and say "What's
yoursV” every time they look at you. So
we had a wet trip for a fortnight, with
banquets in every town. One night we had
all come home from a gorge, and were
having a last one in the Ministers sitting-
room, when the then Speaker of Maoriland's
I louse got very solemnly on to his soles and
proposed the health of Millar,jstating inci-
dentally that he was sure their guest had
not reached the top of the political tree, and
things like that. My pal of the Conserva-
tive rag had been well stunned at the ban-
quet, but he woke up and heard the remarks
and began to write furiously. Then he
rushed off towards the local telegraph office.
After the Minister's secretary had held him
down and taken the copy from him we road
by the local moonlight the headings. "Is
There a Split in the Liberal Party?” “Sig-
nificant Statement by Speaker.” “Will
Millar Succeed Ward?” We put. that re-
porter to bed and tore the copy up. lon
Ward was at that time very, very shaky,
and it is a safe bet that the publication of
those splurgings would easily have swamped
the then-foundering Liberal party. It's won-
derful what a combination of whisky and
reporter can accomplish.
*■ * * *
“Mujik” : He' was standing on a box in
the Domain, a frowsy-looking person, sur-
rounded by a number of individuals whom
I identified as Senators. I don’t know what
he was talking about, but ak the rabble fre-
quently broke into cheers here it is, and it
shows the manner in which some people are
allowed to abuse the right of free speech :
The Government, suspecting that the people might
want to know what was going on, made the business
of telling them a State monopoly. Reporters were
excluded, and an official appointed to write an account
of the proceedings which would convey no information
tu one side or the other. It was also arranged
that other officials should revise the information that
wasn’t there, by striking out all reference to what it
was about. By this means the conspiracy that is
always afoot to prevent public servants doing their
worst was nipped in the bud, and the public was
furnished with matter of no value whatever. When
the people were made aware through other channels
of what had occurred they were at once able to see
how kind the Government had been in not allowing
them to be disturbed through reading matter which
had the circumstances been different might have been
of another character. As an ardent supporter of
the voluntary system I ask you to think of what
would have been the result if it had been known
that
Ac this stage seven constables rushed into
the crowd and arrested the speaker under
the War Precautions Act. Good enough for
him, anyhow.
* * * *
“lyaniva” : Died recently in London, Hugh
Spottiswoode, who, since 1903, has been
responsible for Printers’ Pie, a publication
which has raised large sums for literary and
printing-trade philanthropic funds. Spot-
tiswoode annually entertained all the writers
and artists who helped him fill the Pie, and
a couple of years ago his guests returned the
hospitality with a “Pirates’ Dinner” —
sanded floor, candles in bottles, fearful deeds,
etc. The captive, Spottiswoode, was brought
to trial, found guilty, and handcuffs of silver
were fastened round his wrists, engraved
with the words, “To bind us closer hand in
hand.”
' * ■* * *-
“O’Phimerty”: Katherine S. Prichard,
the Hodder and Stoughton prize-book
writer, comes of an Australian writing-
family. Her father, T. 11. Prichard, edited
Melbourne Sun for some years, and after-
wards took charge of Launceston Daily
Telegraph. He published a novel, “Retali-
ation,” which had little vogue, and has
dropped into oblivion. An uncle, F. .T.
Prichard, has edited Launceston Examiner
for many years. The rival dailies of the
city on the Tamar were at one time piloted
by the two brothers. Katherine has a bro-
ther who writes for Melbourne Argus. Her
only other brother studied law, dropped it
for medicine, graduated at Melbourne, and
is now with an Army Medical Corps in
Europe.
Defence-Minister Pearce has a streak of
verbal resource. “It should add to its title
and call itself the Stone Age” was his com-
ment on an article in the Syme paper
urging economy in the use of motor-cars by
the Defence Department. Far from going
back to tipdrays for the convenience of camp
commandants and others, Pearce says he is
seriously thinking of an aeroplane.

PURE WOOD FIBRE
Night Wind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Avhite road glimmered like a ghost
I Through twilight warm as a caress,
And, like a bird, beat at my side
The fluttering softness of your dress.
The wild, dark magic of the wind
Quickened the tide through pulse and vein,
go sweetly o'er the hills it dreAV
The fragrant breath of fallen rain.
Deep-throated in its glorious mirth
; It trampled, shouting, through the trees
To tousle all your silky curls,
And whip your skirts about your knees ;
To play a thousand wilful tricks
And buffet us Avitli fingers light,
And bear us boisterous company
Down the dark avenues of night.
The dancing stars betAveen the leaves
Like gleeful children peeped and flashed,
While surging madly, Avave on aa* aA T e,
The quivering forest heaved and clashed.
Low down, the moon with silver horn
Tattered the flying drifts of cloud,
And to the harp of straining boughs
The Avind exulting chanted loud.
Uplifted on the Avings of youth,
Beneath Ave scarce could feel the clods,
But footed thistledown that sped
Puffed by the merriment of gods.
Your warm hands clung about my arm.
Your laugh outsparkled moon and star —•
Oh, Avalking step to step like this
The wide world’s end Avere not too far!
Oh. walking gaily step to step
Eternity Avere not too long,
If round us floived the starry night
Wind-shaken into joyous song.
VICTORIA.
SYD C.
Jetsam.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The watch-below came suddenly on deck.
There wasn’t much of it, for it Avas
the skipper only—Peter Joyce by name ;
middle-aged, big and burly, with a black
bullet-head and a square, clean-shaven jaAV.
The other watch was steering. It, too, Avas
big and burly, but it was younger than
Joyce—fair-bearded and blue-eyed, as became
the name of Sigurdsen—Dial' Sigurdsen.
The ketch Avas doddering across the Strait
toAvards Port Phillip ; four knots or so, with
a light breeze abeam. She Avas a craft un-
beautiful, with a hull of faded red and green,
badly slacked standing-rigging, and here and
there a patch upon her dirty canvas. But
she had a pretty name —“Roxana”; which
Avas also the name of the skipper’s red-
haired Avife. Red-haired, but handsome in a
fiercely exuberant way, and not, it seemed,
unwilling to lend a shapely ear to gentle
nothings from the good-looking Norseman.
At all events, that Avas how it struck the
skipper Avhen, as has been said, he came
unexpectedly upon deck.
The Boy, who was all the rest of the
creAV, had been a Melbourne paper-selling
imp, and was sage beyond his years.
“Blime!” he said to himself, “there’ll be a
circus now, all right!”
Mrs. Joyce had walked forward, and the
Noi'Avegian was spoking the wheel over in a
hurry. Joyce stepped close.
“It’s Milbourne Ave’re makin’ for, me man
—not Kangaroo Island. How d’ye come to
be so far off yer course?”
Sigurdsen looked the other straight in the
eyes. He would brazen it out, he told him-
self. After all, wasn’t he just as good a
man as this clumsy dog of an Irelander? So
he sniggered maliciously, and spoke not.
Joyce’s jaAV set hard. “Here, boy,” he said.
“Take the wheel, and keep her just as she
is.”
Mrs. Joyce’s eyes glinted, and her breath-
ing quickened. She saw there was going to
be a fight about her, and all her primal in-
stincts awoke. For the time being she was
paheolithie.
Sigurdsen understood and braced himself.
“I don’d vant no poy to sdeer yor me,” he
said sullenly, and he still had hold of the
spokes when the other, with a sudden
upward right hand jolt, caught him under
the jaw and laid him on his back behind the
Avheel. Then Joyce stepped out of dis-
tance and threAV off his coat and waistcoat,
while the wheel Avas grabbed by the Boy,
delighted that he didn’t have to stand Avith
his back to the fun.
The Norwegian scrambled to his feet and
“peeled” in orthodox fashion; but Joyce
knew more about the game, and as Sigurd-
sen rushed in like a tornado he took a
straight left that sent him staggering against
the mizzen. Back again in a moment, he let
go tremendously for the body, but the Irish-
man—springing back and then forward in
the same instant —swung a right to the
other’s jaw that put him promptly “down
and out.”
But here Avere no Queensberry rules. For
a full minute the Norwegian lay where he
had fallen. Then he sat up, apparently still
dazed, but swiftly forming a plan. “Dod
auis all righd,” he said, as lie slowly got to
his feet. “Dyou vas too goot vor me !”
With a short laugh of contempt Joyce
turned away to take the Avheel again, and in
an instant Sigurdsen was upon him from
behind —got a mighty grip around the arms
and body, and made his teeth meet in the
Irishman’s mahogany-colored neck.
“S —s —s !” hissed the woman. Her own
teeth were clenched, her eyes were like
bayonets, and her fingers fiercely clawed the
air. The fundamental savage in her was
all awake, and that ferocious bite Avas Avliat
she would herself have inflicted upon a foe.
Then, Joyce, unable 1 to Avrench his arms
free, threw suddenly all his Aveight behind
him, so as to knock his antagonist over back-
Avards. Down then they went together; but,
as they fell, the craft’s low rail caught their
legs, and in an instant the sea had them.
The noAV badly-scared Boy had the making
of a seaman in him. As he whirled the
Avheel over to get the helm hard-doAvn he
screeched to the Avoman to run forward and
let-fly the head-sheets. This was Sanscrit
to her, and the Boy himself rushed quaver-
ing half-way to the bows; then, as the helm
righted itself and the craft fell-off again, he
darted back to the wheel. But the Roxana
was at best a lazy lump in stays. The jib
held her off, and the Boy, glancing over his
shoulder, saw the two men only just relaxing
their grip, and beginning to follow the ketch.
But they were already sixty yards or so from
her quarter, and she was moving faster now
than they could swim.
Frantically the Boy shouted to the woman
to come and take the wheel while he ran
forward to slack-off the head-sheets. But
she heeded him not. She had reacted into
modernity and hysteria. But not for long.
A sudden thought seemed to strike her like
a blow; then, Avith lips drawn back from
her clenched teeth, and red hair flying
fiercely from its coils, she rushed so savagely
towards the wheel that the frightened Boy
bolted for the main-rigging. But the woman,
with a swifter hand, grabbed him by the
collar and shook him till his teeth rattled.
“Do what 1 tell you !” she shrilled—“or over
you go to join the others! Or—better still”
—she snatched the slieath-knife Avhich the
Boy, in precocious sailorhood, wore at his
back. “I want the ship to go that way”—
she pointed down-wind, away from the men.
“Make her do it —do you hear? Make her
do it at once, or”
She flashed the knife at him, and, quaking
to his boots, the once audacious Boy put the
helm up. “Please hold the Avheel as it is,
then, ma’am,” he stammered, “an’ I'll do the
rest.” Tremulously then he went forward,
eased the jib and fore-sheets, and slacked the
main-boom well out. Then he came aft,
tended the mizzen-sheet, took the Avheel and
steadied the helm. The woman could see for
herself that her orders had been carried out,
for the Roxana, now dead before the freshen-
ing easterly breeze, was walking away eight
knots through the fast-falling night. And,
Avlien down the Avind came despairing cries,
growing gradually fainter and fainter, the
Boy shuddered, but said nothing, and the
Avoman’s face remained as stone.
“Well, you see,” said the Boy to me (the
boy grown-up and six feet high), “Avitli
nightfall it come down as thick as pea-soup,
an’ I was that flummoxed Avitli that orfui
Avoman that I clean forgot Are Avasn’t
showin’ any lights; so I nearly jumped outer
me skin Avhen I heard the screech of a
steamer’s siren close handy. I dropped the
Avheel an’ scooted for’ard, but I couldn’t see
ten yards ahead. Then the siren comes
boomin’ out again—right overhead, seemin’ly
—an’ all of a sudden I catches the loom of
a big steamer close on to us. Course I hol-
lered for ail I was worth, but, as I could see
both her side-lights, I kneAV Ave aa t us dead in
her track, an’ nex’ moment she simply Avalks
over us. I got a bash on the nut from some-
thin’, an’ I Avas dead to the world till I foun’
meself aboard the chap that had smashed us
up.”
The Boy struck a match and eclipsed him-
self Avith vapor.
“Well?” I said, after vainly waiting for
more. “What about the others? Were the
tAvo men drowned?”
“Y’may take it at that. Never heard of
again, ’t all events.”
“And the Avoman?”
Another long pause. Then —“Kuoav the
Binnacle Hotel?”
I kneAV it; and on the moment a light
came to me. The landlady of the Binnacle
Avas redundant, rufous, resnlendent and re-
pellent. Just the sort of Avoman, in fact, to
—but, stay ...
“Look here, my lad; if what you’ve told
me is true, she's a mur” ——•
“Sh —sh!” The Boy took his pipe out
from his mouth and turned upon me a
cryptic eye. “S’pose y’think she’d be afraid
of me givin’ her away?”
I nodded.
“Not ’er! She’s gone to wind’ard of me
there, all right.”
I Avaited. There was .something in all this
that didn’t quite
“She’s been puttin’ it all about that I’m
dotty—been’s mad’s a hatter ever .since
that” .'
“Since that” • ■
“That bash on the ’ead !”
* * * *
And I —having somewhat dramatised Avhat
the Boy told me—have since been wondering
whether or not “that bash on the ’ead” was
responsible for some of the story.
ALEX. E. MONTGOMERY.
From the First Engineer’s Log.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AT sea. Passed Timor, Flores. Samba.
Sambawa, and knows
how many more rugged islands.
Quite close to several active volcanoes, but
only a wisp of smoke and vapor slttiwod that
they were in business. •
Knocked off to receive a deputation about
Aziz Rahman, the bhandary (fireman’s
cook) again.
Tn Melbourne it was discovered that the
bhandary had been drawing full rations, giv-
ing short commons to the men, and selling
the balance of the rice, etc. A good scheme,
but he was fool enough to be found out.
Well. I cursed him and reprimanded him and
let him off. Whereupon Aziz Rahman made
up his mind that he would get even with the
blighters who had told me about his ways.
He started to do it by serving up curry they
couldn’t eat, and mouldy rice to help it
down. Hence the deputation.
The donkeyman and a fireman headed it.
Aziz Rahman had to be sent for. Curry
and rice were held under my nose in kero-
sene tins, in paint-tin lids, in half-cocoanut
shells and in grimy paws, and I was urged
to taste it. But I thought not. Then the
fireman made a speech. Lightly but grace-
fully he alluded to my world-wide reputation
for wisdom and justice. He told me I was
his “father and mother” (don’t you believe
it), that he/was my unworthy slave, and
that Aziz Brahman was a jackal and his
mother an owl; also that his female rela-
tions were busy members of the oldest pro-
fession in -tiie world. The donkeyman then
made a few remarks to the same effect; but

Spring is here now. The blossom is breaking- the
